Introduction

Here is an intuitive, straight-forward approach to self-directed character design practice for illustrators and animation artists. In this document you‚Äôll find a series of exercises, each one is presented with a particular design style to reference, there‚Äôs instructions to follow, and guides for you to create a new character that matches that style.

Further down you‚Äôll see the setup for these 12 assignments, with a total of 32 different established design styles to learn from. For each assignment you‚Äôll have a section called ‚ÄòSupport Material‚Äô, these are external links to design packs, tutorials, and articles aimed at giving you more knowledge on certain topics from a wide range of sources. There‚Äôs also some insightful character design history scattered through this site.

If you already know all about how design sheets work, along with the process and theory behind making them, and you just want to get straight to the practice exercises, then skip ahead to Assignment# 1 and get straight to work! Otherwise, keep reading

through the chapters below, to learn about the essential foundations and techniques behind the craft of character design for animation.

This is a large document, sometimes you need to give it a minute for it to all load up, after waiting for a bit, if images still come up blank, just refresh your browser or choose a different browser to view this with.

This document is for learning & self-training in the art and process of character design. Throughout your career you will upgrade those fundamentals with new and relevant techniques. Just like learning the piano, cooking, standup-comedy, or carpentry - you begin with the foundational skills, and you build up from there. Over time, with practice, you will gain more applicable and significant skill sets to add in your toolbelt. There‚Äôs also hundreds of external links on this site that lead you to various tutorials for you to watch, listen, read and learn from.

To be a character designer for TV/film animation, you must be able to diversify into a variety of art styles. This course is specific to 2D animation, however, most of these methods and techniques also apply to character design for 3D/CG productions, video game development, toy design, stop-motion films, and any other type of series & shorts for animated commercials, streaming, and media projects.

By reverse-engineering a wide assortment of different character designs, you begin to develop fast and efficient analytical skills for knowing how to replicate a new character in the chosen visual style. This way you become more proficient in creating new characters in whatever design style that is needed for the production. You will begin to develop instincts on how to blend styles together and therefore create new styles for any productions in the future. The more you do this with different techniques, the more you start to recognize the patterns and arrangements used to create these designs. You will get faster at figuring out what makes a design work and how to reproduce it.

‚ÄúDrawing is an escape from all the necessary things in life that get in the way of being free.‚Äù ‚Äî Jamie Hewlett

What Does The Character Designer Do?

Character designers visualize and create the look of individual characters. They work from descriptions given to them by the art director, showrunner, or creative producer. These might include notes on a character‚Äôs personality as well as physical traits. Early concept art or visual development work or online research/imagery may be used as reference or springboards to work from. They communicate the characters‚Äô personalities through their artwork of facial expressions, costuming, anatomy, scale/proportions, and physical poses.

By Adam Fay

Character designers also carry out research into exploring the anatomy and costuming of characters. They discover and use anything they can to inform their work. This role relies heavily on drawing ability, characters can be drawn on paper but usually created digitally, especially in the later stages and for their final format. Character designers create a variety of designs, usually including multiple versions for a single character.

In the beginning, character designers take inspiration from the script, character descriptions from the pitch deck, insight from the creator(s), and any provided concept art, using this to create the characters they‚Äôre working on. They present these versions to the director or producer and address any of their feedback to achieve a result that they are happy with. In the early stages, very loose and rough quick sketches are made to ascertain where the design sensibilities may be, to essentially rehearse some visual concepts. Then they receive notes and comments to further narrow down the designs so they can be refined and eventually finalized.

Character designers create entire casts of characters that all must share the same aesthetics and look to have them all consistently appear to be from the same world.

By Rune Bennicke

Once a rough design has been completed & approved, character designers create model sheets, which show the character from different angles. In 3D animation, these will be used by the modelers as their guide to create the character sculpts and builds. In traditional animation, the animator uses the model sheets as reference to ensure each frame of animation is ‚Äúon model‚Äù as the character is re-drawn frame by frame. In 2D puppet/rigged animation, the builders use the model sheets as direct reference to assemble and produce a finished rig for animators to use in their scenes to animate with. Storyboard artists will also refer to these designs while they visualize the scripts, to incorporate the models into their work as they lay out all the scenes.

By Shannon Hallstein

What is a Character Designer good at?

  • Drawing: have a strong level of artistic and technical skill, be able to create work in a variety of styles showing a high attention to detail
  • Creativity: use your imagination to come up with original and innovative ideas both for the artistic part of the designs and for resourceful problem-solving, and create work that sparks the director‚Äôs imagination which helps to tell a story
  • Collaboration: understand what the director wants and be able to take and act on feedback to alter your designs, while working well with other artists and designers
  • Understanding the Pipeline: know the capabilities of the artists and animators that will take your work and use it, create designs appropriate for the medium and technology used on the production - be aware of the many design stages & review stages throughout the process
  • Watching / Analyzing Animations: have a passion for the medium and a love of the industry - it doesn‚Äôt matter if the production is for 2D or 3D/CG or stop-motion, the ability to research, collect, and gather reference and provide quick sketches is invaluable

A profession in character design for the animation field demands the artist to switch into different art direction styles all the time. The more you attempt and replicate other artists‚Äô design styles, the more you start to recognize the patterns and similarities that exist in all styles of production design for TV/film animation, and gradually you learn to apply elements of these learned styles & absorb the contents of your collected visual libraries to your own tool belt and skill sets.

Designing an animated character involves several steps. Begin with a concept that defines the character's personality, traits, and role in the story. Sketch out these rough ideas to explore different looks and explore various features. Refine the design with attention to proportions, details, and unique traits that make the character stand out.

Analyzing character art styles and re-creating them to match that same look, is the best way to accelerate the growth of your draftsmanship and your personal artistic range. This type of practice is the most efficient way to develop a long career as a designer and character development artist in animation. Improving your artistic skill levels comes with strong commitment and hundreds of hours of dedicated practice.

As we go through this process of copying, tracing, breaking down, researching, analyzing and then producing designs of your own in these established styles - it will have you unlock advancements in your personal artistic growth and you will unconsciously absorb and learn new techniques.

By Patrick Schoenmaker

To help find and develop YOUR own style, you must first explore and test drive many others, as Skottie Young mentions here. There‚Äôs no single one perfect method to learn how to be a character designer. Follow your instincts, along with the opinions, experiences and instructions from various other artists. It‚Äôs up to you to work hard, learn from other people‚Äôs path, their mistakes, their advice, their wisdom, how they learned, what their approach is, and then discover what works best for you, by simply doing it, and practicing it.

The word ‚Äòprocess‚Äô appears over 100 times in this document, and for good reason, because for YOU as a designer, it‚Äôs arguably more important than the final product itself. This course will guide you along the way, showing you how to self-train and self-improve as an artist, putting you on the road to becoming a well-rounded and sought-after character designer.

by Chabe Escalante

Character design has always been a key role in the animation industry. Whether you are designing for film, TV, streaming, games, advertising, and any personal project or simply developing concept art, it can take a lot of work and skill to create an effective and appealing set of characters.

By Chris DeRose

By Dan Muangprasert

Character designers can be employed by the head of production or the animation studio‚Äôs producer, and they can be hired as freelancers, part-timers, full-timers, or as a more permanent resident of the studio, but usually (like all productions) it is contract-based work, where the length of your term depends on the design work available for the department you‚Äôre in.

There‚Äôs several types of designers:

  • Character designer for 2D animation
  • Character designer for 3D animation
  • Character designer for stop-motion animation
  • Character designer for live-action films & VFX
  • Character designer for video games
  • Character designer for branding and advertising
  • Character designer for comics and graphic novels
  • Character designer for mobile games and apps

Designers come from all sorts of backgrounds; illustration, storyboards, comics, and especially 2D animation.

Being aware of the basics of how animation works is a good head start for being an effective character designer:

üëâ Interview with character designer Justin Rodriguez (1) (2)

Like many aspects of animation, similar to storyboarding and writing, character design can feel like a fairly solitary job. Sort of like solving a large puzzle, you navigate through the process, trying many different things, until gradually, things start to look right, and feel right. Where some of the pieces seem to fit together, you start to make progress after many hours or days of experimenting and exploring, only to have some setbacks, and realize and it still isn‚Äôt quite working. Until finally you have that ‚Äúaha!‚Äù moment, it can hit suddenly, when you and others realize that it‚Äôs working and the design is coming together.

There can be times where a client/director loves your first or second attempt, other times, you have lots of people needing to provide their input, opinions, and approvals, so it can take dozens of tries, with many, many versions before you slowly refine things to a final design. The work can sometimes feel very collaborative, other times very isolated. The end goals are always the same, to capture the essence of the character‚Äôs personality in visual form.


Practicing a wide spectrum of established design styles will push you outside your artistic comfort zone, getting you to experiment with techniques that may sit far away from your own personal drawing preference.

  • üëâ Drawing Fundamentals - Practice with all the basic forms
  • üëâ Character Design Essentials - Fundamentals with Nate Wragg

To know HOW to break the rules, we must first KNOW the rules, and know them well. But most importantly; learning the principles makes you see how other characters are conceptualized and constructed. While learning their structure, visual language and the methods behind how they are built - you will inherit new techniques for achieving more striking and appealing designs. There's no better way to learn than by doing, so let's get started.

First off, let‚Äôs grasp the language used in the process of Character Design for Animation‚Ä¶

What is a Model Sheet?

A character design sheet, also known as a model sheet, character board, character rotation, character sheet, character pack, or character study; is a set of illustrations used as a guide for the appearance, poses, views, and gestures of an original character in animated film/series, comics, and video games. These drawings are later used as guides by storyboard artists, modelers, builders and animators as reference to bring the characters to life in the scenes the characters appear in.

To help get you started, here are a few tips on constructing character model sheets!

By Dermot O‚ÄôConnor

By Danny Hynes

Creating characters for the purpose of animation production is a little different from what you may be used to. When it comes to working in this industry, the design process is only one aspect of a vast pipeline, as you can see from this flowchart.

Samples of Typical Model Sheets:

Character Sheets like these are a specific industry-standard format that communicates the design of a character. It can also be used to introduce potential investors to a cast of characters, tell us a little bit about their personalities and relationships, and give us a general sense of what the character looks like and how they might move or behave. These sorts of Character Model Sheets are used in virtually every entertainment field from game design to animation. As shown above, there can be many different types of templates, every studio/production has their own preference. There‚Äôs no right or wrong way to make these sheets, as long as the information is there and clearly presented. Though they can be used in a variety of fields, all character sheets contain roughly the same information: Turnaround views, Expressions, Poses, and sometimes Color. They can go from very simple to quite detailed and elaborate.

Model sheets also show variations for different states for the character, most often for changes on hair style and clothing/costuming:

Model sheets also provide more details on specific parts and assets of the character, along with various angles and poses as well:

These model sheets are like visual guide documents, they are typically illustrated during the pre-production phase of a production for a film or animated series. In feature films, the concept art and visual development stage eventually lead to more final character design sheets to be properly made for the Builds team to use later on. In animated series, the design process is continuous throughout the production since there‚Äôs many stages across each episode that needs to be made.

Observe the image above and below‚Ä¶ Notice how this iconic character would change styles every few years since its creation, these are only two of the MANY design styles he‚Äôs been depicted in.

Main Design Packs are usually the model sheets for all the main cast of characters compiled together. To be used as reference for the story artists and animators.

Sometimes details and specific elements of the character are added to show the mechanics of how the model will work:

Getting to the model sheet stage can be long and arduous, often starting out with lots of explorations to find the correct combination of shapes and forms and proportions that most accurately depict the character‚Äôs persona.

Beginning with research‚Ä¶

Then refinements‚Ä¶

Then after starting over once or twice (or 10 times), a certain character with particular combinations of ph and physical attributes and proportions are picked out, and after many versions and iterations, a turnaround can be constructed‚Ä¶

For a TV/media animated series there‚Äôs an on-going episodic design process, as new sets of character sheets are made for each new story as the scripts get written. When working collaboratively with art directors or production designers, all the characters in the film or series should have their own model sheet created by the character designer. Depending on how large scale the production is, this could be just one or two individuals creating the main characters, or it can be a whole team of artists working together to illustrate a large cast of characters. With multiple episodes always overlapping and many aspects of the design process needing to be made for each character, like Turnaround Views, Expressions, Poses, Head Angles, Mouth Charts and Color - there can be lots of coordination and organization needed in order to meet the needs of the production, along with a strict list of weekly deadlines for each episode to keep everything always progressing forward and on track with the schedule.

How to use a Model Sheet:

Character design sheets should be used as a guide for any members of the creative team who will need to work with the character. They should have a simple layout that is easy to understand without too much clutter. When it comes to drawing characters for design sheets, it is important that you keep things relatively neat to avoid any inconsistencies in the characters‚Äô design further down the line. When you begin to develop and conceptualize your character, you‚Äôre free to be very rough and messy. Once things get refined and polished off, the model sheets are meant to display a more clean, formal and official version of how the character looks.

There are many different ways to construct model sheets‚Ä¶

Purpose of Model Sheets

Model sheets are visual reference guides used in animation to ensure consistency in a character's appearance, proportions, and gestures across multiple artists and scenes. They provide a standardized visual style, including various angles, poses, and expressions.

Model sheets were first created in the 1930s, once animation production began to be truly developed as a pipeline for the first time. Where artists with certain specialties had specific tasks within the departments they were in. In animated 2D feature films often times the supervising animator for a character would be heavily involved in their character‚Äôs design.

üëâ The Cartoon Art of Preston Blair

Key Purposes of Model Sheets.

  • Consistency and Standardization:
    • Model sheets ensure all artists working on a project understand and adhere to the same character design, preventing inconsistencies in appearance and scale.
  • Visual Reference:
    • They act as a visual blueprint, detailing the character's structure, proportions, attire, and even smaller elements like hands and feet.
  • Demonstrate Range:
    • Model sheets can show a character's basic range of facial expressions and typical poses to reveal personality and aid in animating specific actions.
  • Character Turnarounds:
    • Multiple views (front, side, 3/4, back) are included to show the character's design from every necessary angle, which is essential for 360-degree representation.
  • Guidance for Multiple Artists:
    • In large productions with numerous artists, model sheets are critical for unifying the collective effort, making it look as though one artist created all the character art.

What Model Sheets Include.

  • Character Turnarounds:
    • Drawings showing the character from different angles (front, side, back, 3/4).
  • Expression Sheets:
    • Sketches illustrating various facial expressions and emotional reactions.
  • Pose Sheets:
    • Examples of common stances and body language.
  • Proportion Guides:
    • Horizontal lines are often included to help maintain consistent proportions for key features like the head and torso.
  • Design Details:
    • Specific elements like clothing, accessories, and even color palette can be included to provide comprehensive guidance.

1940s, Fleischer Studios:

1940s, MGM Studios:

There are many uses for these across several different industries.

Model sheets can be made to plan out the design and construction for stop-motion puppet rigs:

Creating a Character Model Sheet for 2D Animation

When you think of animation, your favorite Warner Brothers cartoon or classic Disney films may come to mind. There are two primary types of frame-by-frame animation used: hand-drawnand rigged animation.

Hand Drawn:

This animation method requires the animator to draw each frame of the animation by hand, illustrating small changes between each frame to create the illusion of movement, also known as Traditional or Classical Animation.

Animators use the model sheets to learn how to draw the character at any angle, with consistency, scene-by-scene.

This techniquerequires each frame of the character‚Äôs full body (or select layers/body parts) to be redrawn, between 8 to 24 drawings per second, depending on the desired speed and smoothness of the action.

How do animated TV shows and movies keep a consistent art style with so many different animators?

Before any animator starts working there are lots and lots of model sheets created for each character that gives every single detail about how they should look and feel, so the animators know how to keep it consistent. Character Model Sheets are essential for consistency and provide a guide to artists. Ideally, producers, directors, animation directors, art directors or lead animators will work to maintain consistency in the artwork. To stay ‚Äúon model‚Äù is the name of the game, and most professional, serious artists in the industry have strong draftsmanship and can capture character.

Normally, animators are split into teams working on a particular character on 2D animated feature films. That way, an animator just has to coordinate with a small team, not every other animator on the film. Those animators are chosen for their particular strengths. Some animators are really great with animating animals, for instance. Some are better animating male characters. For each character there is a lead animator who is in charge of coordinating their team. To the trained eye, on both feature films and television shows, you can see the individual artists‚Äô styles creep in.

In TV/streaming animated series, usually animators are assigned specific scenes or sequences of scenes at a time and they need to animate everything within that scene, minus 3D props or 2D traditionally-animated FX, but otherwise, all characters within all their scenes. Sometimes on more hybrid series (that have more traditional animation in them), you‚Äôll have two teams making two separate passes on each designated scene, one team for just the key posing part of the animation and another team for in the inbetweens animation.

How can newbie hand drawn animation artists make their art look professional and smooth like realistic animation?

Professional traditional animators, and I mean the very good ones, started animating when they were teenagers and drawing even younger. For many artists, animators included, art is an obsession that starts early in life. By the time they‚Äôve graduated high school and college, and become professionals, they already have thousands of hours of practice.

You also have to consider that high end animation is a chain process that involves several steps. The person that draws the poses/keyframes might not be the same person that draws the in-betweens, and is not the person that cleans, inks and paints. You might be looking at one frame of animation not realizing it is the combined work of 3 or 4 artists. So there‚Äôs a lot of work and technique in professional-looking animation.

What you can do is use a technique called limited animation, in which you make fewer drawings per second and/or hold poses with minimal movement for lengths of time. You separate the elements of an animated character in different layers, like the body from the arms, the mouth, the eyes, and animate only the elements needed. For example, if the character is talking you want to animate the mouth and perhaps the eyes but you don‚Äôt need to redraw the entire body on every frame. You can get away with drawing the body once and using that same drawing for many seconds at a time. That gives you the opportunity to draw a very good character that will stay on for a long time and save time by only animating the mouth.

As you can see, there can be many variations of how model sheets can look.

The visual style of the production and its technical limitations will determine in what level of detail the designs have to be expanded and elaborated upon. The more hand-drawn the animation style is, then the more angles & perspectives the production will be storyboarded in. Therefore the more references you need to create for the builds and animation crew.

Rigged Animation:

This style of animation requires animators to create a puppet rig of their character. This is done by illustrating all of the characters‚Äô body parts and props on different layers and attaching them together to create joints, also known as 2D Puppet Animation.

All the elements and body parts are drawn digitally, piece by piece, assembled and built together as computer generated artwork that can be moved and manipulated around, just like puppetting a rig around frame by frame.

For the most part, rigged characters are meant to look, move, and feel as close to traditionally animated characters as possible, they simply have libraries of pre-designed body parts for animators to call upon, cycle through, and use and controllers to be able to puppeteer the character with virtual bones, joints, and handles to navigate the build with.

When it comes to designing for 2D animation, keeping your designs relatively simple is the best way to ensure an easy conversion to animation. However, technology and software for allowing complex designs to be still well-animated are being improved every year.

Example of simple vs complex:

Example of simple vs complex:

When possible, small details that do not add much to a character‚Äôs personality should be removed during this simplification process. Sometimes the client/showrunner/art director has requirements that need to be met, so there can be lots of versions and revisions made in order to fine-tune the designs to be to everyone‚Äôs liking.

üëâ Watch this clip demonstrating the different types of animation and how they work.

Most 2D animated series nowadays are a hybrid between traditional and rigged animation methods. In an effort to make it look as classically hand-drawn as possible in appearance but still benefiting from the use of rigs with reusing body parts and tweening the animation from pose to pose, with combining the principles of classical traditional animation - this way it makes it appear more hand-made and less computer-generated, therefore looking more like an animated picture book or comic book, giving the characters and backgrounds more appeal and a hand-drawn / hand-painted style.

Learn more about the merging of animation styles and how different technologies are used to achieve various forms of art direction here.

Some series are completely frame-by-frame animated but still paperless and entirely 2D/digital, simply made to look and move like the old classical style, right down to the pencil graphite textured line work:

No matter what the art style is, or the method and approach in which it will be animated, it always starts off as rough concept sketches by the designers, and it builds up from there.

Iconic legacy characters from long-lasting franchises have evolved a lot through the decades.

Creating a Character Model Sheet for 3D Animation

Creating a character model sheet for 3D/CGI animation is nearly identical to the process for 2D with only a few key differences. To help the artists who will be creating the 3D model of your character design, it is best to illustrate characters in a T-pose, displaying both the line art version and the color version as separate images. This is because the character modeler will import the design into their CGI software as a guide, so the character‚Äôs outlines need to be easily visible, UV mapping and shading comes later for the coloring portion of the modeling. As you‚Äôll see from the samples below, certain details on angles and views need to be shown in more precise details, so the sculpting of the 3D model can be built more accurately.

The T-pose is more common in designing characters for 3D animation since the modeling aspect of translating the designs into sculpted builds requires the character to have the arms stretched outwards to properly set up their rigs.

Alternatively there‚Äôs the A-Pose, which means (‚Äúanatomical pose‚Äù in medical illustrations):

A lot of thought and work is put into the character‚Äôs appearance the most functional and practical:

üëâ The Importance of Character Design for CG-animated performances

Character designs for Video Games can often demand a higher level of detail and realism:

Creating a Character Model Sheet for Stop-motion Animation

Character design sheets are made in a similar way for stop-motion/claymation puppet production as well. Illustrating full turnaround views, the many expressions and mouth shapes for lip sync, and full breakdowns for rotations and costume changes.

Compare rough designs to their finished 3D stop-motion models:

The character sheets become reference for the model builders that must hand sculpt and fabricate the body parts, mouth shapes, expressions and clothing needed to create the puppets, including those who make CG model versions for 3D-printing purposes.

Knowing how your designs will be brought to the next stages can help inform your decisions on how better to design them - which will provide more accurate references for those artists that will be building your creations. Whether it‚Äôs for 2D, 3D or Stop Motion, it‚Äôs important to know how your artwork will be turned into models/builds for animators to use. For example, see how these studios created their puppets for Pinocchio, ParaNorman, Kubo, Isle of Dogs, Coraline, and Missing Link.

Research & Preparation

The start of any foray into character design begins with plenty of research and rough sketching, then more research and more sketching. Whether you‚Äôre creating a character entirely on your own from imagination, or you‚Äôre designing characters for someone else - you will need to start gathering and collecting info and producing material for yourself to use as a starting-off point.

üëâ Greg Broadmore talks about fighting self-doubt and getting into your flow-state.

Oftentimes, as a character designer, you‚Äôre working off of a chosen design style, this is your reference to start from, you must LEARN this style in order to create NEW characters that fit with this established look.

But other times you have to start from scratch, with an empty canvas, a blank page, the style is not created yet, and it‚Äôs up to you to FIND that style. So you must discover it for yourself and you need to start somewhere‚Ä¶

Sometimes your client can begin with specific concepts in mind - for example, you can get a note saying ‚ÄúWe are looking for a Vin Vogel type of style, let‚Äôs start there‚Ä¶‚Äù.

It makes your job easy in the beginning, because you have a place to start. Right away, your research has a target look.

You search up Vin Vogel‚Äôs illustrations ‚òùÔ∏è and his picture books, then make a few dozen sketches inspired by this imagery.

Create new rough character drawings according to your client‚Äôs specs. Begin exploring and work your way towards more refined designs.

Other times, just getting started can be more challenging - your client/director isn‚Äôt sure where to begin, they can be vague and ambiguous, only knowing the age or gender or only having a short description of the personalities & backstories of the cast of characters you‚Äôre meant to design. That‚Äôs when YOU find some images of many different styles to present them, and ask which ones inspire them most.

Once you get those answers, then you can narrow the focus of your research and do rough sketches with a bit more direction. If they still don‚Äôt have much information to give you, then you begin with a broad and wide range of styles; grab imagery online, make an image board (some call it a mood board or look board), and present it to your client. This may take several attempts to narrow down the focus of a design style that appeals to them. You don‚Äôt have to start sketching anything until they point out to you which styles they find most appropriate. With the feedback you get from them, you now have a place to start.

In your researching journey you‚Äôll discover other similar art styles that you can use as additional inspiration. For example, in this case, the looser and sketchier designs of Fred Blunt might have aspects you could use:

After 100+ years of illustrated and animated characters to have as potential reference, there are countless styles to be inspired from. Often you‚Äôll need to borrow aspects from several ones and combine them, to develop something original and to match any descriptions given to you, but creating image boards like these can have your client begin to pick which ones they prefer most, and then you have somewhere to start.

Preliminary Process

It‚Äôs time for information gathering - There‚Äôs a few approaches to take for the research and discovery phase of your original character design. The first, which may feel the most satisfying and fun to many of us, is brainstorming (to figure out what it is you are looking for). The second is to really dive in with an intensive research and study. The third, is to create a mood board or reference sheet for yourself, this is a collection of visual themes and imagery that connects with you and the character you‚Äôre trying to create (especially if you are really starting from scratch). This could mean spending some time on stock image sites like Google Images or searching through artists‚Äô portfolio sites like Art Station, DeviantArt and the big one‚Ä¶ Pinterest (examples: Cats / Horses / Anthro Creatures / Alien Creatures / Centaurs / Griffins / Chibi / Vikings / Ninjas / Knights / Pirates / Cowboys / Robots / Steampunk).

üëâ The first steps are: Thinking >> Researching >> Collecting

What style are you looking to convey? Is this for a client? Based on an existing property? Your own creation? Is there a source material like a comic book, children‚Äôs picture book, novel, or game to reference? Does your producer/art director have a design style already in mind? What themes and topics will these designs have to say? What world or time period is the story set in? Put some thought into what personalities you want to visualize and what moods & identities you want to explore. Research can mean looking up illustrators and artists you admire and setting a framework in place to model after, or collecting a wide range of design styles to assemble together to show the client, all to get feedback on what direction to start with. This includes searching for places, objects, and people who may provide more subtle or indirect inspiration for your work.

How to Find References for your Character Design.

Example - Let‚Äôs say you need to design some medieval/RPG/fantasy characters in some sort of D&D style world:

  • Start where you‚Äôre comfortable, with a brainstorm
  • Dive deep into the subject, with an intensive study
  • Whether it‚Äôs analog or digital, get lost in the gathering of inspiration

The idea is to find a wide range of styles, then narrow it down to what feels most appropriate for the character(s) you‚Äôre creating.

The most memorable characters are the ones that make an impression, so getting their individual attributes and personalities correct is key, and this can take lots of experimenting and trials-and-error to get there. Think about some of your favorite characters from movies, television, internet and literature ‚Äî they all started as an idea, perhaps partially based off of a real person the author/creator knew, and was then developed and visualized with careful intention and hard work.

How a Character‚Äôs Choice of Clothing Benefits the Story.

When writing or designing a character, it‚Äôs important that everything you include serves a purpose. The character‚Äôs name, their favorite color, and their choice of companion give an impression about the character, the world around them, and the past that shaped who they are. The more you amplify these elements, the greater the image you‚Äôre painting for the reader and to the viewer.

For example, say the plot calls for the character to buy a car, and they choose a blue one. ‚ÄúBlue‚Äù is a necessary detail to include in the narrative, but by giving this specific decision meaning, you make it important. Perhaps blue was their parent‚Äôs favorite color, and they strive to please them. Perhaps blue is a calming color, and it reflects their calm personality. This makes the narrative richer.

In the same way that characters have ways to emote and show their feelings with their faces and body language, characters also need clothing (at least most of them do), so why not make it a useful element in the story? Take advantage of this opportunity to tell the reader something. Here are three things showing how your character‚Äôs choice of clothing can be used to amplify the story.

  1. It Says Something About Their Personality: The way a character dresses can reflect their tastes, views, and emotions. For example, your boisterous character might be best dressed in colorful shorty shorts to reflect her free spirit. Her sass and disregard for other‚Äôs opinions is what tells us her personality, but this small addition has made the fact visual as well as mental. In another case, your reserved and slightly distrustful character might be better dressed in a bulky designer coat; this reflects on his desire to be enclosed but also regarded as superior. Your character‚Äôs personality is independent, but the proper clothing can complement and amplify their unique views.
  2. It Says Something About The Setting: Dressing your characters in clothing that reflects their setting will reinforce this new atmosphere in the reader‚Äôs mind. Colors give impressions all on their own; dark colors such as greys or blacks will match the dark atmospheres. If you‚Äôre trying to show a contrast between two groups - one more successful and the other starving - dressing one group in bright pinks or yellows will give a sense of light and energy, making them seem healthier. The style also reflects the world; conservative dress such as long sleeves or coats can reflect order or oppression, while less conservative clothing can show rebellion and freedom. Your world itself will determine the setting, but clothing can complement it, amplifying the atmosphere.
  3. It Can Say Something About Them Physically: In addition to symbolism, clothing can serve a literal purpose. If you have a young character, dressing them in outfits which are colorful and airy can complement their younger attitudes. If you have a character with an embarrassing scar or injury they wish to hide, constantly dressing them in long sleeves, despite weather, can subtly reveal this. These details can amplify the characters themselves but also open new doors for foreshadowing.

Every detail included in your design should serve a purpose, and perhaps even tell a story within a story. Seemingly minor aspects such as a character‚Äôs style of clothes and hair, or the choice of colors in their outfits, can breathe new life into the details of your narrative.

Build Your Visual Library:

Seek out and collect reference material everywhere you can.

To begin - explore the various avenues online to get your creativity sparked and ready to go.

Collect sites with resources on character development, sift through archives of design packs, and have them saved and ready at your fingertips for quick reference. And most of all, seek out tips and advice on style and technique, and always search out daily inspiration.

An option to explore sometimes is to create a mood board (inspiration boards / reference boards). These are the perfect place to work out what the foundation of your design style could be. Knowing basic details about your characters will help you create references for your image board throughout the writing or drawing process - and will keep you focused in the direction you need to go towards for creating believable characters.

Reference Sheet for the visual development of the short film Phantom Limb:

Backstory.

If possible - as part of your initial brainstorming session, create the character‚Äôs backstory, it doesn‚Äôt have to be super detailed. If someone else created the character, then find out what their backstory is by asking them (the writer/creator/director) of the production. But if you are creating this character entirely on your own, then start with a few words or short sentences. Write a summary of their personality traits and history, it can be point form, with a list of traits, or write a short blurb about who they are. This can be a simple chart about who this character is and try to capture their key essence. What kind of character do you want to bring to life? What kind of character do you need to tell your particular story?

Some questions you want to ask yourself are:

  • How old are they?
  • Where are they from?
  • What do they like?
  • Are they shy, fierce, serious, goofy?
  • What do they sound like?
  • What do they do in their free time?
  • What kind of music do they listen to?
  • What does this person‚Äôs day look like?

The answers to these questions can change also, just as the character‚Äôs appearance will evolve before the time comes that the final design is done and approved, so too can the character‚Äôs personality. Whether this is a character entirely of your own creation, or from the mind of your client/boss - either way, answering these sorts of questions will help inform your decisions in how to visualize your character.

The character‚Äôs backstory will give us ideas of what makes them unique and what kind of wardrobe/accessories they could have. This will also make the development for poses and attitude drawings flow more naturally and convincingly later on. Asking these kinds of questions before you start researching and designing allows for a better understanding of your character and their personality/history. It‚Äôs also possible that all you‚Äôll have to go by is a written paragraph describing the character, or maybe even a brief conversation with the creator.

When reading a script you are using for the information found in it to help you gain insight on your character‚Äôs personality. To get you started, you will be searching for characterizations and clues to their disposition and attitude. What is an example of characterization? Direct characterization tells the script reader about the character - what they look like, their personality traits, what they like, what they dislike, and their appearance. For example, an author may write: "Charlotte was a frizzy-haired bookworm." In just a few words we have a hint of their personality and physical attributes.

Characterisation by definition: The making of a character by constructing a description of the distinctive nature or features of someone or something.

Visual characterization is the process through which an artist communicates a character‚Äôs appearance to an audience. In writing, characterization is achieved through dialogue, actions, and descriptions - In animation, illustration, video games, it is achieved through the character‚Äôs design and physical representation.

Remember, if you are creating a character, you are also helping to develop their personality, and by doing so, you essentially become a writer.

  1. You start with the personality. Once that‚Äôs done it‚Äôs relatively easier to know what they look like. You sculpt and pick virtues and vices, flaws and qualities ‚Äî perfect characters are not interesting. When it comes to protagonists and antagonists, they‚Äôre neither 100% good nor 100% bad, because there are not fully good or bad people. Get what I‚Äôm saying? Round characters are the thing we‚Äôre going for. Take details from people in real life, if you want: funny habits, mannerisms, what makes people human.
  2. Work on the appearance of your character. What‚Äôs their body type, their eye color, skin color, hair color, shape of their face/nose, if they have birthmarks or scars somewhere.
  3. Pick a name as you see fit. This can be the first step depending on how you work. Is there a meaning behind it? Does it somehow show their character‚Äôs personality? Remember sometimes they are relevant to the setting/genre.
  4. Flesh. ‚ÄôEm. Out. Think of hobbies and background/history. How‚Äôs the relationship with their family and friends, how they act around authority, what kinds of clothes they like to wear‚Ä¶
  5. Always remember: character development is an ongoing thing. You never ‚Äúfinish‚Äù developing your character, just like we, as people, don‚Äôt stay the same.

Focus on Authenticity

  • Realistic Personality: Give your character a unique personality with likes, dislikes, quirks, and firm opinions, rather than a generic"wise and courageous heroine" archetype.
  • Believable Backstory: Provide a backstory, even if tragic, that actually explains the character's current state and motivations, rather than being a generic detail.
  • Avoid Self-Inserts: Don't create a character that is a thinly veiled version of yourself. Instead, aim for multi-dimensional characters with both virtues and vices.

The 3 Categories:

  • Primary Characters - The main characters of the story, seen the most often on-screen, they drive the plot forward, we follow them on their journey, we see the narrative through their point of view, the show is about them or revolves around their actions.
  • Secondary Characters - The supporting cast, seen less often, recurring characters in the story, sometimes they can have as much dialogue or screen-presence as the main characters, but they play a supporting role to the primary cast.
  • Incidental Characters - Background characters, the focus is rarely on them, these could be pedestrians walking in the distance, random civilians populating the scenery, and citizens in crowds or scattered around in the backdrop of the scene. In live-action, these are referred to as Extras.

Example of incidental characters from various design styles:

The 4 main types of characters:

  1. Static characters are characters who never change during a story.
  2. Dynamic characters are characters who go through an inner change within a story.
  3. Flat characters are underdeveloped, one-dimensional characters without a layered backstory, no motivations, or internal conflicts.
  4. Round characters are characters with well-developed strengths, weaknesses, goals, and relatable flaws.

A "character arc" is the transformation or inner journey a character undergoes throughout a story, where they start as one type of person and gradually change into a different version of themselves due to the events and challenges they face within the narrative. Not all characters will have an arc.

Static vs.¬†Dynamic is a different dichotomy than Round vs.¬†Flat:

  • Static vs.¬†dynamic refers to whether or not a character changed and evolved throughout the story.
  • Round vs.¬†flat refers to whether or not a character has depth and layered characteristics.

By Bastien Pouchier

Starting off with these foundations can help you distinguish your character development and properly balance their stories in more satisfying ways. From here, you branch out into other categories, like choosing their archetype‚Ä¶

Archetypes for Memorable Characters:

  1. The Hero or Warrior: A brave and confident character who is working to help a cause. Heroes are typically the main character and you will see a lot of square shape language in their designs. This archetype is perfect for characters that stand out and really care about protecting others. An alternative would also be the Everyman, this archetype is ideal for helping the reader or audience see themselves in the story. These types of characters are ordinary people, often thrown into extraordinary circumstances. These protagonists don't need to have any special talents and are often reluctant to continue their journey, even though it's usually the right thing to do. They are often Humble, Sympathetic, Overwhelmed, Quietly Courageous.
    • The Incredibles (2004)
  2. The Rebel or Outlaw: A character who is talented but who may not work for any cause or dislikes authority figures. This type of character is often known as an anti-hero, the anti-hero, a character who lacks traditional characteristics that are associated with a conventional hero. While typically guided by the best of intentions, the anti-hero is a classically flawed character with a history of bad decisions and a questionable moral code. They still really stand out but can often be more morally gray than your typical hero/protagonist. You will see a lot more triangular shape language used in this archetype. The fallen hero or the anti-villain are also options to explore, while the classic are more of the conventional villain.
    • Megamind (2010)
  3. The Explorer: These characters are rugged, free-spirited, and always looking to discover new things. Confident, smug, adventurer. Explorers are typically a combination of square and triangle shape language, providing them with a lot of interesting design possibilities!
    • Sinbad: Legend of the Seven Seas (2003)
  4. The Creator or The Innocent: An inventive and creative character - often nerdy and introverted. This character style will typically have a combination of curves and angular shape language. It is also common for creators to have an exaggerated, large head size to communicate their intellectuality. Traits - Driven, Asocial, Fixated, Perfectionist. An alternative in this category is The Orphan, another widely used archetype that's fairly easy to spot. The Orphan doesn't have to be a child, although it's common that they are. This character type doesn't even have to lose his or her parents to be an Orphan in this sense. Sometimes Orphans have abusive or absentee parents and are looking for the ‚Äúfamily‚Äù that they can be happy with.
    • Meet the Robinsons (2007)
    • Puss in Boots: The Last Wish (2022)
    • Up (2009)
  5. The Ruler: A leader character who is focused on maintaining order. This archetype usually depicts a controlling character with very strong features. Shape language for these characters can vary, however, square shape language is always a solid foundation. An alternative is also, The Leader, they are a well-known archetype that often mixes well with others, such as the Hero and the Warrior. The Leader has a goal and a plan to get there, which makes for conflict when something happens to derail the character from his or her goal. Leaders have the ability to inspire action in others. Since power attracts all types, the Leader can come as a hero or a villain.
    • Spider-Man: Into the Spider-Verse (2018)
  6. The Magician: An eccentric character who is able to create/wield magic. You can have a lot of fun with exaggeration in these characters - their shapes are typically angular and whimsical.
    • Sword in the Stone (1963)
  7. The Caregiver: A caring character who has a soft, comforting appearance. Caregiver designs use primarily circular shapes, giving them a wholesome, huggable aesthetic. These traits make them a typically cute character type.
    • Big Hero 6 (2014)
  8. The Jester: A comic relief character with a quirky often endearing appearance. This is another archetype where you can experiment with exaggeration! Jester‚Äôs design will often veer away from the confines of the ideal beauty standard in a way that can be both comical and comforting. As an alternative, this can also be The Trickster or The Sidekick, always a staunch ally, supporting sidekick characters come in all shapes and sizes. You've seen them as robots, humans, pet animals, and supernatural creatures. Helpful, Devoted, Humorous, Loyal (sometimes to a fault). Examples - Ron Weasely from Harry Potter, R2D2 from Star Wars, Tinker Bell from Peter Pan. Olaf from Frozen, Timon & Pumbaa from The Lion King, Patrick from Spongebob.
    • Lilo & Stitch (2002)
  9. The Lover: A character who is devoted to what they love. There is no right way to depict the lover, as different characters will be attracted to different appearances. However, if you are stuck, using features that are usually associated with society‚Äôs beauty ideals can be a good place to start.
    • Enchanted (2007)
  10. The Sage or Mentor: Also known as The Herald, a character who guides other characters ‚Äì typically portrayed as old and wise, perhaps monk or wizard-like. This character could be merged with The Magician as well. This archetype often has a combination of circular and angular shape language.
    • Kung Fu Panda (2008)
    • Clone Wars (2008)

There are many other sub categories as well, like The Character Foil, a character who contrasts another, usually the Protagonist, in order to highlight his/her traits.

The Lord of the Rings (2001)

Once you are aware of these archetypes you will be armed with more ammunition to create designs that will clearly communicate your character‚Äôs personality. If none of these categories feel quite right for your character, you can always combine multiple tropes or motifs, or subvert them completely to create a more unique design!

Trends in storytelling and character development for archetypes have evolved over time. There are many other classes to explore and choose from, see here for more lists, and here for more stock character archetypes.

Other classifications that often overlap many of the identities outlined above are The Protagonist and The Antagonist, these are usually the two central characters of a story. A protagonist is the main point-of-view character, while the antagonist is the opposing force who stands in the way of the protagonist's goals. The protagonist is often the hero of the story, while the antagonist is usually the villain. These are usually tied to one or several types of conflict found within the core of the story.

On a deeper level, you must always ask yourself - what does your character represent within the story and world that is being shown? Are they symbolizing a thing or abstraction as a person? For example - Personification allegory is a type of allegory in which a fictional character represents a concept or a type. Symbolic allegory is one in which a character or material thing is not merely a transparent vehicle for an idea. Often these concepts are not for the designer to decide upon, but rather they should have these concepts described to them, to help in the discovery process of visualizing the character. If you are the creator of this story and the world they live in, or helping to develop it with others, then these are factors you may want to consider - since it could affect the look of your character design.

A classic example of a personification allegory is the character "Everyman" from the medieval play of the same name, where "Everyman" represents the universal human experience and embodies concepts like mortality, virtue, and sin, essentially giving human qualities to an abstract idea like "the average person." In symbolic allegory, the characters have an autonomous narrative outside of the messages they convey. The character Virgil in Dante's Divine Comedy, from 1320, is a symbolic allegorical figure because he represents both the historical author of the Aeneid and the human faculty of reason.

The Art of Personification.

It was long common in the visual arts to use human figures to represent a range of natural phenomena, personal qualities, abstract conceptions, and so on. The ancient Greeks and Romans have literature and mythologies depicting these; many of their gods and goddesses represented their own single things, be it Dawn (Eos, Aurora), Wisdom (Athena, Minerva), or War (Ares, Mars). In Roman mythology, Justitia was one of the four Virtues, she was often portrayed as a stately woman, blindfolded, holding scales and a sword, each became a personification of that phenomenon or quality or concept, in this case, Justice. Inspired by classical art, Renaissance painters and sculptors likewise began producing thousands of artistic personifications -- Time, or Folly, or France, or Vice, or Poetry, or the Americas. And in the 18th century English-speakers began using the word itself. Today artists are less inclined to such depictions, and the word gets used more often to describe actual individuals; when we call someone the personification of style, or greed, or loyalty, we mean the ideal or epitome and embodiment of that quality.

Who is the Demographic?

When you design a character, it is important to know who your audience is. For example, a design that appeals to young children may not always appeal to adults. So before you start your design, take a moment to think about your audience (and the target demographic of the production they‚Äôre in. How old are they? Are they aimed at any gender in particular? Answering these questions will allow you to cater your designs more toward your audience and therefore ensure your character is more engaging for them, making the property more successful.

The Main Categories:

  • Preschool (3-5)
  • Bridge (5-7)
  • Kids (6-11)
  • Co-viewing/Teen
  • Adults

Another way to classify the audience age groups is with the rating system found in media and video game guidelines:

What is the Genre?

The world and story your characters will be placed in will directly affect the style they will be visualized in, the environments will influence the look of your final character designs and their look will also influence the design style of the world they‚Äôre in. A dark animated horror film for adults will have a very different aesthetic to the design work compared to a whimsical adventure series for preschool kids. For example, here‚Äôs a the main genres:

Comedy, Action, Drama, Fantasy, Thriller, Adventure, Mystery, Science Fiction, Musical, Educational.

There are several sub-genres as well, and you can mix these up too, most series and films are categorized with at least two combined.

  • Rick and Morty is considered to be Science-Fiction / Action / Comedy (and even Dark Comedy)
  • Over the Garden Wall could be known as Fantasy with brief elements of Horror / Comedy / Musical / Drama
  • Gravity Falls is seen as Adventure / Mystery / Comedy
  • Teenage Mutant Ninja Turtles is described as Action / Adventure
  • Bluey is known for being an Educational / Comedy
  • The Grim Adventures of Billy & Mandy is a Supernatural / Comedy / Slapstick / Surreal Humor

Defining these isn‚Äôt too important (and not usually your job as the designer), just be aware that knowing the Genre and Demographic can give you an idea of what the art direction might look like. Sometimes you dive into it and you accidentally discover what‚Äôs most appropriate for the character, and this can influence or change the intended personality and art direction as well. Your client or director should be the one to provide you with this sort of information. If you are working alone and creating the character entirely yourself, then determining what the Genre and Demographic is for this world and its characters can help to get you started on the look of your characters. If you only have to go off of a script or text descriptions, then re-read it often, to get as much info and inspiration from it as you can.

Sometimes subverting the look of the character designs is the goal as well; like the series Drawn Together, purposefully mixed various styles, but was a series made for a mature/adult audience. Gumball, mixes styles too, and it‚Äôs made for kids 6-11, however Smiling Friends mixes styles in a similar way but is intended more for teens & adult audiences. Some terms and examples of mixing styles together are on display here.

Inspiration

Research more and more, explore lots of options, discover new and different artists, saving collections of examples of the potential styles you may need to refer to later. Doing this makes certain you‚Äôve considered all your possibilities before starting another round of rough drawings. There‚Äôs thousands of character art styles out there, it‚Äôs easy to be overwhelmed, begin your searches as broad explorations, then refine it down to the ones that you feel fit best with what you‚Äôre doing and what you‚Äôre looking for.

Narrow down your search to what the world/story your characters are set in, and focus on what their personalities may be. If you‚Äôre working with a client or director, gather as much information as you can from them, to determine what parameters or limitations or pre-established art style they may have in mind to draw inspiration from. Seek out, find, build, gather information & references for you to use as a springboard for your rough exploration sketches.

The goal is to organize all of your inspiration in one place so that you can reference it as you work.

Become influenced and excited by artists on Instagram, Deviant Art, Tumblr, Art Station and Pinterest - Examples like the art style of Jin Kim, Glean Keane, Jamie Hewlett, or anyone that you find inspiring. Looking at the amazing works of your favorite artists can really help improve your creativity and give you new techniques and ideas to try in your own work. If you got a glimpse of a show you liked, you can always go on IMDB and skim through the full cast & crew section of the name of that show or movie, this way you can find who the designers were, then you can find their portfolios online.

Everyone has different charms in their own way, and paying attention and capturing these details in your characters will make them feel genuine and unique.

Thumbnails

Planning is a critical stage in the process. You‚Äôd be surprised how important it is to really think through and plan your designs before you get started. It‚Äôs extremely important and helps you avoid problems that could show up later, and also discover things that can really enhance and bring out the best in your potential designs.

Thumbnails is a term used in many areas, like roughing out poses for animation:

The quick and loose preliminary sketches in storyboards:

And those tiny title card images you see on YouTube or Netflix:

In Character Design, thumbnails are all the small rough sketches made to hash out your ideas.

While you are collecting references and preparing the info you need, you will begin to have an idea of what you want this character to represent. Start making lots of thumbnails by sketching the character(s) you have in mind, but keep your sketches very loose and rough at this stage.

Make LOTS of sketches! -- This can be the most fun part of the process‚Ä¶

‚ÄòRoughs‚Äô can mean different things to different artists. For example, animation director Glen Keane is known for drawing VERY rough:

Other artists prefer to draw their roughs much cleaner:

Just like your handwriting, everyone has their own unique personal style of sketching.

Doing random doodles can sometimes lead to happy accidents and discoveries.

By Jeremiah Alcorn

Rough Drawings tend to be not as rough as Thumbnails, but are not Clean/Final Lines either. It‚Äôs always your personal preference for how rough you want to be, everyone has their own style on how they produce ‚Äòroughs‚Äô, there‚Äôs no right or wrong method, whatever you feel most comfortable with. Many artists/directors have varying opinions on this, but it doesn‚Äôt matter too much anyways, as long as the IDEAS are clear and presentable. Producers and art directors all have their own preferences for how many stages to take between thumbnails/concept to clean+color, and how rough or final they‚Äôd like to see designs delivered and presented as. Simply go with their requests and adjust accordingly.

  • Thumbnails are VERY loose and sketchy, it‚Äôs lines and shapes that are searching for something.
  • Roughs have most of the details and structure present, they also look fast & scratchy, but all the shapes and volumes are in there.
  • Tie-downs are cleaner than roughs, they‚Äôre almost final lines but not quite, all the details are there.
  • Clean-up/Line art has all the final lines laid down, the lines are refined to the weight and style needed.

You can say there‚Äôs no difference in the volume of these two circles (shown below) and you wouldn‚Äôt be wrong. The one on the right is two-dimensional and so is the one on the left. What is important is what you do not see ‚Äî the thought process behind the lines‚Äîthat helps you to create more dimensional sketches with flow to them.

Don‚Äôt get caught up with the details in the beginning; this is the moment to play with proportions and shapes and see what feels right for the character. Try to come up with a lot of variety at this stage.

Always start off very loose and rough, then draw over your own work, over and over to refine and shape the character. Sketching is NOT about‚Å† refinement. The darker lines show your ‚Äúdecision lines‚Äù and the lighter ‚Äúsearching lines‚Äù become less important. ‚Å†

By Griz & Norm

Quantity will find quality: When starting a sketch, be sure to draw quickly and confidently. Gloss over mistakes and don‚Äôt spend too much time erasing. If you‚Äôre not happy with the way your drawing is going, just start a new layer to build something fresh or iterate again. Through this process, something will eventually click and you‚Äôll know when things feel right. A lot of the time you may not know what you want to draw until it‚Äôs directly in front of your eyeballs.

For example, the story about the pottery teacher is a parable about the importance of quantity over quality when learning something new. The original version was told by photographer Jerry Uelsmann to motivate his Introduction to Photography students at the University of Florida. The story was later retold in the book Art & Fear by David Bayles and Ted Orland, while the art medium changed from photography to ceramics.

In the story, a pottery teacher splits his class into two groups and gives each group a different assignment‚Ä¶

Group A: Make one pot per day for 30 days

Group B: Work on only one pot for the entire 30 days

At the end of the month, the teacher judges the quality of the pots and finds that the highest quality pots came from Group A, the group that made 30 pots quickly. The teacher explains that by focusing on quantity, the students were able to learn from their mistakes and steadily improve their skills. They made 29 pots, one at a time, learning from their faults and missteps, they had to work fast, having to complete one per day, and by the last pot, #30, they had refined their process to create a well-made pot. The students in Group B, on the other hand, became paralyzed by perfection and were left with little to show for their efforts, even though they had a whole 30 days to perfect the one attempt at a single pot.

The moral of the story is that quantity leads to quality, and that it's better to create and improve over time than to focus solely on perfect quality from the start.

There are several ways to do thumbnailing, but it is always meant to be a way to jot down lots of small ideas as a visual rehearsal. They can also be just blocks and blobs and simple silhouettes, or quick gesture drawings made of squiggly lines, or just various shapes and forms to see what works and what doesn‚Äôt work.

Sketches by Jay McKay:

Keep spending time researching and discovering plenty of imagery to inspire you, go online, find the types of design aesthetics, costumes, and environment that your character will be in. Compile lots of references, then start by roughing out some concepts of what the character should look like, explore and experiment with loose & quick sketches. All of this will help you to learn in a more tactical way. A well-researched character will have a lot more credibility and really resonate with their audience. When you are finished researching, you should have enough reference material to start designing your character.

Sketches by Glen Keane:

Digital sketching is a low-stake scenario, so try not to stress too much about drawing. It‚Äôs okay to make mistakes in the beginning. Embrace it and try not to worry about the details. The main goal when doing initial rough sketches is to jot down your wild ideas and then build upon them afterwards. It can look like senseless blobs, but as long as it makes sense to you, you‚Äôre doing the right thing.

Once you‚Äôve created lots of thumbnails:

  • Revisit your most successful sketches and refine them further
  • Pull together the most compelling visual elements into a definitive design

After all this sketching, ideally you create something that you want to push further. At this point you can create a new layer in your drawing program, while also lowering the opacity of the previous one, so you can easily draw on top of it. It‚Äôs important to stay loose and to not stiffen up when starting a ‚Äúcleaner‚Äù drawing. Strengthen the design and the shapes you already put forth. In the past I often strayed too far from the sketch and became unhappy with the drawing. You chose to move forward with your sketch for a reason, so don‚Äôt disregard it too much when fleshing it out.

Shape Language

There are three basic shapes in your toolbelt: round, box, and triangle. If you follow your intuition, each shape conveys a personality. For example:

  • Round = charismatic, harmless, endearing, friendly, soft, cute, innocent (circles, ovals, eggs, beans)
  • Box = reliable, uniform, traditional, stable, balanced - (squares, rectangles)
  • Triangle = movement, harmony, cunning, dynamic, competent (downward pointing more aggressive or villainous)
  • Shapes can also be combined for more complex characters

There‚Äôs a variety of other combinations/variations like diamond-shaped, tear-drop-shaped, oval/egg-shaped, hourglass-shaped, and bean-shaped.

When we deconstruct visual images, most objects will break down into one or more familiar shapes, each carrying their own visual signposts. See how the characters from Pixar‚Äôs Up can be visually broken down to basic shapes.

As you can see from the images above, it can help to think of the human body as a series of relatively simple shapes when you are starting your design.

Squares and rectangles are reliable, rigid and sturdy, possibly stubborn/unchanging too. Visually they‚Äôre used to symbolize order and robustness. Think of a robot or a heavyset man, which are often represented by rectangles in caricature, and note the correlation of sturdiness to each one.

Circles (and ovals, ellipses, egg-shape) are friendly, harmless and fun. Due to the continuous nature of the line in a circle, they also represent progress and synergy. Animated characters made up of the circular look are generally protagonists, though sometimes bumbling or innocuous ones at that. Think about the Powerpuff Girls, Homer Simpson and Santa. Babies, teddy bears, dopey types and jolly characters all fall under this category of cuddly, friendly circular fun.

Triangles are dynamic shapes, indicating both balance and energy. Down-pointing triangles are used in character design for fit, active characters: think of the V-shaped chest of an athletic character, or the classic hourglass figure of a femme fatale which is largely a series of triangles stacked on top of one another.

üëâ Watch this approach to defining cartoon anatomy and shape language

All these main shapes can be squashed, stretched, combined & mixed together:

Shapes are unlimited; it simply comes down to the most important ingredient in design, which is its organization. Break your human character shapes into three parts: head, full torso, and legs.

Head shapes of various sorts can immediately create a distinct personality for your character:

Three-dimensional structure is extruded from these basic shapes:

There are thousands of possibilities when exploring various Shape Dynamics:

See how Porky Pig starts off as a kidney bean-shape:

Shapes can contrast and compliment each other, while having shapes within shapes to balance each other as well:

Look at the many different shape combinations used here:

The shapes that make up the character can symbolize what they represent or give hints as to their personality, background and alignment. Combining shapes together will have their own visual significance depending on their arrangement. A rectangle and triangle together can make a house or an arrow, just as a character design with a rectangular body and circular head could be a dimwitted lackey (large rectangle, small circle) or a mega-mind genius (thin rectangle, large circle). Of course, you can always flip the tables to play with expectations. Maybe the small circular little girl is the powerful dynamo, or the stocky square guy turns out to be unstable and hyperactive.

Advice from Cory Toomey:

Exploration Sketches

A character designer is the artist responsible for conceptualizing and designing all of the characters in an animated production. Sometimes you are given a predetermined style to follow, and therefore very little reference searching is required of you. Other times, a lengthy process of exploration studies at the very beginning is made to discover what the style should be. This specific branch of the design team focuses on using comprehension of design language as well as their considerable draftsmanship skills to create new characters based upon the stories and descriptions provided to you.

See this solid advice from Don Corgi for strategies on design and color.

When you first try to experiment with what your character might look like, produce some rough drawings that allow you to rehearse, explore and discover a variety of shapes, sizes, and proportions to see what feels best. This stage is often referred to as Concept Design or Predesign.

Character Development or Visual Development (VisDev) is what many artists and producers will also call these stages.

In the writing stages; Character development is the process of creating and evolving the personalities, motivations, goals, and emotions of your animated characters. It involves defining their traits, flaws, strengths, weaknesses, relationships, and arcs.

The designers do their best to visualize these concepts, materializing them into a character.

This is one of the most creative parts of the entire production process.

These initial research drawing andrough sketching stages are all about the visual character development process. Creating and evolving the look of your animated characters, first through research than LOTS of sketches. It involves defining their shape language and visualizing their personality, from the lead characters to supporting cast. All with the purpose to drive the narrative and engage the audience on an emotional level. A well-designed character possesses a strong visual aesthetic that instantly captivates the eye. This is not easy to achieve, but through lots of practice, you‚Äôll get better and better at nailing it down sooner and sooner.

Reference. Reference. Reference.

You‚Äôll see and hear about character development - which can be a broad term, often you are not only trying to figure out what the characters look like, but also WHO they are and what their personalities are. As you construct their design it is easy to see how much the personality description and writings obviously influence the evolution of their design, but then the designs can also have an affect on their personality as well.

Similar to how a television actor is influenced by the writings in the scripts they read, what their character does, how they do the things they do, and what they are told to say as dialogue, all shape what the character they are meant to portray. The actor studies the written material, has discussions with writers, directors, producers about performance choices. But the actor themself adds and subtracts their own opinions and qualities to the behaviours, mannerisms and quirks that the character has. Inevitably evolving the character persona into something different than what the original intentions might have been.

Sometimes exploration artwork can be more refined and colored‚Ä¶

There‚Äôs no right or wrong method, it‚Äôs whatever you prefer, and adapt according to what your client prefers on a case-by-case basis.

You‚Äôll see that there‚Äôs a certain charm and tactile energy in rough drawings that can‚Äôt be shown in cleaner line art.

Explorations sketches, conceptual sketches, thumbnailing, blocking out, vis dev, shape design exploration - it can have many names, but it‚Äôs all the same approach; loose, rough, experimental and a visual rehearsal to see what works best. It‚Äôs fast, it‚Äôs messy, and some artists naturally draw with cleaner lines, others like Glen Keane, can be notoriously very rough - no matter what the style, this stage is meant to be all about developing the look of your character.

As you can see from all these examples, this stage of visual development for character concepts is all about trying out lots of different things, to see what looks good, and what works best for the character‚Äôs function and personality and the story/themes being portrayed. Sometimes color concepts are asked for as well to be mixed in with the roughs, especially when there‚Äôs one being fine-tuned to be the final possible design.

These sketches can explore variations of the same design with color concepts included to provide some options and clearer representation of how the character would look, colors are easy to replace later, they‚Äôre not meant to be final quite yet, just a way to see the tones and how they could possibly work.
