How clothing affects a character‚Äôs personality

Researching clothing design can be lots of fun too.

Shapes, forms, proportions, anatomy, costuming and many other aspects of ‚Äústyle‚Äù must be experimented with. The word itself, style, appears nearly 500 times in this document - because it has such a broad definition that covers many aspects of visual art. Once certain parts of a character are nailed down, there may still be other aspects that need further exploration. Usually clothes and hair are specific areas that can be further explored separately to show many more possible varieties, to see what feels best. As they say with actors; the hair styles, make-up and costume choices help to define the characters:

Just like an actor, costuming and hair styling alone can be an entirely grand and long venture to embark upon to discover the many outfits your character can have and which costume should be their default. However, with over 40 years of The Simpsons, you‚Äôll notice they‚Äôve worn the same clothes the whole time. The reason for this is because it can be difficult in the production pipeline to change a character‚Äôs outfits, it‚Äôs more efficient to keep them usually the same as often as possible, when you have a large crew of animators across several studios producing a series.

Concept Art

Exploration drawings (shown above), along with research drawings, visual development art, character development art, predesign art and concept art - all serve the same purpose, and they can be black and white, rough, clean, or color - but these can all be considered the conceptual stage of design. Sometimes concept art is loose and rough character sketches, sometimes it‚Äôs full color, or mixtures of rough colors thrown in with some greyscale tonal studies, or just rough black-and-white line art. It can have roughly painted environments integrated with them, displaying what a snapshot of a scene could look like (color keys or lighting keys), fully illustrated with the character placed in a location. Concept Art is simply short for ‚ÄúConceptual Artwork‚Äù.

Here‚Äôs concept art for ‚ÄòPuckhead‚Äô by Tavis Silbernagel. Developed for a video game that was meant to be a parody/ripoff of Cuphead:

Here‚Äôs some character concept art from Fabien Mense for three different productions, see how his personal style is present through all properties, but always provides lots of visual information and personality:

Character Development Art by Chris Sanders:

Teacher Jazno Francoeur explains: Chris Sanders‚Äô development work on Lilo & Stitch is a masterclass in unified vision‚Äîwhere design, story, and tone emerged from a singular voice. His loose, expressive linework radiates warmth, roundness, and imperfection, making the characters feel handmade and emotionally immediate. These weren‚Äôt just sketches; they were the DNA of the film‚Äôs identity. Sanders, in collaboration with Dean DeBlois, carved a heartfelt, offbeat narrative that broke Disney convention by grounding the story in personal, familial stakes rather than traditional fairy tale tropes. His clarity of vision permeated every department. I recall animating a sequence where the genetic guns blast through the bay doors on the mothership‚Äîmy instinct was to have the blasts scatter randomly. Chris paused and said, ‚ÄúThink of it like a rotating Gatling gun‚Äîclockwise, circular.‚Äù That suggestion reframed the entire moment and made the scene work better in regards to both timing and design. Suddenly, it wasn‚Äôt chaos‚Äîit was controlled rhythm. That‚Äôs Chris in a nutshell - not just a designer of characters, but a shaper of moments, bringing purpose and clarity to even the smallest beat. The film‚Äôs enduring charm owes much to that meticulous yet intuitive touch.

Character designs for VFX/live-action film are similar to video game design work, they tend to be more realistic and detailed.

Pre-Design/VisDev

It‚Äôs all about ‚Äòfinding the look‚Äô -- sometimes the style is already locked in, but you still need to try out lots of different options to see which one works best with the character‚Äôs description and personality...

Character Turnaround

Now that you‚Äôve collected all your references, sketched out lots of concepts and exploration drawings, and started choosing which roughs to nail down for the look of your character, based upon any prerequisites or parameters you might have to follow. You are now beginning to lock down what it is you (and your supervisor/director/client) are looking for. You are now ready to get started in producing the actual individual views of the character design by refining the look and various angles of the character‚Äôs physiology. Here you begin to explore how the structure and anatomy of the character work, it‚Äôs time to begin developing a model sheet for your character!

The Rotation

It starts with the 5 basic views, allowing yourself and others to see how the characters look when rotated and viewed from different sides.

To illustrate a character model sheet or turn-around sheet, typically you will be required to draw your character from 3-5 different angles, to develop a rotation (or partial rotation). Depending on how much of a main or primary character they are in the story, this will determine how many views will need to be produced, the more present they are in the story, the greater the chance the animators will need more reference to know what the character looks like from different sides. Another factor is the style of the animation (will it require lots of turning or changing perspectives), You may need 5, 8 or even 14 views to fully see the character from all views to ensure consistency. But to start off, your primary views will typically include: front, front three-quarter(3/4), side (profile), back and back three-quarter views.

With the initial 5 views completed, you‚Äôll have the foundation to create many more.

1 - Draw the front view of your character

The first pose is the frontal view. Your standard turnaround should have 5-views of the same character: a front view, a back view, a side (profile) view, a 3/4 front view and a 3/4 back view, these all help modelers/builders and animators better understand the volume & structure of the character and what details are seen from various rotations. But a 4-view turnaround works good too, it‚Äôs the bare minimum for most productions, but for asymmetrical characters you may need up to 8-views to accurately get the full rotation, since the left and right sides of their bodies aren‚Äôt mirrored.

Symmetry:

Asymmetry:

Symmetrical:

Also be mindful and producing any draw-throughs, to show any areas that might be covered up by hair, clothing, or overlapping body parts:

A good way to ensure consistency between the different views you will be drawing next is to use one of the poses as a background, and the frontal view is the best one to start with.

2 - Create some guidelines

Once you have your character's frontal view, place some guidelines at different levels to keep consistency between the poses. These lines will help the character have the same height throughout the sequence, but also maintain the proportions of the different parts of its body.

Accuracy in the proportions and positions of all the elements of your character‚Äôs rotation is always important. Creating these guides in Photoshop is very simple. Just drag the mouse from the document rulers. Make sure that the "Snap to..." function is not active because it will make the drawing process difficult.

3 - Draw the 3/4 view

The 3/4 pose next, using the front view as a template. Imagine what the features of the character would look like if they were a little tilted. The nose and other facial features will have to be altered slightly and not quite in the same position. The shoulders, symmetrical in the front view, will now change and have slightly different sizes. The legs will no longer be the same size, either. One will be wider than the other because it will be closer to the imaginary point of view.

Once you have made the first approach to drawing the new 3/4 pose, do the flipping or mirroring method. It will be easier to see if something doesn't have the proportions it should.

It's essential to pay attention to the feet and the relationship with the ground and make sure they don't appear to be shifted.

If your eye level is at the waist, then lines above the mid-section will gradually curve slightly upwards, and lines below the mid-section will gradually curve slightly downwards. See here for more info about this.

All characters follow some sort of perspective rules, same as any geometric shapes:

Notice the ground plane that the character designs stand on, sometimes it‚Äôs flat, like they‚Äôre standing on a flat line, other times they are in perspective.

Pay attention to consistency in foot placement & perspective for the turn-around views:

Also, in this above example, the camera would be in line with her eyes, not her waist, this is more common in character sheets with turnarounds, Notice her collar, sleeves, belt, pants all curve downwards.

Even when certain character designs only follow their own rules of flat-graphically-stylized perspective, some ‚Äòcheats‚Äô need to be made to have the characters look good in whichever views they‚Äôre being depicted in:

4 - Check the guidelines

Once the first rough drawing of the pose is finished, check the guidelines to make sure everything aligns properly. Not all of the elements will at first, but that's ok. Just make adjustments and corrections as needed.

It is essential to spend a good deal of time on this and check carefully because it is better to notice any possible mistakes now than when we are already working with the refined version of the drawing.

One of the most important things to remember when drawing your character turnaround is that each part of your character should line up exactly in each angle. A good way to make sure you are getting your proportions correctly is to draw guidelines that correspond with key elements of your character. Example: top of the head, eyes, nose, mouth, shoulders, and other key parts of the body and costume, and where the joints are.

5 - Draw the side view

For the side view, it is crucial to consider that it is the pose that will show the character's volume, especially for facial features like the nose or lips. Some designers draw the arm as a separate detail in this view or raised up so that it doesn‚Äôt block what the torso looks like, but it's up to you. Don't forget, as with the rest of the poses, to check that every element is properly aligned before refining the lines of your sketch.

6 - Draw the back view and 3/4 back view

The 3/4 back view might be one of the trickiest poses, but don't despair. The back view is more straightforward. Just copy the front view's silhouette and add all the elements in the back of the character. You may never have considered what your character looks like from behind, things like how long the hair is, or what decal might be imprinted on the back of their sweater, for example. These are important details in a turnaround because modelers (builders/riggers) will use them to achieve a more faithful character reproduction.

Then comes cleaning up the linework and choosing final colors (more about this later, in the Color Styling chapter). Often providing a few different color schemes and variants can be helpful if you have a producer/director or client needing to do approvals on your work - offering a few choices can be helpful in arriving at a consensus for the final color pass.

Depending on your chosen line art style, your clean-up pass is essentially tracing your rough artwork with a ‚Äòfinal line‚Äô, ensuring consistency and volumes are maintained. Study other design critiques to learn from other designers' improvements.

Head Angles are an additional aspect that is common to include in more detailed model sheets:

Head angles can be challenging to do, combinations of cheating the perspective to make the angle work while still making it ‚Äúlook good‚Äù is key.

Here‚Äôs another similar approach to the turnaround process‚Ä¶

Rough out some concepts to discover who your character is for their look and personality. Produce exploration drawings to find the shapes, structure, and style you‚Äôre going for:

Then refine your character(s) down to a few possible body types:

Refine and lock down the look and structure of your character. To make your turnaround views, setup your horizontal lines, use them as your guide - rough out basic forms:

Then a detail pass (roughs) to refine the shapes, make decisions on anatomy, clothing, hair:

Line Art pass (clean-up) - pay attention to the accuracy of the rotation, the volumes and proportions of all body parts relative to one and other - the position of all details and any revisions and adjustments you want to make:

Color pass - start off with flat colors as seen here, but this may also include shading & shadows, if that‚Äôs the style you‚Äôre going for:

üëâ Here‚Äôs a great short summary of this process

Finding useful templates (or creating one of your own) can have you be more efficient when reusing the same body types for multiple characters.

Sometimes the drawing of the character in each view is in a different pose, instead of the standard arms resting on their sides or pointing outwards in the same postures for each view, this is purely the preference of the designer and their supervisor/director.

Sometimes, all you need is 2 or 3 views because the amount of screen time they have is short and limited and they do not animate to make full turns in the scenes they are in. This saves you time by not designing more views than is needed, the storyboard will show how much they are moving, so always refer to that. From this information you can determine how many views are actually required, if there are no storyboards to reference, then start off with the first view (3/4) and once that is approved, depending on the time you have and the requirements set out by your director/supervisor, you complete, 3, 4, 5 point turnaround views as your time restrictions allows.

When there‚Äôs a team of designers, often two views are enough to get general approvals from all necessary people in the production. A junior designer or assistant designer then completes all the other views of the turn. The lead character designer or designer supervisor or art director then approved the accuracy of the additional turn around views.

More Views

8-point turnarounds:

In 2D animation, most of the time designers need to ‚Äòcheat‚Äô the look of each individual view since most times they are drawn not how they would actually look three-dimensionally, but more how they should look to appear the most appealing from that angle.

Study these many samples to see how other design styles illustrate their character rotations:

How many views must you create for your character?

This often depends on the production‚Äôs needs and even the time constraints of the schedule. The more primary the character is, the more screen-time they‚Äôll have, the more views and references need to be drawn. Usually the script and storyboards are a good indicator of how much the character will be present on-screen for. The more screen-time they have, the more they‚Äôll act and move around, therefore the more likely you‚Äôll need more views in their rotation for the model sheets to depict how they‚Äôll look from various sides. Main characters will be seen the most and will be in the most episodes (in a series) and therefore will need more references since more robust and refined builds will need to be made since they will be doing the most action and acting.

There‚Äôs also an optional 5/8 and 7/8 views (the views between the Front and 3/4, and between 3/4 and Side), sometimes called Off Centre Left and Off Center Right (depending on which way they‚Äôre facing) or Front Near Left / Front Near Right, these are usually reserved for the main cast of characters, since they get lots more screen-time than incidental or secondary characters. Adding these inbetween views makes for a more complete rotation, examples of this further below.

To distinguish between these inbetween views like 5/8 and 7/8.

Adding in these in-between views creates 10 and 12 point rotations.

10-point turnarounds:

Even more inbetween views can be made, it all depends on the production style of the animation, and what is demanded of the characters in their stories. 12 point Turnarounds can have an additional view between the Side view and 3/4 Back view as well, or additional view between the 3/4 Front view and Side view. You could add more inbetween views for the full rotation, adding up to a 16 point turnaround.

12-point turnarounds:

In addition, there‚Äôs the view between the 3/4 Back and the Back View, these would be called 5/8 Back View, as seen here.

Sometimes you will need to create new poses between each main view of an 8-point rotation, to make it a 16-point turnaround:

It may seem technical or complicated, but when it comes to adding more inbetween views; these are actually easier to draw because it's a very close reference to the views around the ones you‚Äôve already made. It becomes only a slight rotation change, often reusing the same artwork, and only shifting and turning the body parts slightly in perspective to complete these new views.

Turnarounds are not the only type of character model sheet you will want to create for builds artists and animators to reference. Some other common design sheets include: Expression Sheets, Pose Sheets, Color Keys, Hand Models, Mouth Charts, and various Style Guides!

Expression Sheets

Creating an expression sheet is a great way to provide the animators with a basic guide on the way a character expresses different emotions. Try to include a combination of extreme and subtle expressions in your illustrations for these model sheets. Expression sheets are super fun to sketch as you can really explore a range of personalities for your character.

The type of the show and the stories being portrayed decide on the exaggeration level and range that can be pushed for facial expression styles. It can vary from subtle to extreme, experimenting is in order to discover what the limitations are.

More Samples of Expression Sheets:

Depending on the style of the design, your expressions may be reserved and subtle, or quite exaggerated with lots of squash & stretch. There‚Äôs always a range to experiment with between realism and cartoony-ness.

Often poses and expressions can be combined.

How extreme you get to be with the expressions depends on the personality of the character and the style of the production.

Have fun with poses and facial expressions - What you will find helpful in the design stage is sketching the characters in action (poses) as well as their facial expressions, to see what feels right and suits them best. Think about how this character would act in different situations. What kind of faces do they make when they are happy or excited or angry?

Let them emote and communicate! This is the moment when the characters reveal themselves and truly come to life.

Breaking down a character design‚Äôs expressions can be great learning practice:

Create your expressions to be unmistakably clear as to what they‚Äôre doing, how they‚Äôre feeling, and what is happening. Basically, always think about producing strong poses & expressions, not ambiguous ones.

An inspirational example here is some old Tiny Toons expression sheets. There were some fantastic moments and some incredible animation shown in this series, and a few times some crazy over-the-top & hilarious situations were shown. The cartooniest and most enthusiastic artist on the crew was Eddie Fitzgerald. Here are some of his sketches of the Tiny Toons cast:

This Buster character looks pretty conservative for Eddie. His exaggerated style of expressions seem a bit subdued here, but these are still really good, really pushed, and great inspiration.

There's soooo much to learn from these. It's free, loose, energetic, and seemingly effortless drawings of expressions and body language. These show a special love for the character. Late in Eddie‚Äôs career, he actually got to storyboard on the 1999 version of Woody Woodpecker too.

Check out these awesome expressions. You have to be a real cartoonist to realize the creative beauty and humorous potential of Warner Bros' seemingly most conservative character.

These are some more great poses. It's weird to see Porky wearing pants, but somehow Eddie manages to draw it well. He knows what people like about the old Porky Pig character.

You know somebody can really draw if they like to do back poses - and can make them funny! Many artists are afraid of drawing characters from the back - not Eddie. He embraces the backside.

See how he can get perfectly clear poses, construction and attitudes in just a few lines on a tiny thumbnail drawing. Eddie's storyboards would be super fun to lay out, because he's done the hard part for you. You can't go wrong with appealing poses like this.

Here's some Plucky Duck expressions that look way better being rough and exaggerated than the final version that got toned-down on screen.

Eddie was one of the first directors during the idealistic days of Tiny Toons' birth in 1989, and he was the perfect choice for the job. He had a unique vision, a strong individual drawing style and a really funny way of seeing the world. Plus, he intimately knew all the original Looney Tunes cartoons that they were basing these baby versions on.

Use this as inspiration to stay loose in your roughs, be exaggerated, and use the wild poses/expressions as a starting point, then tone down, refine, and afterwards, reduce them down to the style and structure they need to be.

Pose Sheets

Similar to expression sheets, pose sheets/action poses are used to show the type of gestures a character is likely to make. These can be really important to provide your animators with information on the way a character moves in accordance with their personality and how far to push dynamic poses as well. Whilst traditional character turnaround sheets can feel a bit stiff, pose sheets allow for a much wider range of motions and body language to be expressed.

Your poses are meant to be the tip of the iceberg, making the observer wonder: what‚Äôs this character‚Äôs back story, and giving hints as to their personality and intentions.

@randybishopart

  • üëâ Inspiration for poses can be found everywhere, like ‚Äúdynamic action posing‚Äù as we see here.

Use C-Curves and S-Curves in contrast when Posing your characters:

The line of action is a key ingredient to making your character‚Äôs poses look more dynamic.

This helps your poses "read", it makes them clear and understandable and gives them a distinct non-ambiguous direction.

Drawings by Sherm Cohen ‚òùÔ∏è

  • üëâ Tim Mcburnie on how to rough out poses
  • ‚Äã‚Äãüëâ Don Corgi‚Äôs advice on how to express body language in your poses
  • üëâ How to use reference images when posing

Body language is a crucial element in character design, used to convey a character's personality, emotions, and intentions through posture, gestures, and movement. It's a visual shorthand that helps audiences understand a character's inner state and relationship with others, even without dialogue. It's a visual shorthand that helps audiences understand a character's inner state and relationship with others, even without dialogue.

  • Conveying Personality:
    • Body language can be used to visually represent a character's traits. For example, a confident character might have an upright posture, open body language (like hands on hips with relaxed shoulders), and a wide stance. A nervous character might slouch, cross their arms, shoulders up, and have a downcast gaze.
  • Expressing Emotions:
    • Different postures and gestures can indicate various emotions, like joy, fear, confidence, or doubt. A character lowering or raising their hands might express fear, while a character with a straight back and head held high might convey confidence.
  • Showing Intentions:
    • Body language can also reveal a character's intentions. For example, a character leaning forward might indicate they are trying to engage in conversation, while a character looking away might be trying to avoid something.
  • Building Relationships:
    • Eye contact and how characters physically relate to each other (e.g., touching, embracing, or keeping a distance) can also convey the nature of their relationship.
  • Considering Context:
    • The context of the scene and the overall style of the project (e.g., realistic drama, cartoon comedy) will also influence the type of body language used. A realistic drama might require subtle and nuanced body language, while a cartoon comedy might be more exaggerated.
  • Individual Voice:
    • Each character should have their own unique body language, reflecting their personality and habits. Universal gestures (like smiling, nodding, or shaking hands) can be supplemented with individual quirks, like shaking a leg when nervous or avoiding eye contact.

Samples of Pose Sheets:

Drawing your characters in various perspectives and angles can be the most difficult thing to do, especially when the design of your character is very flat and stylized, they may not be very ‚Äúthree-dimensionally-friendly‚Äù.

As you can see, Pose Sheets have expressions illustrated in them as well. Full body poses + facial expressions will serve as double the information for those using it as reference.

Pushing the pose doesn‚Äôt always mean exaggerating the gesture to the max - it means that each exaggeration is further supporting the ‚Äòbig idea‚Äô of the pose with readable emotions:

Drawings by Christian McCord ‚òùÔ∏è

What does your pose say about your character?

The pose you choose for your character can communicate a lot about their personality, emotions, and desires. For example, using a concave (c-shaped,) line of action for your character‚Äôs pose can help them seem timid or sad, whereas a convex curve can be used to make them seem more confident or happy.

So, before you choose your pose, think about what you want to say about your character and how you can best illustrate this information through curves, tilts, and expressions!

Drawings by Tony Bancroft ‚òùÔ∏è

There are so many different ways you can alter your character‚Äôs pose to make it more effective.

Once you have got your pose roughed out, you can start thinking about how you will pose your character. An easy way to check whether or not your pose is working is to create a silhouette of the pose and see if it still reads well. Good poses are easily understandable even when silhouetted!

Block in the character. If you can still recognize what their body language is doing, then it has a strong, readable silhouette.

By making strong posing choices for your characters you will greatly improve the readability of your character designs!

Recognizability in silhouettes can be great for your audience to instantly identify the character on screen.

Especially when making a cast of various characters, some thought should be put into the character‚Äôs outer shape. Ideally some contrast between a pair or trio of characters makes for a more appealing ensemble.

Stages of Drawing Poses:

Thumbnail - Rough - Clean-up - Color

The thumbnail/rough stage of posing allows you to see aspects of the character you couldn‚Äôt see before. Look back to what you found out about the character before you started designing, when researching different aspects of the character, you‚Äôre being purely creative and exploring, you do a Turnaround but once you start POSING your character, you see areas in the design that might need to be revisited and tweaked. It‚Äôs like taking your model out for a test drive.

Then you enter the ‚Äúclean-up‚Äù phase where you refine the posing, correct any issues with proportions, volumes and find the final line you want to have.

When posing, start off loose and rough to get a feel of how the character‚Äôs acting and attitudes could come through.

Sometimes pose sheets and expression sheets can be combined, their purpose is always to show animators and builders/riggers what the range of anatomy and flexibility is intended for the character‚Äôs style, and how to illustrate various attitudes and postures.

See Victor Navone‚Äôs collection of thumbnail sketches here. He developed these to work out the posing and acting for the characters in his shots prior to animating his scenes. Created as a way to rehearse the poses for the best performance and actions, finding the attitude and mindset of the characters. He‚Äôd use these sketches as a way to explore how to depict their mood and body language.

Variations & Re-dressing:

Often, creating variations in costuming and hair styling can be common practice before full turnarounds are drawn. This way the director can see what looks better for the purposes of the story, location, and the sequence the characters are in.

Other variations are often designed for extras and secondary or incidental characters, they serve as background filler and often share similar interchangeable design traits to keep things efficient and simple:

By Marta Andreeva

Incidental characters are usually background crowds or random extras needing to populate the scene, reacting to the events transpiring in the shots. Sometimes you‚Äôre able to re-use body types, interchange clothing, hair styles and skin tones in order to be more efficient with your time and to easily add variety amongst a crowd of characters.

Variations in age are more rare, but still a possibility depending on the story being told with the character. For example, if there will be flashbacks (or flash-forwards) or visual ways to chronologically depict the character at various stages in their life, then variants in the designs to show younger and older versions will be needed. In which case, displaying the states of their changing physical appearance needs to be mapped out.

Sometimes when developing the character, variations are requested to make them a bit older or younger, adjusting proportions and hairstyles/clothing can go a long way in showing these iterations.

Pose sheets can be made up from the drawings created out of Character Layouts and Special Poses too. These are certain moments captured from the storyboards that need to be redrawn in more details and placed on-style, here‚Äôs samples of this from Marc Bachand:

Pose sheets usually include separate Hand Charts too:

Color Styling

A color stylist (also known as color designers) chooses the colors for characters, props, and FX in an animated production.

Using their knowledge of color theory, their skills as a painter, and their design sensibilities, they create palettes and lighting that allows the characters to fit within the backgrounds and match the overall style of the show.

As always, it‚Äôs important to remember no two productions are alike, and the roles of a color stylist can vary greatly from show to show.

When a color stylist first begins on a show they will receive information from the Art Director about the style of the show and the various painting approaches that are employed when coloring the characters. This is known as a style guide.

Whether an actual document or just a conversation with the Art Director, the style guide serves to give parameters to the color stylists to help the character treatments look consistent.

For example, a style guide might say such things as‚Ä¶

‚ÄúThe shadows of the characters are always hard edged and so when you are blocking out shadows, use the lasso tool.‚Äù

üëâ The Color Wheel and How it Works!

Terms to know:

  • Hue is color (blue, green, red, etc.)
  • Chroma is the purity of a color (a high chroma has no added black, white or gray)
  • Saturation refers to how strong or weak a color is (high saturation being strong/vibrant)
  • Value refers to how light or dark a color is (light having a high value)
  • Tones are created by adding gray to a color, making it duller than the original
  • Shades are created by adding black to a color, making it darker than the original
  • Tints are created by adding white to a color, making it lighter than the original

Handout

The color stylist‚Äôs job begins once the clean black and white character designs have been approved and turned.

Similar to the other design positions, the color stylist receives their new assignments from the Art Director along with any caveats or specific instructions the Art Director would like for the color scheme.

The Art Director will also give them any context for the character/scene that may affect color.

Once the Art Director has delegated the designs, it‚Äôs off to the races!

Paint With All The Colors

While the Art Director may have some specific thoughts about certain aspects of the character, the color stylist for the most part makes all of the decisions about the color schemes of the character from hair color to skin color to outfit color/coordination.

Occasionally there may be characters that directly references or parody a real person and in these specific cases, the palette might be pulled from the reference in order to help it as a reference or something already in existence.

üëâ See here for the fundamentals of color theory and terminology.

Sometimes ‚Äúcolor roughs‚Äù are needed, where the artwork is still rough, but a color pass is needed:

üëâ Color Theory for Artists

Matching The Backgrounds

Another very important consideration the color designer has to make is whether the character‚Äôs color scheme/treatment will make sense against the background.

Often, color stylists will bring in the finished backgrounds where available to make sure that the color scheme of the world and the color scheme of the character are logical and aesthetically pleasing.

Your character colors can clash with the background, it‚Äôs always good to make a quick test to see:

Color Design Review

Once the color stylist has done a few color iterations (usually 3 to 5) of the character in 3/4 front view, it is time for the Art Director and Showrunner to review.

The Showrunner will pick their preferred color scheme from the line‚Ä¶ or possibly frankenstein pieces from different versions.

For example: The Showrunner may say, ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs use the hair from #1, but the skin tone and outfit from #4‚Äù

The color design usually goes through two or three reviews before it is approved.

Once the 3/4 front view colored version of the character is approved, the color stylist will then go in and paint the rest of the black and white turn around.

It‚Äôs a full time job because you have to do that for every single character, prop, or FX in every single lighting situation that happens in a show. The character designer(s) on a production can sometimes do the colors for their own characters. The smaller the production, the more tasks each artist is made to do. On small productions, a designer may have to do concepts, roughs, turnarounds, clean-up and colors for all the design assets they create. While on larger productions, those tasks could be divided up amongst 4 or 5 different people since there is such a large amount of characters to do each week, designing characters, props and color styling ALL of them.

Color Design might sound like a relatively simple job, but it‚Äôs not. You have to take into account a lot of things: how the colors will read on multiple surfaces and backgrounds (and clashing with multiple colors within one background), when you should have shadows on the model, how the shadow actually affects the model, why you‚Äôre choosing cool colors or warm colors, how colors look after they‚Äôve been compressed to be seen on phones versus broadcast TV/streaming, and even the subconscious meaning of colors culturally/psychologically.

üëâ See here for how color combinations can be applied.

Colors combinations can help to create a very iconic casts of character:

Local Colors and Lighting

Color stylists create and set the character palettes for the characters, props and FX within an episode, taking into account lighting changes that might happen while said elements are on screen. Something that can vary from show to show is whether color stylists are responsible for doing the light adjustments for characters under different lighting conditions.

Local Colors (the base colors of a character under neutral lighting) can be greatly impacted by the lighting of specific shots.

For example: A scene takes place in a nightclub with neon lights could turn a brunette character‚Äôs hair bright pink or have rim lights added on.

Sometimes color stylists do these lighting passes, and sometimes they are done by the Art Director.

If you watch the Infinity Train pilot, Tulip is a different color palette in the puzzle car, a different palette outside, a different palette in the corgi car, a different palette inside the temple, and a different palette in the foggy area. Then she has to have a different palette every time she looks at glowing numbers on her hand in each of those locations. All of that just for one character, not including any props or lighting that affects other characters.

Here‚Äôs a quick rundown from that pilot episode (note, the colors are a little washed out because the screenshots were from youtube, but you can still see the difference):

As you can see, she doesn‚Äôt just have a green hoodie, she has like 9 different green hoodies. When eye-dropping these colors, there are colors in there you don‚Äôt even realize were being used in there. These are all decisions that a color stylist makes.

üëâ Here‚Äôs an excellent portfolio example from an experienced color stylist: Grey Chen

Here‚Äôs a short guide by Jess Marfisi:

For example, a character wouldn‚Äôt be colored the same in broad daylight than in complete darkness, right?

LINK

So Props and FX need to be colored as well, keeping the character and background colors in mind while designing the color palettes, so that they will go well together side-by-side. Every object that a character touches and every puff of smoke or falling leaves that might show up in an episode all need to be designed.

A pool cleaner from the Craig of the Creek episode ‚ÄúLost in the Sewers‚Äù. Note that the color stylists have set the palette for when the object is in shadow. The episode was designed by Maaike Scherff and Angie Wang. Color by Carolyn Ramirez.

Here‚Äôs an example of very specific FX design from the Unikitty episode ‚ÄúAction Forest‚Äù. The eye glint effect has been given several frames of action for animation reference. Designed and colored by Jess Marfisi.

üëâ Here‚Äôs an excellent portfolio example from an experienced color stylist: Cam Chan

This can vary from show to show, not all productions have the color stylish responsible for exclusively character colors, it‚Äôs common for them to also do all the colors for other objects and FX. On some productions, the color stylist is responsible for any color design that is not a background.

On other productions, there may be a whole other designer for props, oftentimes a prop designer both creates the designs and colors for all hand-held objects featured in the series.

Another thing that can improve the effectiveness of your character design sheet is a color guide! To do this, all you need is a simple key of all the color swatches used in the character design, alongside an example illustration of the character in full color. This is really helpful when you are handing off a design for a whole team of artists to use, as it ensures all of the colors remain consistent.

‚ÄúDigital media gives access to every color out there. While this is truly amazing, I think limiting yourself to a few key colors is actually a better strategy. Using a limited color palette helps a sketch feel cohesive. Using too many colors can sometimes create a sense of messiness and a lack of focus. I would say to stick with between one and three primary colors initially. Afterwards, you can dive between the values of each tone when detailing. I truly believe that color is the most important piece of the puzzle when creating eye-catching art. I‚Äôve done a few‚Äùtests‚Äù on social media. When posting a drawing with no color, versus the same drawing with added colors, the latter will always get a stronger reception. So if it vibes with your artistic vision: be bright, be bold. It also doesn‚Äôt hurt to get inspiration from photos or other artists‚Äô color palettes.‚Äù -Erik Ly

Right on Schedule

Similar to both background design and character design, there are usually about 2 weeks allotted in a production schedule for color design for each episode, however the timeframe can vary. These 2-week-cycles include producing the color concepts, having them reviewed, painting the one view of the character again, having that reviewed, revising those colors, then painting the other views, at least 5 views for a primary characters and at least 2 views for a secondary/incidental character, and usually you are working on two episodes at once, with multiple characters, with some partial re-use and re-dressing from previous episodes, and all overlapping at various stages.

Color stylists usually finish around 3 to 4 fully-colored character turns per day. Sometimes a very extensive character may take more iterations of exploration.

Lighting Keys:

Rim Light and Rim Shadow

Sometimes you need to make a custom color palette for night time scenes, which includes creating samples for light sources and how they are painted.

Sometimes it‚Äôs not just a dark tint, but an entirely new color scheme:

Night time colors with shadows:

Workflow Pipeline

Start off with only coloring one view at a time, you want your color palette approved before applying it to all the other turns.

Also, if you were not the one that designed the characters (you are painting someone else‚Äôs design) can you communicate with them about ‚Äúcolor details‚Äù? - This is the discretion of adding in some highlights or shading or the removal of lines once the shape is painted.

Or the adding of textures or patterns, like this:

Because character design can sometimes shift right up until the episode‚Äôs assets go out to the Builds Artists and Scene Setup crew, sometimes color design can also be tweaked after the design was already approved. It‚Äôs not the norm, but it happens!

Most shows have a level of flexibility and fluidity in their pipeline to account for changes that may occur along the way.

No pipeline is perfect and TV animation schedules are notoriously hectic with many many moving parts all working at the same time with various stages all overlapping each other with many levels of changes and adjustments being made on every design asset right up until they get given to the animation crew.

Who Do I Work With? - Part two

Primarily, color stylists work closest with the Art Director, this is the person foremost in charge of the overall look of the series.

The Art Director guides and supports the designers to make sure all the color treatments suit the style of the show.

Color Stylists may communicate with Character Designers to discuss their black and white designs which are the basis the color stylists work off of.

Alternates (ALT)

Sometimes creating versions of color schemes and surface details are referred to as ALTs.

Who Do I Affect? - Part two

Color Stylists most heavily affect the Builds team, they take the finished and approved color designs and convert them into rigged assets, which then goes to the Scene Setup (scene prep) crew to assemble each individual shot for each sequence of the episode, which then goes to the animation crew. When the animators open up their assigned scenes, they see everything has been imported, placed in, sized up and laid out for them to use, and then they can begin animating the characters.

Early color explorations models can be made to determine which combination works best:

COLOR CONCEPTS

Placing the color exploration designs over the background art of the location the characters will be at, is usually a great way to see how the colors will work together. This way you can arrive at the colors most appropriate for the scene:

These can be called ‚Äúcolor keys‚Äù as well, since they give you a snapshot of what a finished scene would look like.

So the background colors are usually more subject to change since we want the characters to stay consistent in their personal color schemes.

Color explorations are often needed to see which tones and palettes work best - for the purposes of character personality.

Also, placing your painted character in their environment (if one is available) is a good way to spot any clashing colors as well, or to adjust for the possibility of the character blending in with the background too much.

üëâ This Simple Color Theory Always Works!

Usually a character stays in their same outfit for the entire episode, and most of the time for entire lengths of multiple seasons! The Simpsons, for example, have been wearing the same clothes for over 40 years.

Character ‚Äúredress‚Äù is when a different versions is required to be designed for your character, this could be a drenched in water version, a dirty/muddy version, or a covered-in-paint version, but most commonly it‚Äôs a hairstyle or costume change:

üëâ Wouter Tulp‚Äôs Advice on Color!

Sometimes less is more. You can limit the color palette for unity and impact. When working with three colors, keep the 60-30-10 rule in mind. Pick one color to make up about 60% of the character, a second color to make up about 30%, and the last color is about 10%.

When working with just two colors, use the 70-30 rule. One color is about 70%, the second is about 30%.

Colors have a similar subconscious effect on our visual interpretation. Creating characters with an appealing and suitable color palette can make an immediate impact in grabbing and holding a viewer‚Äôs attention. There are a few different aspects of color theory to consider when designing your character:

Symbolism

Each color carries its own innate effects on a color scheme. Red is universally alert and energetic, white is universally neutral. Warm colors are associated with action while cool ones are linked with calmness.

If you‚Äôre creating content for an international audience it‚Äôs worthwhile keeping in mind that different cultures have different views on color symbolism. Red, for example, can signify danger in the western world, but happiness in China. Likewise, blue represents freedom in many parts of Europe, but can symbolize villainy in Japan. Your intended audience will determine your use of color symbolism.

Color Relationships

The relationship between the different colors in your character design is just as important, if not more so, than the separate color significance. Some of the most well-known color arrangements include: monochromatic (different light values of the same hue); Complementary (two colors which are directly opposite each other on the color wheel), Analogous (colors which are adjacent on the color wheel), and Triadic (three colors spread around the color wheel in a triangle formation).

Each color scheme and color combo has its own advantages: monochromatic and analogous palettes are often soothing, complementary colors are both contrasting and harmonious, while triadic color schemes are dynamic and rich. Take a look at these color wheels and use these systems to help find a color scheme that‚Äôll be naturally pleasing to the eye.

You can see how the character‚Äôs colors can showcase their personalities. How we perceive color‚Äôs significance and meaning is both psychological and cultural.

üëâ Color Theory in Storytelling!

Complementary Colors

Complementary colors are the exact opposite of each other on the color wheel. This gives a highly contrasting and bold look. If you want your character to stand out, a complementary color scheme is perfect!

As you can see in the below example, the use of complementary colors for a character design really allows for the details to pop in the illustration! This can be a useful tool when you are creating a protagonist who needs to be the focus of your drawing.

@meikearts

Monochromatic Colors

A monochromatic color scheme uses only the tones, tints, and shades from a single hue on the color wheel. This creates a really unique and often emotive look in an illustration. If you are looking to make relatively simple color choices, this could be the best method for you!

This color scheme is great for creating characters with well-defined traits that may be best expressed through a specific color. If you have a very complex character design, monochromatic colors can also help simplify their appearance and make it more visually appealing.

A monochromatic color scheme is also a great choice if you are looking to improve your understanding of values when coloring and shading your work!

@meikearts

Triadic Colors

If you are looking to have a bit more variation in your color scheme, the Triadic method might be just what you need! These color combinations use 3 colors that are equally spaced apart on the color wheel.

When it comes to using triadic colors, it is best to choose one of the three to be the most prominent in your illustrations and use the other 2 for highlighting smaller details.

This color scheme creates a very vibrant look, even when the colors are toned down. It is best used for characters with playful energy, as these color combinations can often feel very youthful. This makes triadic colors perfect for characters aimed at a younger audience!

@meikearts

Warm vs Cool Colors

When it comes to using warm vs cool colors there are a few key things to consider. When added to an illustration, warm colored objects will typically feel closer to the viewer, whereas cooler ones will feel further away. This is because of atmospheric perspective, which is essentially just the effect of particles in the atmosphere making things further away have a bluish tint.

The color temperature of your characters will have a similar effect, for example, warmer-colored characters will stand out and feel confident, whereas cooler-toned characters may appear more closed off and mysterious.

@meikearts

By Noah Ruff

Remember, sometimes there‚Äôs added details that need to be inserted in. Like rotations of hands, the underneath area of hair, arms, robes, capes, cloaks and wings, including underneath the feet and the inside of mouths - these are all factors that need to be designed for certain productions in order to provide consistent design and color reference for animators later on.

Muted colors are the opposite of vivid colors, and they are created by simply adding black, white, or grey to a base color.

Dull or muted colors like these, refers to colors that have a low saturation or chroma.

COLOR BREAKDOWNS

Another aspect a color stylist needs to do is to provide alternate versions of the model (usually close-ups) so that other hard-to-see areas are made more visibly clear. Insides of mouths, insides of sleeves and collars, eyelids, and under shoes/feet, just to name a few examples.

When a whole cast of characters are arranged together, often adjustments need to be made so that the color schemes for each character that are often seen together should compliment each other, and not clash or compete with each other.

In 3DCG animated productions, these color designs are used by UV/Texture artists to re-create the surfaces & color shaders for the character models.

There‚Äôs many types of color schemes you can create, try out a few different kinds for your characters to see what works best, experiment and see which ones match your characters‚Äô personalities too.

üëâ See here for more fundamentals of color theory.

Color Styling / Color Designing is an integral part of the animation production pipeline.

It‚Äôs not unusual for color stylists to move into background paint, and maybe eventually to an Art Director role. Everyone‚Äôs journey is different.

Being observant of the way colors interact in the world around you, and exploring different ways to build out color palettes will sharpen the skills you need to excel as a color stylist.

Advice for the Aspiring Color Designer

If you‚Äôre interested in becoming a color stylist/designer, here are some helpful things to keep in mind for both your personal growth and your portfolio!

  1. Characters and Colors
    • A strong color design portfolio shows you know how to make clever and appealing color choices.
  2. Embrace the BG
    • Character color palettes must always exist within a world, having work in your portfolio that includes a background, and shows you know how to color key a character to their world in an appealing way. If you don‚Äôt have your own BG to use, grab one from anywhere and BLUR it a lot. If you‚Äôre still not game with that, use a dark gray background, it‚Äôs better than stark white.
  3. Light and Variation
    • Another exercise that can both help you hone your skills and showcase them in a portfolio, is doing several color variations of the same design‚Ä¶ as well as doing lighting variations to show a character under varying lights (example: warm light, cold light, neon light).
  4. Masks Abound
    • Less of a thing for a portfolio and more of a skill to practice is utilizing layer masks to cut and color different parts of your character designs and character linework. While each show will have its own style guide, getting familiar with the use of layers and masks can make it easier to transition to designing within a production pipeline. In a TV production, making changes fast and easily is everything so it‚Äôs important to become acquainted with setting files up in a way that allows you to color shift quickly if needed.
  5. Reference and Style
    • An important consideration color stylists make is how to choose color schemes that are appropriate for the character in the sense of age, personality, and fashion. Looking up references of different outfits, palettes and clothing sets will help hone your eye for what sorts of colors pair nicely and what the effect of such colors have. Example: More muted, earth tone colors tend to read as ‚Äúmature‚Äù while brighter colors tend to read more youthful, and black.gray may read as edgy/dark. The color of an outfit can radically change the way a character‚Äôs personality/attitude will be interpreted.

Mouth Charts

For reference for lip sync animation, designs must be made of the various mouth positions that animators will need for reference. There are many different ways of going about it, typically there‚Äôs anywhere from 8 to 14 main key positions (for each view) to make.

Front, 3/4 Front and Side views are the 3 positions to build mouth charts for.

As with everything, the style of the production will dictate how exaggerated or subtle the changes in the mouth shapes will be, and how far to push mouth shapes and expressions. Also, usually there‚Äôs a ‚Äòhappy‚Äô version and a ‚Äòsad/angry‚Äô version made for each speaking character, sometimes a third ‚Äòneutral‚Äô version as well. For main characters, a front, side, and 3/4 view is made for each set of mouths as well.

Even with simple/cartoony mouths, keep the character‚Äôs anatomy (and graphic style) in mind, think about what range of squash and stretch you may need, what are the limitations, many characters need to talk without teeth as well, many factors need to be considered:

These mouth shape guides are usually the last step in the creation of a full character model pack, as you can see, different productions have different codes and slightly different variations on the look for each syllable.

Sample mouth charts

Size Comparison Charts

Line up your cast of characters side by side to create a reference chart for every character‚Äôs scale relative to one and other. This is usually made later on the the design process, once the main/primary characters of the series or film are made. All other characters and props created afterwards, use the one main character as the ruler to visually see the scale for all other designs in comparison. Storyboard artists and Animators will use these charts as reference throughout the production.

See more line-ups here.

Distance Models

A less common aspect of character design for animation is creating ‚Äòdistance models‚Äô. As the name suggests, these are characters that have been re-designed to have less details so that they are more easily read-able as they are further away in the distance.

Usually only seen in 2D animated series. Simply shrinking down the usual design causes the lines to get mashed together, so new custom-made models for far shots are created, to make a better and more readable design.

Style Guides

Design Guides, Posing Guides, Drawing Guides, Breakdown Sheets, Construction Models, Style Guides - there‚Äôs lots of names for them, depending on which department creates them, what group they‚Äôre for, which studio produces them, and what sort of production they‚Äôre for. These are becoming more numerous and more frequent in this industry to be made and used, and usually assembled once characters are all completed and finalized. The more time there is in the schedule for them, the more ‚ÄòDOs & DONTs‚Äô and ‚ÄòHow to Draw‚Äô guides are drafted from the character designs, art directors and sometimes the showrunners/series directors, to help other artists & animators understand their construction and mechanics.

Hand Guides from Green Eggs and Ham:

Hand models are a unique aspect of character design in 2D animation. They are the one item that is difficult to build a library of assets for, often lots of examples of hand poses are made, but unlike all other body parts hands are usually drawn out by the animator. Pre-made hands can be made available in the build for the character, but animators will need to also custom-make new hands all the time for all the actions, gestures and acting performed by the characters. Therefore as much reference as possible should be made for them to use as a guide for how to draw and pose hands.

Storytelling in Character Design

An informative essay by I ndia Swift and Michael Doig:

Designing characters can be so much more than just creating a 'cool' or 'appealing' image. True innovation comes from digging deep into your character's personality and their role in your story!

It's not too much of an overestimation to say that character design is one of the most sought after positions for freelancers and studio artists. Designing characters all day is an amazing, fun and inventive process that allows for a lot of rough exploration and idea generation, so it's no wonder that so many people want to pursue it as a career. To become truly excellent, I believe it's important to weave the character's central concept/conflict into their visual identity!

I'm not a professional character designer, but I wanted to talk a little about the way I go about designing my characters and why I do it that way! Hopefully it will help people avoid some of the common pitfalls. Please feel free to share your tips and advice in the comments below, too. I would love to hear your thoughts and techniques!

Way Past Cool

The most obvious and common pitfall is designing a character just to look 'cool'. A character should be designed around a function or personality! Sonic is designed to look cool, sure - but he is also designed to be the fastest thing allliiiiiive! Hence the 'blown back' spikes (from running so fast, obviously) reminiscent of comic speed lines, his red racing car sneakers, and the endless triangles in his designs!

You should have known I would use Sonic as an example. I love Sonic. More about shape design later!

His cool attitude is exemplified not only through his design, but through his pose. Getting inventive with the way the character stands or sits, using that to squeeze the most personality into your design you can is key! No more t-posing or facing the camera with hands by your sides.

Have fun with it. How would your character sit, stand, wait, run? All of this is part of the design of the character, not just the job of the animator. When you think about iconic characters like Bugs Bunny, Superman, or Jafar, they all have poses that they call to your mind. An important thing to note too is that they are all confident characters. However, the way that this comes through in their posing is very unique to each - they all hold themselves completely differently.

Keep it simple

So, a side effect of having no true idea of purpose or function to your character, and of designing them simply to look appealing is festooning your character in stuff that clutters up their design. Needs more belts!

Try to give any accessories your character has a reason for being there. It could be environmental - maybe if they live in a harsh world, they need to wear/equip some kind of day to day protection. Maybe it's something that was given to them and has sentimental value. Maybe it's a relic of their past. Maybe it demonstrates their personality. Whatever it is, use it as a way to deepen the world or the character. If it doesn't have a function, your character design might be better off without it.

Using Midge from my comic The Girl and the Glim as an example, I chose to give her two accessories. One is a friendship bracelet, and one is her brother's old hoodie. Midge is a character whose core conflict revolves around the fear that people won't like her. The bracelet represents how valuable friendship is to her as well as being an indication of her past, a reminder that moving house means she has left her friends behind. The hoodie is a familiar object that Midge uses to hide herself from others. It helps me a lot when demonstrating her shyness and gives me lots of nice posing options that are unique to her as a character.

"Perfection is achieved, not when there is nothing more to add, but when there is nothing left to take away." - Antoine de Saint-Exup√©ry

Style and Influences

The style you choose to design your characters in will vary depending on the story you are designing them for. Your target audience will also play a big role in these decisions! Setting Stranger Things in the 80's had no functionality story-wise. The same story could easily have been told with a modern day setting. However, the creators/designers knew that the story was targeting the generation who grew up on The Goonies and E.T., and wanted to evoke that nostalgia in their audience, and so they chose to set the story within this time period to create the kind of mood and atmosphere they felt would connect with their audience.

Each of the styles here communicate something different about the story's intent.

The Ghibli style character top left is unlikely to be involved in the same kind of story as the screaming Arin Hanson caricature below her. Likewise the far right Unicorn's world will be very different to the bird girl's below.

Choosing a style that communicates is important, as establishing audience expectation and then not living up to that promise can cause a knee-jerk reaction from your viewers (though you can also deliberately use it to create twists!). Just as important is being consistent with this style.

A pet peeve I have is when a show or comic will have a lineup of characters, 90% of which are consistent with one another, but then have one design that will completely throw off the aesthetic of the show.

A Silent Voice (2016)

I don't have a problem with designs being broad and having more variety in lineups is something I am strongly in favor of. However, if you're going to make zanier design choices, you need to sprinkle that throughout your world, rather than having just one character following a different ruleset.

In the above screenshot, Tomohiro the larger character has a head that is disproportionately large and cartoonish compared to the rest of the cast. I think it's great that they included a character with a larger body type, but it feels like he is from a different movie. This dissonance kept pulling me out of this fantastic movie, which is a shame. I think they could have pulled his facial design more in line with the rest of the cast to avoid this problem. Also - go and see A Silent Voice if you get the chance, it's a wonderful film!

There are times when you might want to break this ruleset deliberately - for example, if you are deliberately making the statement that this character is not of this world, and doesn't belong. However in general you should try to keep your style consistent in order to keep your world feeling believable.

When experimenting with the various possible design elements that make up your character, think about what associated imagery you can use:

  • Advice 2
  • Advice 3

Function and Purpose

Design for Animation Needs:

A further consideration when designing characters is how they will function with the chosen animation style. A character animated with the puppet tool in After Effects will have very different animation needs to one designed for traditional frame-by-frame animation in TV Paint or Harmony.

Think about which character animation style will work for your chosen aesthetic and carefully research what type of world your story is set in. A cartoony, squash-and-stretch animation style may work great for a quirky cast of characters.

While a more rigid or realistic structure & anatomy will work better for stories holding a bit more gravity, since they‚Äôre grounded in a less-cartoony, more realistic world.

üëâ Conceptualizing Intent: Jack Burke‚Äôs Process

The word ‚Äòshape‚Äô appears more than 90 times in this document, you will see that it‚Äôs the most basic element to have and use in all

character design creation.

Designs can range from the very simple‚Ä¶

To the very complex‚Ä¶

There‚Äôs a wide spectrum between simple and complex, characters can vary from realism to caricature and excessive to minimalism - inhabiting many degrees of diversification and stylization.

The character designs you create will influence how the animation style of movement will develop.

This includes the characters‚Äô flexibility, mobility, and type of hair & clothing.

There can be a predetermined set of rules and parameters in the style of animation that may influence how you design the characters as well. For example: you may be on a production that won‚Äôt be able to accommodate for drapery and long flowing capes, cloaks and hair, for either a 2D or 3D production, because these technical limitations could be beyond the scope of the software (or budget), and this would restrict you to creating more tight-fitting clothes and maybe LEGO-style stiff/blocky hair.

Most of the time designers need to research and ask lots of questions and perhaps even see the shooting-style of the storyboards in order to ascertain what limitations there may be in the characters for their restrictions in movements, clothing, hairstyles and range of expressive attitudes. Sometimes there‚Äôs technical limitations with what the characters can do; Perhaps they must have form-fitting clothes and short hair because it would become too complex to animate long-flowing hair and loose-baggy clothing.

üëâ The dangers of over-designing your characters are well explained here.

Simplifying vs Complexity

Iconography in Character Design

Digging a bit deeper on the use of images and symbols to represent ideas and how there‚Äôs a range within the spectrum of all art and illustration when it pertains to realism, stylization, and visual symbolism. Adding symbols, icons, or signs to your character design can add another layer of depth and mystique. This could be giving your character an object that is strongly associated with them, adding symbolism in the character's clothing, or including a unique scar or tattoo.

From the Arcane series, see here how they implemented basic symbolism into Jinx and Vi's character design:

These symbols are used as metaphors - imagery that represents their character, visual metaphor is a common tool in film to help convey a story's deeper meaning. They can help establish themes, character arcs, and communicate important ideas. They can also help audiences relate to the story and grasp complex ideas more intuitively.

See this excerpt from Scott McCloud‚Äôs wonderful book: Understanding Comics

Visual Motifs can be depicted in your character design‚Äôs outfits, or the props they hold, or within the environment they live in. All this can play a part in informing the audience as to their personality and history. Visual development artist Sam Nielson created a reference sheet for these types of representation and their meanings:

Downloadable large version here.

Design for Technical Needs

If the production is using cut-out/puppet-style animation, you‚Äôll need to design each part of your character with a mindset in how it will connect and rotate with other body parts. 2D rigging, with bone tools and deformers, requires lots of forethought on the interactivity of the body parts and often a trial-and-error process to determine how each body part is connected and what pivot shapes will work best for creating smooth or believable movement, while keeping the rig functional and easy-to-work with for the animators that pose and operate the characters.

Knowing the Limitations

Being aware of the production‚Äôs parameters and any specific restrictions involved is important knowledge to have and use in your quest to determine the style. Oftentimes, because the design process can start very early in the production pipeline, there are no clearly defined lists of conditions to be conscious of. At some point, your thought process should be mindful of any constraints in the artistic and technical aspects for the characters‚Äô anatomy and physical capabilities. Budgetary concerns and even what software/technology is being used can also be factors to consider.

For example - the series Pickle & Peanut of the characters in Toonboom Harmony could be made practical and functional for animators. The stories and circumstances the characters would find themselves in required them to manipulate, bend, deform, and contort their bodies to meet the needs of the animation style.

A hybrid animation approach was taken for some aspects of the animation (a combination of traditional animation with rigged animation), since their faces and limbs were so flexible and unique in their poses and expressiveness from scene to scene. Therefore a unique production pipeline had to be made: Where the character rigs were customized from scene to scene to meet the demands of the exaggerated and dynamic posing the characters had to do. This way, the models the artists would animate could perform the actions depicted in the storyboards. Very rough storyboards were made and delivered to a team of character layout artists, who would use those storyboards as reference and hand-draw/clean-up and add details to the extensive posing/acting of every shot, placing the characters on-model on as they go. Then another team would take these drawn out character layouts and make customized rigs using the character builds and breaking them, to make the poses match the artwork scene by scene. Then a team of animators would animate the movements from pose to pose using these custom rigs.

If you‚Äôre designing with no finished scripts in hand, you may only have some rough ideas as to what sorts of stories these characters will find themselves in, this is also common. Getting just a hint of what the characters will need to perform in their scenes by analyzing scripts from the episodic stories will help in the refinement of your designs. Knowing if it‚Äôs a fast-paced action/adventure series is very different from knowing it‚Äôs a slow-paced lots-of-talking style series. On feature films the story is constantly evolving through to the very end of production. You simply adapt with the changes from the very beginning as things are developing right until the end of the main production phase as you assist and change designs according to the needs of the production.

The term function over form comes from the architectural principle ‚Äúform follows function,‚Äù which means that the shape of the building should primarily relate to its intended function. Make-up Design, Set Design and Costume Design all have the same principle. In interior design, function over form means prioritizing how you will use the room or piece of furniture over how it looks. In character design, the same term can be identified when trying to reach a balance between the artistic needs and requirements of your design, versus its practical use for the purpose of the story being told. ‚ÄúForm‚Äù is the look of your design, how good, and how cool, and how appealing it looks. ‚ÄúFunction‚Äù is how practical and usable it is for animation production.

When developing the look and structure of your characters, you must ask yourself: why are you creating this character, what environment are they from or live in, what are the stories and scenarios they will appear in, and who are you creating these characters for; the target audience is something to consider when designing your characters.

In fine dining, every second counts. In animation, every frame counts. In character design, every line counts.

  • Every line must have a purpose.

Designing how characters will move can often be overlooked. The manner in which they move and the mechanics of how they move, how much stretch-ability and flexibility they may have, are all factors to consider. Especially once the demands of the production kick in. Getting to explore and design how movements communicate and enhance character in any Pre Production Test Animation stages can often spotlight some changes in your designs that need to be made. Never forget: your work will be taken by many other artists and they will animate your designs!

A famous story in animation:

Bill Melendez, the director of the 1965 TV Special ‚ÄúA Charlie Brown Christmas‚Äù, had to explain to broadcast executives that the characters in a Peanuts cartoon simply could not hold items ‚Äòabove their heads‚Äô - No matter how much the producers insisted upon it, this wasn‚Äôt physically possible since their arms were so short and their heads were so big. So cheats and creative solutions always had to be made when staging the action.

Modern animated series like Green Eggs & Ham, Animaniacs, and Central Park are just some of many that had traditional model sheets drafted for animators to use as reference, there were NO rigs or Builds created at all for the characters even though they were entirely digitally-produced. Classical frame-by-frame animation methods were used to create every scene in every episode, and it was animated using Harmony software. They were made to look as if drawn traditionally on paper. Tablets and Cintiqs were used to essentially animate in classical methods with entirely digital paperless-animation tools, with very specific custom brushes built for the clean-up animation process for the look of the final line.

The more traditionally-animated the characters are, the more beneficial it is to keep the characters simpler in design, this reduces the amount of work for animators must do to make them move. The less lines and textures to animate, the easier it will be to have the characters perform complex actions and acting.

Animation By Tim Larade

Animation by Mihka M√§ntynen

As a character designer you may be asked to create the full cast of main characters for a story. You will eventually need to assemble your designs into a model pack. At first you may only be assisting a lead designer, but if you are in charge of multiple characters and to make an ensemble cast out of them, a model pack is inevitable:

The Model Pack refers to the character design sheets for a cast of characters from an animated film or series. A Model Pack attributes to just the characters. While a Main Design Pack (MDP) usually refers to all main characters and key locations/props, that sets the style and tone for the entire series or project.

A full model pack would have the main characters with full turnaround views and poses/expressions sheets + select incidental characters with some various views drawn out for them as well, all contained in a workbook / reference guide as shown here. Some studios also refer to the term ‚ÄúFun Packs‚Äù - these are usually reserved to being a bundle of bare-essential designs, like main characters and key locations/environments, for the Storyboard Artist to use as reference.

In an animated series, Storyboard Artists must often create all sorts of new incidental/secondary and alternative designs that go beyond the initial design pack they were given to use as reference. Therefore, after a storyboard/animatic is done and approved, a post-board design pass is done; A new comprehensive list is made, artists then complete all remaining on-screen design elements that will need to be animated later on, this builds up and finalizes a more complete design pack. Layout Artists and Build Artists/ Riggers use these new designs to complete all remaining assets needed for the animators to have in their scenes to animate with.

A consolidated design pack is made after the storyboards are done, and before Rigging, Background Layout/Paint and Scene Planning has started.

In the beginning, the designs you develop and sketch and create are to find out what the characters would look like, and eventually they get refined and locked in, and decisions are made as to which design is best suited for the character.

Afterwards, these designs are made to be used as references for all the artists that come after you, to use in production. So keep in mind, the more visual information you can offer, the more helpful it will be to the crew that will bring your characters to life.

In some cases, animation tests are made, and the outcome of those tests causes there to be revisions in the designs. Unforeseen limitations or obstacles are discovered in the testing process, leading to adjustments made to the designs and builds in order to make them more functional for animation purposes.

If the story requires a character to perform an action that is physically impossible for them to do, then changes to their design may be needed in order for the performance to work, or ‚Äòcheats‚Äô are made in the poses and builds to accommodate the action to happen.

Thoughts on Appeal

Spend enough time talking to animators and you‚Äôll start to hear the word ‚Äòappeal‚Äô come up. In terms of animated characters, appeal is what makes an audience see the character as charismatic, relatable or realistic. It‚Äôs what makes us feel for them, and remember them, even after the animation is over.

Advice from Dean Heezen:

It‚Äôs imperative to keep in mind the purpose of these characters and their stories, what is the intended audience of the animated work you are designing for, and how your character design will fit within the context of the production.
