The art direction, your client‚Äôs needs, and the requirements of the production will dictate how simplified your character style needs to be. You may want a more artistic look to help your characters stand out from the crowd - this may call for more of a ‚Äòbroad‚Äô character design, or a stylized, abstract, or angular look, maybe a combination of realism and strong graphic qualities.

Tilt, Rhythm, & Twist - Here‚Äôs tips to use when posing. Former Disney animator Glen Keane coined tilt, rhythm, and twist; if you have a background in studio art or art history, you‚Äôll be familiar with the term Contrapposto (‚ÄúCounter Pose‚Äù) Tilt and Twist are easy enough to explain: you can get tilt/twist in the head, shoulders, and hips, this will help the pose feel less rigid‚Äìyou‚Äôre essentially applying overlapping action to the pose, causing counter-weights or contrast in the postures.

Rhythm is a slightly more complex concept. Rhythm is about the flow of your lines and how they lead the eye around the drawing. It‚Äôs hard to describe in words, hopefully this graphic helps:

Simple vs.¬†Complex - Rhythm is also tied into the concept of simple vs.¬†complex. You see simple vs.¬†complex in the elements that make up a character, but also in the pose as a whole, and the textures within the character design and scene composition. These contrasts within the pose itself helps to create appeal.

Part of achieving Appeal in character design is to apply these theories when you can, the root of this comes from the Solid Drawing Principle in 2D animation.

  • üëâ Watch Mollsie Art talk about the subject of appeal here.
  • üëâ Watch J. Marrons‚Äôs tutorials in structural drawing and anatomy here.

Avoiding Tangents in designs and posing is another factor that helps with appeal:

Simply overlap or separate shapes and lines, preventing them from merging at the edges.

You can fix tangents by shifting the line or pushing the line back.

Creating appeal in your designs can be the most challenging aspect of the work. There‚Äôs thousands of variables that all play off of each other. Only with time, countless hours of study & keen observations, with lots and lots of practice, can you build up your instincts to know how to recognize appealing designs and how to create them.

Anatomy & Structure

The following section is about analyzing the inner-workings and construction of various cartoon character styles, how they are rooted in realistic forms and volumes found in figure-drawing, all from referencing real people and animals. Studying, analyzing, and practicing anatomy can be one of the most challenging and important aspects to learn on your journey to becoming a character designer.

  • See here for a guide on the very core basics that you need to master first by Marc Leone.
  • See here for a guide on understanding cross contours for thinking more like a 3D modeler.
  • See here for a guide on how to study anatomy.
  • See here for a guide on developing the structure of character anatomy by Rad Sechrist.

See here for a guide on the advantages of using more structural drawing, from Tracy Butler. Her work is great to sift through, with plenty of tutorials.

How to Improve Your Character Form and Structure

One thing to practice as much as possible - Take screenshots from shows, videos, movies you watch and make draw-overs, analyze the structure, figure out what makes it work, break things down, create these studies for yourself to learn how they did it:

A key part of creating strong character designs is having a strong understanding of form and anatomy. Here are some exercises and tutorials on how to improve these skills:

  1. Do many many many anatomy drawing studies.
  2. Take a life drawing class in person or online.
  3. Practice drawing areas of the body that you find the most difficult - hands, feet, head angles, etc.
  4. Visit a zoo or natural history museum and sketch the animals - keep it loose, fast, rough.
  5. Go to a park and draw different plants and quick-sketches of people you see.
  6. Do lots and lots of gesture drawings, practice the human form.
  7. Practice your proportions.
  8. Follow the methods from experts.

Rough turnaround and construction guide by Jesse Bechtold:

Everything has anatomy and structure, even these designs:

Design sheets are often made showing breakdowns and constructions to figure out the mechanics and movements of your character and their range of motion for animators to have as reference, but these are also made to maintain some rules for the solidity and structure of your characters and useful strategies on how to pose them.

To maintain the volume consistency and structure of your character when posing them for their turnaround views and pose sheets, you must analyze and practice the fundamentals of human (and animal) anatomy. This will give you knowledge on how to simplify and stylize them to accommodate the specific anatomy for the characters you‚Äôre creating. By doing so, you‚Äôll learn how to customize the anatomy for your own characters, to make it fit in the style that is intended.

These breakdowns are rooted in all forms of structural anatomy and these theories can be adapted to most design styles, but every character has their own unique construction:

Samples of breakdowns for the construction of a character design:

Figure Drawing

Drawing quick gestures is a great method of practice as it is a significant exercise that can help you fully grasp how to draw a body.

The practice of gesture drawing involves making a quick and simplified sketch of your subject matter (typically a human body).

When gesture drawing, you are trying to plot down the main elements of the figure in quick and simple linework to capture the general feel of the body and the pose you are trying to communicate.

This is very helpful in allowing you to get a feel for the flow of the body and how to visually communicate body language, gestures, and poses, in a very simplified manner.

To do gesture drawing, all you really need is a pen and paper, or maybe a drawing tablet or an Apple Pencil and iPad, and some sort of reference.

This can simply be an online reference image, or if you wanted to try gesture drawing the traditional way, you could use a live model ‚Äî maybe even try to find a class to attend.

Then, all that‚Äôs left to do is start sketching the body as simply and quickly as you can, ignore specific details, and instead try to draw the flow and linework of the body.

More about the benefits and techniques of Gesture Drawing here.

Remember to give yourself a time limit, ranging from 30 minutes to under 30 seconds.

As well as this, don‚Äôt use an eraser! Just let your pencil flow as you try to capture the essence of the body.

This time limit and lack of eraser will force you to really try and quickly plot down the main elements of the human form, making you more easily grasp any important details and therefore providing you with a better understanding of how to draw a body.

A lifetime can be spent learning, analyzing, practicing sketching the human body.

Watch these tips and techniques from Robert Marzullo.

Human Anatomy

Every character you create will have their own unique style of anatomy, the key is to stay consistent within your own style, you will have different body types and body shapes for different characters, but as you create a cast of characters in the world/style you are creating. The more you‚Äôll see the range of proportions you will need to do, the more you‚Äôll refine the anatomy to match that character‚Äôs physique and personality.

The body is difficult to capture through art as there are many components to consider. You must remember to engage the fundamentals of art, specifically considering form, structure, and composition ‚Äì as well as considering proportions and figure anatomy, body movements, and stylization. This sort of approach to how your characters are constructed from the inside out, takes time to develop, like any illustration skills, there‚Äôs several ways to mold and shape your characters, and every style has their own brand of anatomy.

There‚Äôs a wide range of anatomy styles to pick from and to create yourself.

Hands, in general, can be the most diverse and complex things to pose and the most difficult to master.

There‚Äôs many lessons online to help you improve.

When you‚Äôre doing more human anatomy based in realism, there are different factors to consider, compared to when you do more flat/graphic/cartoony styles.

Before you can begin adding personality and stylization to the bodies you draw, it is integral to understand the proportions of human anatomy. Getting the body proportionally correct can be difficult, but there are a few shape-work techniques you can use to sketch the human body as shown in the samples above and below. Practice drawing your forms in poses that demand a challenging perspective.

Drawing a body is a difficult task, especially because there are so many different components to consider. Trying to create an anatomically correct figure is not always easy. The body is integral to many aspects of design; you need it for any character you might want to create or even in environmental artworks with people in the background.

So studying and practicing how to draw a body is important. You want to feel comfortable creating stylized figures in whatever poses and designs you would like.

Animals/Creatures

How to breakdown and learn from the references you save & compile‚Ä¶

Here‚Äôs a post from the amazing, HelpfulHarry:

So let's make Bad Art of a Deer because I happen to have one handy.

Start with a photo of your subject in a nice/neutral pose with all four feet visible (so not like me).

Freehand copy it. Try not to stylize, focusing instead of matching proportions and pose. Don't get too detailed!

Either lasso + distort or redraw a copy of your first sketch with your reference behind it (scaled to match the main body of your sketch).

Put the original and modified sketches together and compare the differences. Write it down if you want. This shows you where your eyes saw things the wrong size, so you can correct for that next time.

It's ok if your art looks terrible and has broken legs. I've drawn LOTS of deer so I have a leg up. Everyone's art sucks in their own eyes and here's where mine went wrong: Video

After learning about both deer and yourself, try freehand copying again.

Marvel at your newfound knowledge and skill!

But there's always room for improvement‚Ä¶

You can stop here and move on to your real drawing, Or do another freehand-fix-compare cycle. I actually overcorrected my "draws heads too big" and veered into "heads too small."

Another note on tracing: Learning HOW to trace is more important than anything you could learn by tracing. Draw the Anatomy, not the outline. In real life, things don't have outlines, they have bones.

These are from the same shoot which is extra useful for consistency. The lines are minimal and follow where the animal's joints are, and only important parts are drawn.

You won't know what Important Parts means right off the bat, which is where in-depth study comes in. You need to do this over and over, to learn the hard parts, to do the easy parts right.

How to study Bones and Muscles
  • "Study the anatomy‚Äù
  • ‚ÄúStudy the anatomy"
  • But they never tell you HOW.

It's not "read a book," It's more like flailing around wildly and crashing your browser from too many tabs.

This is all about How to make a bones and muscle chart. Because even if your art sucks, you learn so much more by doing than by seeing, finding skeletal and muscle anatomy references online. Get set up. Get photos, like below, but it doesn't have to be the same photos. And now... gather and organize references.

References I gathered:

  • Refrence 01
  • Refrence 02
  • Refrence 03
  • Refrence 04
  • Refrence 05
  • Refrence 06
  • Refrence 07
  • Refrence 08

We'll start with bones. Search up "[animal] skeleton" and get photos or super scientific illustrations. Add in things like "top view" to spice it up.

Next, search "[animal] skeleton sketchfab." This pulls up 3D models that you can rotate in your browser. Remember that these are art and the anatomy is only as good as the artist, so pick a good one.

Time for bone!

The spine is the most important, and in a lot of animals it will surprise you. Draw it in over your photo and then add spikes because skeletons are punk. These are not scientific and I didn't count them because their number doesn't matter to art. So you better be referencing scientists and not me!

The rest of the bones and some notes. These are my notes to myself about things I want to remember. My personal discoveries in anatomy that made my art better. You can make the same notes but also make sure you have your own thoughts on there as well. That's how you help yourself the best. Be as detailed or vague as you want.

Same deal with muscle. Here are my personal notes to myself. Label stuff that is important to you. I actually grouped a bunch of muscles together based on what is visible from the outside. Muscles are way more complicated than this, but Baby's First Anatomy Chart gets to be simple.

This is good enough for me because I have intimate knowledge of the other muscles working under and over these ones. Feel free to add as many or as few muscles as you like. You chart your own course.

This is very VERY much not an anatomical chart. I'm sure there's nerds out there pulling their hair out looking at this. But listen, it works for art!

And you know the wildest part about this?

I don't need to look at it to use it. The act of making your own anatomy chart puts that knowledge in your brain. Like how you can make "cheat sheets" even for tests that don't allow them - the act of making the sheet helps you remember what you struggle with most.

And after all that complexity? Your simplification will be based on Real Knowledge and you'll put those random circles in the right spots.

Look at all this hard work you've done. Eventually this will be second nature to you.

And you can apply this TO ALL HUMAN AND ANIMAL ANATOMY - your skill levels will advance VERY quickly the more you do these types of breakdowns and analysis.

Back to basics

The following exercise will improve your hand dexterity and ability to efficiently draw the forms you need to construct anatomy by sketching circles, ellipses and other shapes while also getting you into the mental state of calmness needed to start work on a real drawing, here‚Äôs a breakdown:

You should practice filling a page or digital canvas everyday with circles, it‚Äôs great for motor skills and to get you hand to sketch these out fast and with confidence.

Avoid this:

It needs to look more like this:

Practicing drawing cylinders is a good way to warm up and is a surprisingly useful way to get perspective sketched out freehand.

Cylinders are quite versatile. You‚Äôll often end up in a situation where it is useful to be able to draw them quickly and accurately. For example, it is helpful to approximate arms and any limbs foreshortening through cylinders when drawing the human form.

You can also deform cylinders into organic shapes.

It‚Äôs just useful to be able to freehand cylinders! And it is a great drawing exercise when you have no reference to work from or no inspiration. Become better at drawing by doodling circles, ellipses, cylinders and cubes. All character designs can be constructed with these forms.

Stylization

This can be a very subjective thing to define. How much ‚Äòstyle‚Äô should you put into your design? What is ‚Äòstyle‚Äô? And where do you start on this broad spectrum?

‚ÄúOften your limitations dictate a lot of your stylistic and creative choices, and I think that‚Äôs OK, as long as you have an interesting concept to work from.‚Äù - Drew Daniels, Cinematographer.

This involves you finding your specific and individual style of drawing, and sticking to it consistently throughout your artwork. In a broader sense, the stylization process and the stylizing of character designs can encompass the countless ways of creating art, and everyone will find their own style through practice. It's all about how to make these drawings and designs feel like your own, and for each new production and concept you dive into, you find the style that best serves the character personalities, and the world and scenarios they will find themselves in.

How much style is too much style? Everyone has their own preference and opinion, and many factors are involved in the defining of style, and how far to push the stylization of something and how much detail to add or subtract. Many decisions are made on how much exaggeration should be injected or removed from the designs.

Here are designs by Ana Piedrafita for the Pocoyo series:

Simplicity and cuteness is obviously the goal for these designs.

Of course, ascetic minimalism itself holds little meaning. Context imbues significance. The process of simplifying designs and the art of reducing the amount of details often expresses more with less. Restrictions hone communication to indispensable signals, the easier and faster a designer is recognized, the more successful your character model will be. At the intersection of restraint and intent, very simple designs with basic shapes and forms uplifts truth and beauty: Keep it Simple

Examples of Bold Exaggerations:

  • Johnny Bravo ‚Äì Crazy hairdo, bulging pecs, and arrogance exaggerated for laughs
  • Adventure Time ‚Äì Friends frolicking through surreal candy-filled worlds
  • Samurai Jack ‚Äì Jagged lightning style pushed into epic presentation
  • Borderlands ‚Äì Dark humor and visual edge match its absurdity

Johnny Bravo turnaround model:

Tiny Titans comic book:

Pioneers of low-poly / pixel art / 8-bit styles:

  • Minecraft ‚Äì LEGO-like blocks empower creative construction
  • Re-Volt ‚Äì Toy RC cars gain tactile charm
  • Voxatron ‚Äì Voxel retro effects evoke gaming‚Äôs dawn
  • Habbo Hotel ‚Äì Minimized avatars enable diverse expression
  • Thomas Was Alone ‚Äì Geometric shapes brimming with personality
  • Super Mario / Megaman / Link from Legend of Zelda ‚Äì Nintendo mascots

Simple geometric models tap a nostalgia vein through their blocky charm. Low poly art employs hard edges and elemental shapes rather than pursuing endless detail. Less complexity focuses attention on possibilities over realities.

Abstract shapes leave space for imagination to fill the gaps. Our minds complete the visions through personalized perceptions. Low poly models guide eyes through implied motions along crisp vector paths. By finding virtue in technological limits and distortion, low poly art spotlights stirring essence below surface elaboration.

All styles contain strengths to amplify their personalities and stories. Photorealism conveys humanity, cartooning enables humor, and low poly maximizes minimalism. Wise character creators mix and match sensibly by considering impacts on emotion, branding, and performance. Most importantly, they pour passion into every line and pixel. That personal touch makes all the difference.

Highly stylized art similarly cranks up reality through visual hyperbole. Every element gets exaggerated just shy of breaking believability, amplifying the stories they‚Äôre into operatic spectacles. Super-exaggerated anatomy heightens the humor. By escaping the shackles of realism, artists can bend the medium to meet the vision. Costumes morph into √ºber-symbolic power fantasies. Weapons swell to outrageous proportions mirroring their warrior‚Äôs outsized strength. Highly distorted bodies become human ideograms. Backdrops warp surreally to externalize psychological subtexts. Realism depicts what is; stylization reveals what could be.

But the most effective exaggeration remains anchored to humanity. Caricatures work best when we perceive the real person peering through the warped/simplified/exaggerated version you see. Unique character art style choices should align with and amplify authentic traits rather than pile on arbitrarily. Like a bungee cord, the most elastic visions snap back to their origins.

Preferences in stylization and appeal can be a subjective topic of discussion, it‚Äôs all about how much to simplify, or not simplify, how representative to real-life people, animals, and objects it needs to be, and how far can the exaggeration be pushed.

  • üëâ Tomm Moore: Style is Defined by my Limitations
  • üëâ How to Create Your Own Aesthetic

A term you might hear a director or client say: ‚ÄúPush the style‚Äù.

It may seem like an arbitrary statement, but it‚Äôs usually referring to revising/improving the design in a way that further exaggerates the aspects and elements that give the artwork its unique appearance. If the eyes are proportionally quite big, make them bigger. If the hands are small, make them smaller. If the character is quite square and blocky, make them more so. If the characters seem very flat and rigid, make them more so. If they are very squashy and gooey, then make them more so. If the characters have long skinny limbs, make them more so.

All these are just samples of various looks you can find and collect - showcasing the incredibly vast possibilities of the graphic styles to be inspired from.

Pushing styles is about finding out how far is too far - if the eyes are small, make them extra small. If they have big hair, make them extra big. If they have long skinny limbs, snake them extra long. If they have tiny hands, make them extra tiny. If they have thick feet, make them extra thick.

Pushing style levels is always fun, as you can see from these examples, shapes and proportions and scale are the main factors that can have anyone experiment with how to stylize your designs more or less, it‚Äôs boundless the range you can explore.

Discovering what the limitations are within the particular designs you are creating is part of the process. Developing the structure, design logic, and anatomy of your character‚Äôs style can be a journey in itself:

Remember, all cartooning is to some degree, essentially caricaturing

Here‚Äôs good advice from KeshArt: How To Draw Cartoon People Here‚Äôs good advice from Hossein Afzali: How to Cartoonize People

There can be a vast spectrum to consider when finding the style you‚Äôre looking for. Most times, people don‚Äôt know what style they want to see, or create, until they‚Äôve seen it. But usually offering a range of realistic to cartoony, covers a spectrum of possibilities to begin narrowing down the style a client (or you) may be looking for.

One method you can use to find your style is to test out different ways of drawing a body until you find something that works for you, adapting it until it becomes your own. For methods and inspiration for drawing stylized anatomy specifically, see this and this.


Research can be gathered from all sorts of different places, here‚Äôs an eye make-up guide a designer to help create the eyeshapes she needed for her characters:

  • Tutorial 2
  • Tutorial 3
  • Tutorial 4

Experiment with linework; try different sketching styles, like using harsh confident linework or smooth-flowing lines.

Because styles are so limitless, there are so many methods you can use to learn how to stylize your body.

Staying consistent within your own style can take a long time to figure out and accomplish, the secret to fostering and developing this with yourself is through lots and lots and lots of drawing, it‚Äôs the only way to discover how YOU can draw your own thing, in your own way, and explore and fine-tune how YOU would handle any type of character. Observe the works of Mike Mignola, part of the appeal of his art style in how consistent he is within his own style.

Remember that the term for ‚Äústyle‚Äù in character design for animation can refer to a few different things:

It can refer to a specific person who originated a certain look, like Matt Greoning (seen in shows like Simpsons, Futurama, and Disenchantment) or Norman Rockwell (seen in American commercial illustrations from the 1940s and 50s).

It can refer to a property, like Scott Pilgrim (created by Bryan Lee O'Malley) or Adventure Time (initially created by Pendleton Ward, then many others refined, expanded and continued the style).

It can refer to a time period, like the 1930s (as emulated in the animated series and video game Cuphead, which was derived from the very early works of Disney/Warner Bros/Fleischer).

It can refer to a studio, like Aardman (as seen in the Wallace and Gromit films), or UPA Studios cartoons from the 1950s, or Studio Ghibli (as seen in many works of Hayao Miyazaki), or both a studio and a certain time period, like Hanna-Barbera (as seen in many 1960s and 1970s animated series).

With many other combinations of these categories as well.

For studios in particular - it‚Äôs referred to as ‚Äòthe house style‚Äô when a specific studio has a very particular and consistent look to all their designs for all their productions. Old Disney, compared to Modern Disney, same as Klasky Csupo, Pixar, Don Bluth, Filmation, Nelvana, Aardman or Ghibli, they all have their own distinct, consistent, and long-running visual styles (especially in their early years) that they are most known for. Other more modern animation companies like Studio Cachette, The Line, Madhouse, Laika, and Screen Novelties all have fairly unique and distinct house styles seen in most of their work. Though the art direction may vary from production to production, they seem to have a signature ‚Äúlook‚Äù to all the shows and films they‚Äôve made.

Study various graphic styles from art history and architecture too, you never know when you‚Äôll need to borrow ideas from Neo Expressionism, Brutalism, Art Deco, or Cubism.

There can be lots to learn from the old comic book ways too, including the criss-crossing spectrum between Realism vs Exaggeration and Low Detail vs High Detail.

  • See here for some insight on these various styles and methods.
  • See here for Scott MacCloud‚Äôs breakdown from the world of sequential art.

There‚Äôs hundreds of different combinations of styles to draw inspiration from.

There‚Äôs over 100 years of character design history to become familiar with. Every few years we see trends change, tastes change, and new talent gets injected into the industry with new and fresh ideas. Often old styles tend to resurface and get re-imagined, and as new designers discover and rediscover old art styles, they often get remixed into the new modern styles we see today.

For example, looking back at the designs from the 80s and 90s, the characters are more realistic and in a way, have many similar looks and traits, comparable to the comic books of that era. But going into the 2000s and 2010s, many shows began to have a wider range of shapes and silhouettes for character design styles, borrowing aspects from the 50s and 60s:

Trends, visual preferences, and styles seem to change and evolve every few years, then old trends circle back around and become popular again.

A stylized character, in general, is a character that has its proportions deformed and exaggerated in some ways to create a magnified character - made out to have a softer/cuter look, or just a super-simplified version of what would be a realistic character as well. Other ways to ‚Äúpush the style‚Äù is to overemphasize certain physical attributes, if the character is blocky and angular - make them more so, if the character is bubbly and cute make them more so. There are many ways to approach how you want your character to look.

Robert Valley

Sylvain Chomet

You can try creating a Chibi version (a term derived from Japan), or a stylized character similar to those seen in contemporary animated movies.

The main distinction between them lies in the level of deformation these characters undergo. Chibis, originating from already stylized characters in manga or anime, exhibit significant deformations.

Currently, these types of characters find use in several applications, primarily in animated series or video games. Their visually appealing aesthetics easily capture the attention of younger audiences, but their charming appearance also attracts adults.

Typical Chibi Style:

However, there are numerous ways to create highly-stylized characters, making them one of the most challenging aspects to achieve, in both 2D and 3D/CGI. They can also be used ironically for a cute or humorous effect.

‚Äã‚Äã

When it comes to proportions, stylized characters share common characteristics - the process can be divided into three parts: head, body, face. Depending on the proportions you choose, you can achieve a more realistic impression similar to Fortnite or a more cartoonish character.

  • Head: the bigger the better. The head, along with the face, is the most crucial aspect of these models. Not only do you need to enlarge the head, but you should also make it rounder to give the character a softer appearance. If there are special features on the head that are characteristic of the character, such as scars or wrinkles, include them, but exaggerate their appearance.
  • Face: the face, together with the head, is one of the most important parts. The face should be proportionate with the head, so maintain balance in their proportions. Pay special attention to the eyes, making them rounder and larger than the other facial features. Even if you are aiming for a more realistic proportions, consider enlarging the eyes slightly to enhance expressiveness.
  • Body: in contrast to the head, the body tends to lose its shape and become smaller, often as small as two and a half heads. Additionally, the body tends to lose its landmarks on the muscles. To create the desired shape, you will need to exaggerate the basic forms of the body. For instance, you can use a triangular shape to emphasize a muscular body or a rectangular shape to create an slender body.

‚ÄçBig head and big eyes - Body proportions of a Chibi or ‚Äòvery cartoony‚Äô character:

Compare the 2003 version with the 2013 redesign of Teen Titans:

As a general rule; try to use a small color palette, ideally with no more than five main colors. This will help you focus on your artistic goals.

Mastering stylized art requires a thoughtful blend of proportion and a limited yet impactful color scheme for charming character design. With these small tips, hopefully, you can start designing your own characters.

Always remember, the devil lies in the details.

As you continue to practice and refine your skills, you'll discover your unique style and approach to character design.

Don‚Äôt forget, every artist has their own journey, and every character you create is a step on your path of artistic discovery and growth.

What is Art Direction?

It is the LOOK of your production. Design, Color, Lighting, Shapes, Textures - many elements are involved. Art direction can be a broad term depending on which industry and what types of productions you‚Äôre referring to, but this usually pertains to the design style of the backgrounds (locations / sets / environments / scenery) for an animated production. However, in a broader sense, it also includes the character art style as well, which also consists of clothing, props & FX, and how well they all work together - for the overall look and visual aesthetics of the entire production. As a result, lots of research and development is made to find the right look.

These are important factors to know since character design does affect other aspects of the design process on a production. Usually many levels of communication between various artists is needed for creating all aspects of the characters, props, and backgrounds. Keeping everything consistent while meeting the production‚Äôs demands can be challenging. Be aware that as a character designer, you may have a Design Supervisor or Character Supervisor or Art Director as your manager/department lead.

By Alan Stewart

The title of Art Director and Production Designer are often interchangeable. Sometimes they can be a solitary artist working on concept designs and visual development alone, or they can be the lead on a team of designers and held responsible for keeping consistency and cohesiveness through the many departments and stages of the design process.

The duties may vary from production to production and studio to studio, but Art Directors are usually responsible for all aspects of the visual style of the animated film or series. On big productions, there can be an art director for each department - bringing to life the production designer‚Äôs vision and style. They decide how the characters, props and environments are going to look and provide a basis for the rest of the design department to work from.

  • Art Directors on a 3DCG animated film
  • Overall Vision: The Art Director sets the overall style and aesthetic of the film, ensuring it aligns with the director's vision and story.
  • Collaboration: They work closely with the director, production designer, and other artists to guide the creative process.
  • Creative Leadership: They provide direction, feedback, and guidance to the art team, ensuring the film's visual elements are cohesive and impactful.

For example, you can have cartoony-looking characters within hyper-realistic environments like here and here, or semi-realistic characters with highly-stylized or abstract backgrounds like here. The art direction of any animated production can be a very wide spectrum of visual possibilities.

üëâ Pixar Art Director On How To Bring Characters To Life

Art directors start by examining the script or story premise and working with the director and any lead production designers to understand the vision for the film or series. Then they create concept designs and determine the tone, mood, and color palettes of the story.

@AkiiRaii

  • Responsibilities of an Art Directors on a 2D animated series
  • Define the visual style: They determine the look of characters, props, and environments
  • Create designs: They create designs for backgrounds, lighting, and visual effects
  • Establish the tone and mood: They determine the color palettes and mood of the production
  • Work with the director: They work with the director to understand the vision for the production
  • Work with concept artists: They work with concept artists, background designers & character designers to develop sketches and artwork to find the style of the production.

By Alex Kirwan

In animated feature films - Art directors can also be known as CG Supervisors, VFX Supervisors, and Production Designers.

Concept art is the earliest exploration for finding the look of the production and to create visual ideas for everyone to see and comment on and this work is often used as part of a pitch to sell the project, film or series, before production is fully underway. The work of an art director is more specific and directive than the work of a concept artist or visual development artist.

Concept artists are usually freelancers, they come in, bang out a few sketches or color concepts and out they go, and they are the first to draw the exploration sketches for the production. Sometimes when a concept artist‚Äôs work becomes the style that the production is targeting to be what they are going for (whether it be for environments or characters) then they stick around for longer. Since their art is the template that all other designs will be based off of. Usually a few different concept artists are brought in, so they can do a scatter shot of possible visual styles for the creative leads to use as reference. This way they can see which artistic direction they would rather go with. Their artwork is then used as a ‚Äúlook target‚Äù for background and character designers further down the line.

In these very early stages, they produce artwork and collaborate with the studio and directors to find a visual style that works. It‚Äôs important that they communicate well with the client (creator/producer) to understand what is needed. They will often have to go back and redo their designs several times before they find something the studio/client is happy with. Sometimes the first passes are quick and loose for faster results and feedback. Other times, they add more detail and quality. They use computer software as well as hand-drawing and painting to create their art.

Sometimes concept boards or reference boards are made, where lots of images are gathered as an initial inspiration pack by the visual development artist to see if they‚Äôre going in the proper direction. Before committing to sketches, these style boards can cover color palettes, costuming, and hairstyles of all sorts. They can also be made by the art director or creative producers to give to the concept artists and designers, to guide them in the general direction they need to go.

These can also be called mood boards, image boards or aesthetic boards; when it‚Äôs a big job, they are the best place to start before you begin working on your characters/story. It‚Äôll be where you lay out the inspiration for your character as a whole, from their personality and traits to what they wear and how they look. Think of it like a collage of ideas for who your character is. It‚Äôs a chance for you to brainstorm and gather your thoughts before diving into the writing or drawing part of your project.

Art directors determine the ‚Äòdesign language‚Äô of the film, which requires them to have an understanding of what the final, on-screen image will look like and how to get there. They work with the concept artists to develop sketches and artwork that communicate the artistic vision to the rest of the crew. In big-budget feature films, the Production Designer tends to be the one to give the consistent and singular LOOK for the visual style of the world, and the team of Art Directors supervise the many departments at every stage, to execute that vision into realization.

By Nikolas Illic

Associated with Art Direction is the process of Look Development - it‚Äôs the stage in most 3D/CG animated productions that builds the world and characters by creating the colors, textures, and materials for everything you see in the final rendered images. Look Development also explores how to actually achieve the intended visual look, pursuing the technical side of taking the concept art and discovering the process needed to practically attain and achieve the look for the actual production.

In many 3D/CG feature films, Art Directors are involved throughout the entire project, up to its release, acting as a supervisor to the other artists, such as modelers, character designers and environment designers. This is a job that involves a lot of communication with people and needs strong management skills. They are responsible for ensuring all artwork is of a high quality and in keeping with the director‚Äôs vision. They are also responsible for making sure everyone in the art department stays on budget and on schedule. Art directors are sometimes employed as freelancers working on a project by project basis. Art Direction usually splits into two main categories - Environments (also known as: Sets / Backgrounds / Locations) and Characters.

For animated feature films, hundreds upon hundreds of sketches & color concepts are drawn and painted in order to explore and later refine the final look of the characters and settings found in the movie.

In a 2D animated series - Character Design is a portion of the Production Design phase of animation (which can also include Environment & Background Design, FX Design and Prop Design), which is all part of the Pre-Production process, where scripts, voices, visual development, and story art are also produced.

Prop Design

A quick note about prop designs - Most of the time prop designs are made by a separate artist from the design department, there are times where a character designer may be asked to help design props as well. Items like any hand-held objects or vehicles and any item the characters interact with, that are separate from the background or environment are known as ‚Äòprops‚Äô. It‚Äôs important to cover prop design here since they are unavoidably linked to character design.

Just like characters, props need to be designed from various views and angles:

By Alexis May Lambert

For Mobile Games, Prop Designers and BG Designers often share the same tasks as props and various items found in the environment become part of the backgrounds, whether they have animated aspects to them or not:

It‚Äôs common to extract elements from background designs and construct these as their own self-contained item with turnaround views as well, depending on the art director, sometimes props are designed out of the background concepts and sometimes background artists borrow from the prop designs first to incorporate the item in the scenery. Sometimes both background designs and prop designs use the storyboards for reference, other times the designs need to be worked out in detail first, so that the storyboard artists use them as reference and then it gets carried through to final design in the animation scene setup process.

Prop designers have very similar skill sets to character designers and background designers, usually with a strong sense of perspective drawing and structural drawing, with the ability to sketch complex objects in various three-dimensional views and angles.

  • üëâ See these samples by Brent Noll
  • üëâ See these samples by Cat Sukiman
  • üëâ See these samples by Allison Kim
  • üëâ See these samples by March Bachand

Just like characters, prop designs are created in a certain visual style, usually matching the graphic art style of the backgrounds/environments. Sometimes they match more closely to the character art style, especially if the backgrounds are very different from the characters‚Äô aesthetics. But they are objects needing to be designed at various angles since they are meant to be animated. For example, Lilo & Stitch has watercolor-style painted backgrounds, so the prop designs needed to look like they could easily fit within the environments, but still match the characters‚Äô overall art style, for when they‚Äôre interacting with them. Sometimes the props would be watercolor-style, sometimes they would be cel-painted with flat colors when needing to be animated.

Rotations are illustrated for props that will be seen and used a lot:

Size comparisons are usually needed by having a faded version of the main character on display for size relationship, especially for props and items that are being handled and used by characters:

Just like for character models, various views and angles are illustrated:

By Eva Strautkalne

By Skifati Moe

Prop designs follow the same visual language as any character and background design:

Props can also be attached to backgrounds, a common example are doors, they are first designed by background artists, but then handled as prop designs once they need to be animated to rotate and be shown at many angles, and being handled by the characters as well. A car parked in the background will only need to be designed from that one angle, but once a character steps inside and begins to drive it around, it will need to be properly designed and built with many angles and views, both the interior and exterior.

Entire buildings can be considered as props, whether or not they are animated (2D or 3D), even if they are levitating around by magic, and not turning or rotating, they still become an object needing to be moved around, therefore they become props.

üëâ Advice on using Varied Proportions in Prop Design

Color concepts and color models are made in the same way as characters, you start off as roughs, then clean lines, then color.

It‚Äôs common for Prop Designers to create their own colors, they may have guidance from the Art Director or need to use background designs or character designs for color reference.

There can be some visual symbolism and personification involved in prop design:

This is the attribution of a personal nature or human characteristics to something nonhuman, or the representation of an abstract quality in human form. Like a tree designed with its branches resembling long arms reaching out, or simply adding a tiny face or an eye on a prop design, all these can add a quality of personality to the object. Examples found in creative writing: ‚ÄúThe sun smiled down on us.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe story jumped off the page.‚Äù

‚ÄúThe light danced on the surface of the water.‚Äù

Examples of this could be: a backpack being designed to look old, saggy, weathered and beaten up, to match the state-of-mind of its owner, or to show what hardships this backpack has undergone. A mirror designed to look monstrous and evil because it holds a magical creature inside. You can always subvert expectations by having a sweet, cute, innocent-looking object actually be used by someone dark and sinister for a certain effect. Imagine what an enchanted key would look like if it was designed to be linked to an all-seeing entity.

In novel writing and scriptwriting, personifying an object means to figuratively describe it with human traits in order to craft a vivid image of that object in your reader‚Äôs mind. But to interpret it in illustration and animation would be to visually depict it so that it is instantly recognizable; to subconsciously have the viewer identify it with a personality type or behavior. The simple act of making a prop look old, worn out and drooping, versus making it look fresh, young, new, clean, perky and firm - can drastically change the feeling of how the design will be perceived and what history may lie in that prop‚Äôs narrative and purpose.

Principles of appeal, structure, and contrasting shapes also apply to prop design, even the most simple objects:

By Isaac Orloff

Just like for characters, research is often necessary to do. For example, something as simple as old fashion forks, could have you researching the history of silverware to help in your inspiration.

There are sites like hum3d.com that are great for searching up and observing hundreds of objects, props, vehicles and anything else of this nature at various angles, for drawing reference.

Gathering photo references to help in the design process is important to the exploration and discovery part of your early concept art and rough sketching. For example, let‚Äôs say you need to design an item for an alchemist‚Äôs portable toolbag. Items to search up would be a portable tackle box or tool bag (adding old or vintage in your search) - begin sketching with a wide range of research images and see what items you can combine together:

Sometimes props can become very complex, as a single item may need to be illustrated from many different views and angles, and may have to be opened up to reveal its interior. Vehicles are often the case, as scenes can take place inside the vehicle as its own location, with plenty of moving parts, and lots of items needing to be handled and interacted with.

Oftentimes props can be very detailed and need lots of guides and instructions on how they work, along with versions made for close-ups and for far shots so that detailed versions can be reserved for up-close/insert shots.

Notice how a red line is commonly used to indicate to the color stylist that those lines should be self-colored or for that linework is meant to be removed once the shapes are painted:

In a typical 2D animated series, there‚Äôs frequently more props to be designed than they are characters. Sometimes props and character designs cross paths, like when a character removes an article of clothing from their body, then it becomes this independent item that comes off the character and is now technically a ‚Äòprop‚Äô, since it‚Äôs now an item another character could grab and pick up and interact with, and maybe dropped on the ground, then becoming part of the scenery.

Sometimes, as part of the character design, the model sheets need to have props included, like a samurai warrior with their katana, or a wizard that‚Äôs always holding their magic staff, a character always wearing their backpack, or a butcher always carrying a cleaver with them. Usually it‚Äôs items that are inherently linked to the character at all times, making it part of their character design by default. These are accessories that the character always has with them, therefore become a part of that character‚Äôs design.

Just like for character design, reference is just as important (if not more so).

When it comes to prop design, an element of selective exaggeration and caricaturing is often implemented as well, just like you would for any character stylization/simplification - it all depends on the needs and the look of the production. Often the environments, props, and vehicle designs must be shaped and constructed in very particular ways to make them appear like they belong and match the world they‚Äôre in, alongside the characters they‚Äôll be used with. They can evoke lots of life and personality in their appearance, but they are usually built to be appealing and functional. As an example, let‚Äôs look at one particular aspect of prop designs; focusing specifically in the area of vehicles in the ‚Äúchoro-Q‚Äù style:

‚ÄúWhen designing vehicles for an animated production, it‚Äôs always good to clearly display the characters‚Äô faces. Many shows have the characters with larger proportioned heads, so the windshields should be adjusted accordingly. Toriyama knew this, and a lot of his manga work featured appropriately-designed vehicles, but cartooned in a style that works well for showcasing the characters. This chibi-style of vehicles became a Japanese toy fad in the early 80s named choro-Q, and they were known in North America as penny racers.‚Äù -Murray Bain, Art Director

Keep in mind that the props your characters handle and the vehicles they operate are essentially acting as supporting characters. They need to be practical for the needs of the production and what is happening in the story. How the characters USE the props must be kept in mind when constructing their shapes, forms and proportions.

For example, drawing vehicles can be one of the most challenging aspects of prop design. Just like for character design, vehicle designs need to be researched and collected for you to be inspired from.

Shape Language plays a major part in the design process for props & backgrounds, and an exploration phase when creating the concept art is important as well.

Props and certain aspects of background design need several views and angles (for locations that will be seen very often):

By Steven Sugar

Style guides can be made for prop design packs as well, these cross-over into background designs of course, as seen here for the Big Hero 6 series:

Notice how interior design and furniture as well as exterior architecture and be ‚Äúcaricatured‚Äù to give buildings a sense of personality and distinctiveness as well:

All animated productions have rules and guidelines like this, sometimes they are outlined in large volume and detail, many times they are barely documented officially at all, for reasons like lack of time to do so, and usually these style guides and directives are not solidified until late into the production.

The spectrum of Realism vs Caricature is present in all aspects of design stylization, including Backgrounds and FX designs as well. Some productions can barely have any FX design in them due to the nature of the stories, they don‚Äôt require too many animated elements like, water, smoke, fumes, fire, splashes, ripples, sparks, electricity, and explosions. Other shows can have massive amounts needing to be made, depending on what the style and genre the story and world are set in. Sometimes the FX animators create their own FX designs based upon the storyboards and established art direction, other times dedicate 2D FX animators become designers to create key poses and colors to make sure the FX animations will stay on-style and consistent in its design and appearance. FX design are often overlooked and usually grouped into the Prop Design category of the design process.

üëâ Rise of the TMNT movie: concept art and design sheets

Sometimes FX designs cross-over into character model sheets, these could be special poses or custom design sheets to show how the FX are functioning with the characters:

Character

Character designers are essentially creating props all the time, the clothes their characters wear, the glasses on their face, the hats on their heads, as soon as they come off the character‚Äôs body technically becomes a prop.

Walkthrough the Process

Pre-Boards and Post Boards

While character design happens continually through the production of each episode of an animated series, there are usually two primary stages:

Designs done BEFORE the storyboard artists start boarding, in order to give the storyboard artists the reference they need to draw the characters in their shots‚Ä¶

And designs done AFTER the storyboard is complete, wherein the designers base their designs on the storyboard artists‚Äô drawings.

The completed pack of design assets is something always in progress, from first reading and analyzing the script, through the storyboards stage and their revisions, then during character builds, and then even last minute additions and changes during layout & scene prep stages (just before animators start to animate the characters in their shots), and sometimes after animation has started. The odd thing here and there will need a quick design, since often some changes occur throughout each stage, and also, items get missed all the time and need to get caught and designed.

Sometimes there‚Äôs a strict and detailed breakdown made of the script, where most assets for characters, props and backgrounds are designed before storyboards begin, this way the storyboard artist has all the reference they need to plan out all their shots and sequences. Other shows have a very small, loose, rough preliminary design pack made, with barely any reference for the storyboard artist to use, giving the storyboard artist more creative liberties to create the concept of these designs, for you to use later to make the official design sheets out of. In this method the storyboard artist is encouraged to create, invent, expand upon the location designs, to make-up any secondary and incidental characters,whatever costume changes required, and create props/FX as needed, whether they were mentioned in the script or not.

This leaves a long list of items for the character designer to tackle, beyond all the assets mentioned in the initial script, but then they have the storyboards as a rough reference to start from, to eventually complete the list of design assets.

Incidental characters, designed using the storyboard as reference, is a very common part of the job.

No two productions are alike and the role and scope of a character designer‚Äôs job can change significantly depending on the production, the pipeline, the supervisors/directors in charge and the style of the show

Design Handout

At the beginning of each episode, the character designer is given the script to read in advance of their design handout.

The design handout is the meeting where character designers are assigned their specific characters that they will need to design in preparation for boards. Sometimes the designs made before boards are called the Fun Pack.

This initial meeting, often called a Design Brief (or Briefing) is basically where it is decided what character(s) will be assigned to which artist and for what purpose. If the designer works directly for a client, for example, this is the part of the process that includes their communication with the person (Producer / Art Director / Supervisor) regarding the character(s).

In this phase, the artist should know the core personality of said character: is it a human, an animal, a creature, or an object (sometimes combinations of a few of these). It‚Äôs not always obvious from the script or story outline alone. This brief also includes a list of certain personality traits and common emotional core values. Establishing the overall mood and mentality this character will communicate to the viewer. This list can be made by the designer by jotting down notes they made while reading the script or during the meeting with the art director or showrunner.

For example, if the project is an educational presentation aimed at third graders, it will require a cute and cheerful character that will appeal to children. Often your client may not be too certain or rock-solid about the backstory or personality, so they offer suggestions of what visual style they are looking for. They can instruct that the overall mood must be very warm and welcoming, even silly and fun. They may point you in the direction of a series, film, or short that they want you to use as inspiration. This phase, depending on the role of the character, is when the artists (or the clients) decide what the characteristics are. Some of those fundamental questions could be:

  • What is the character‚Äôs age? Where is the character from?
  • What do they look like? What are their likes and dislikes?
  • What are the character‚Äôs dominant traits and personality?

During this meeting the Showrunner and the Director of the episode will also tell the designer if there are any particular attributes, racial specificity or personal characteristics that they want the designer to incorporate while designing.

Examples:

  • This character has a kind of Ryan Gosling, comical-cool type vibe.
  • This character is half Korean/half Puerto Rican so try to be conscious of this while designing them.
  • This character is an old alien boss, think Mr.¬†Burns; make him frail, no teeth, hunchback, skinny limbs, big big-eyes, potbelly with a large cape.

The designers receive their character assignments based on several factors:

  • The specific designer‚Äôs strengths and sensibilities
    • For example a character designer who is better with fashion may get the majority of the costume designs. A character designer who specializes in drawing animals will be favored to design the cast of characters for a pride of lions.
  • The availability of the designer
    • Because multiple episodes are in play at once, some designers may wind up with more on their plate than others.
  • The designer‚Äôs speed
    • If a design needs to be turned out fast, it will usually be handed to the speedier designer.

Once the character designer has their allotted characters, they head back to their Cintiq/Tablet and get cracking.

Rough Sketches

Using photo reference, online images, and any specifications from the Director/Showrunner as their guide, the designer will start sketching out their rough ideas for the character.

After doing any necessary research on the subject, the designer will also incorporate their own taste and interpretation to give their designs flair and use their own artistic vision to really push the design.

The designer will create 3-6 different variations of sketches for the character with different body shapes, outfits, hair styles, etc -- this is the visual development/concept art stage. The goal is to create a nice variety of potential directions that still serve the story‚Äôs designated characteristics. Make several iterations and go through your checklist.

Example:

  • The script calls for a ‚Äúgym teacher‚Äù character‚Ä¶ Are they tall? Short? Lean? Buff? Super buff?
  • The script calls for ‚Äúkids in the classroom‚Äù‚Ä¶ Are they 6, 8, 10 years old? Are they bored? Rowdy? Energetic?

The character designer explores all options.

Design Review

Once the designers have their rough sketches lined up, it‚Äôs time for the review!

The Design Review is a meeting where the Art Director (main overseer of the overall look of the series) and the Showrunners look at the new designs and give their feedback and notes.

Usually the result of this initial design review will be a frankensteined new design, based on specific parts from the different sketches submitted.

The Showrunner might say: ‚ÄúI like the head from #1, but the body from #3 and let‚Äôs use the hair from #6‚Äù.

Sometimes the Showrunner or Art Director will just like the one whole design with just some tweaks and adjustments needed -- ‚ÄúLet‚Äôs go with #4, just shorten the hair, make the legs a bit longer‚Äù.

But the Frankenstein-ing approach is far more common.

Revisions and Revisions and Revisions

The process of Design Review >> Tweaks/Adjustments >> Repeat, can go on several more times until the Art Director and then the Showrunner is satisfied with the design.

It can take a while to get the exact look they are going for, to make certain it fits just right for the purposes of the show.

Usually if the small tweaks persist past a certain point, a Line Producer or Producer will step in and tell the design team and the Showrunner when time has run out, and to move on with the latest version, applying the final round of notes. Production Managers and Line Producers keep an eye on schedules and budgets, they let the creative process grow, flourish and expand as it‚Äôs needed, but deadlines and deliverables can only be pushed so far, then they intervene to make sure the flow of production doesn‚Äôt get bogged down for too long.

Red-lining, this the process an art director takes to draw-over your work with visual notes:

Variations in clothing and hair styles are the most common last-stage adjustments to make.

By Jeff Merghart

Rotations

Once a rough 3/4 front design of the character is somewhat settled and approved, it‚Äôs time for the designer to turn the character!

TURN- AROUNDS are the process by which the character designer draws the design in different rotations: 3/4 Front, Front, Profile (Side), 3/4 Back, Back.

In TV animation, turnarounds are essential.

They are the guides that show the storyboard artists and more importantly, the animators, how the character looks from different angles and shows them how to keep the character on-model as they move them through different poses. On rigged character builds the builds artists use the designs to setup and assemble the character as a digital puppet for animators to move around and pose them in their scenes to prep them to be animated.

Does This Turn?

In order for the Builds team to construct and setup your characters into a proper and efficient rig (and for the animation to be fluid and believable), character designs NEED to be able to turn properly‚Ä¶ and often times during this stage of rotating a character, designers will realize where certain features on the character do not turn well. Sometimes more flat and graphic designs are made out of many cheats where they are only meant to look good in a certain way at specific view.

Common things that can come up are:

  • Body shapes or costuming that looks awkward from different angles
  • Facial features that do not translate to certain angles well (ie: when the nose doesn‚Äôt work in the front view)
  • Clunky or angular parts of the design that are messy to turn

If a design doesn‚Äôt turn well, chances are it won‚Äôt animate well.

As a result of this, the designer often has to keep tweaking and changing the design to get it to rotate just right.

By Parker Nielsen

Find the supervisor who will lead the animation crew, get advice from them and watch through the animatic/storyboards (if possible) to see if the character is meant to only be viewed in certain ways. This is because turning their head/body into these views might be quick-and-snappy in order to keep the stylistic qualities of the designs.

Communicating also with the department that will convert your designs into builds is important because oftentimes you can‚Äôt be drawing attention to the fact that the character does not translate well in a three dimensional way. Some designs have lots of ‚Äúcheats‚Äù to make them turn. The cheats make the design look good for each view, however it‚Äôs made to look appealing with its graphic shapes, even though it may not be actually possible to three-dimensionally turn as a rotation, and that‚Äôs fine for more stylized characters.

Clean Lines

Once the character designer gets the design to rotate and has the roughs approved, they start cleaning up the line work‚Ä¶ and once again more inconsistencies or odd things may arise in this stage, final detailing and fine-tuning in this stage of tying things down.

Designs are often tweaked again as they are cleaned up because the tighter line work makes design issues more obvious.

The entire character design process for the pre-board characters usually takes about 2 weeks from handout to clean-up.

Of course this can vary, depending on the production.

Is The Design Finished?

Designs may still be in flux right up until the episode actually goes out to go get animated.

The storyboards can also greatly affect the design.

For Example: The Director may request design changes based on new story changes or physical actions required of the characters made in the boards.

‚ÄúI have a scene where Character X pulls up their hood to cover them from the rain, so let‚Äôs be sure to give their jacket a hood‚Äù

DESIGN and BOARDS are very collaborative, almost in a hidden way, some shows independently operate in their own bubbles but rely on each other to use each other‚Äôs work as reference, other times they‚Äôre often informing each other both ways. The Director and Art Director usually work together to make sure their respective teams are alert to the shifts in design and story.

Another collaborative overlap are the riggers, who come in after the characters are completed in design and proceed to build and set up the designs for the animators to use. Rigging is a crucial process in animation that enables characters and objects to move fluidly.

Rigging in 2D animation is the process of creating a digital skeleton for characters or objects. This skeleton allows animators to control and manipulate movements with precision.

Effective rigging is essential for producing smooth, believable animations.

Key components of 2D rigging include:

  • Joints and bones
  • Control handles
  • Deformation tools
  • Hierarchy systems

By setting up these elements properly, they ensure that characters can be easily posed and animated. This saves time and enhances the overall quality of the animation.

As a rigger, they‚Äôre primary responsibility is to build the underlying structure that brings characters to life. They work closely with animators and character designers to create rigs that are both functional and efficient.

Typical tasks include:

  1. Analysing character designs
  2. Placing joints strategically
  3. Setting up control systems
  4. Testing rig functionality

Riggers need a combination of technical skills and artistic understanding. They must anticipate how characters will move and ensure the rig supports these movements seamlessly.

Post Board Designs

Once the boards are done, the character designers may receive new designs in addition to whatever designs need to be tweaked based on boards.

To save designers time, during that initial Handout, Directors can call out which characters the board artists can board WITHOUT a pre-design‚Ä¶

But once the storyboards are done, those boarded characters will still need to be designed properly.

Screenshots of the boards are sent to the character designers who then create on-model character designs based on what the storyboard artists roughed in.

It is very common for storyboards to be completed with un-approved character designs in them, storyboards are rough, never 100% on-model or on-style, and the characters sketched in the boards are meant to be place-holders and used as reference for the process of creating the final designs. Then the rigs created from these designs will be the ones placed in & set up in each individual scene for the crew to animate with.

Alternates and Multiple Versions

These can be sometimes referred to as ‚ÄúALTs‚Äù -- these are designs required for hair and costume changes, but is also used for different states as well, like a charred or burned or wet versions of the character design.

Expressions, Mouth Charts & Special Poses

Another stage in the TV animation character design process is designing expressions and mouth charts. Once the boards are finished, the character designer will typically design mouth charts for the characters to be sent to the Builds team along with the turn-around which will have the characters get assembled and rigged into a build then sent to the animation team, along with the animatic (the storyboard assembled into a video with voices and sound effects to have the episode constructed into a blueprint showing all the camera moves, the length of each shot, and with the episode as a whole, at the proper length of time.

The mouth chart will guide the animators when they animate the characters speaking. They show what the character‚Äôs mouth shapes are for the different syllables for all characters with speaking roles, this is for all the lip sync animation they‚Äôll have to do. This ensures that the character remains consistent as they speak their dialogue.

Expressions sheets are usually only made for primary characters, to show how to stay on-style as a spectrum of expressions are made as reference for animators to have so they can understand the characters better for their facial anatomy and range of emotions.

A Special Pose is a specific character design based on extreme or exaggerated or purposefully off-model drawings done by the board artist in the storyboard.

The character designer will tie down this extreme pose and make it more on-model, while keeping the same energy found in the board drawing.

This is to help the animation team know how to interpret and handle the very exaggerated or very specific pose in the board so it doesn‚Äôt break style. These are usually drawn by the best artists on the crew and can be the most fun (and most difficult) to do.

Sample of Special Poses with storyboard comparisons:

Do Character Designers Color Design?

Typically NO.

Most TV animation production pipelines have separate artists known as Color Stylists or Color Designers whose job is to color the characters and props.

TV animation character designers usually work predominantly in black and white.

Some character designers may use gray tones in their designs to help break up the linework, to make the designs clear for presentation purposes. Blocking out different shapes and subtly guiding the color stylist in the direction they were thinking.

Every show is it‚Äôs own unique animal and there may very well be instances where you may be allowed (or asked) to color your characters designs. Especially on smaller productions where character designers are asked to complete many aspects of the design process.

If you happen to have a knack for color, you may be asked to do color concepts in your rough drawings as well, at the very least, to get the process going.

Who Do I Work With? - Part one

Primarily, character designers work closest with the Art Director.

The art director (or design supervisor) is the foremost person in charge of the overall look of the series and tracking all assets. They guide and support the designers to make sure all the character designs stay ON-STYLE with the look of the show.

Character Designers will also receive notes from Directors and Showrunners. They may communicate with COlor Stylists to discuss their ideas for the color treatment on certain things. For example: ‚ÄúI thought maybe this character‚Äôs hair could be two-toned‚Äù.

Who Do I Affect? - Part one

Character designers affect the storyboard artists but they are also heavily affected by them, along with directors as well. The way designers are used for the story can greatly dictate what the design needs. Designs and Boards are two very interdependent departments, and are often very symbiotic.

Another department they communicate with are the artists that take the designs and create rigs out of your designs. The Builds team take the turnarounds and assemble it all into a model that the animators can use and manipulate in their scenes.

Tools of the Trade

For the most part, character designers in TV animation are working with Adobe Photoshop, or similar programs like Procreate. Photoshop provides a tidy UI to both sketch and polish designs and is a software any aspiring designer needs to be fluent in.

There are many other illustration software like Gimp, Affinity, Clip Studio Paint, Krita, Paint Tool SAI, and Procreate. In reality, they can ALL do the job very well. Anything that allows you to export layers as bitmap (PNG alpha) images for the next artist to grab and use to build the character as a 2D rig is all you need. Your employer or supervisor may have specific requirements to use certain software, templates and resolution settings, these are all easy to ascertain and adapt to. Ask your client/director so that you can get familiar with the tools and templates ahead of time.

  • üëâ Guide to all the various file types for design and illustration
  • üëâ Full tutorials on drawing characters in Photoshop my Dermot O‚ÄôConnor

Pen Tablets/Drawing Tablets are an essential tool nowadays, whether you are designing with bitmap or vector-based software, or designing for TV/film or video game industries.

Look into this guide on the best digital tablets (expensive, but it‚Äôs the best investment you can make and they last forever)

  • Using Harmony as your character design tool:(1) (2)
  • Using Illustrator as your character design tool: (1)

Never underestimate the power of sketching in your sketchbooks, it‚Äôs one of the most important self-teaching tools to use, and try it with a pen instead of a pencil, the results may surprise you.
