Terminology

  • Intellectual Property: Pertaining to creations of any artistic works, usually referred to as ‚ÄúIP‚Äù. It can include designs, symbols, logos, names, and images used in all film, television or video games. It can be for individual characters, the series or film title, and even the concept of the world and story they reside in. A ‚Äòproperty‚Äô is always referring to the concept or show itself. See more general info on the term here.
  • POC: Proof of Concept is material (artwork or writings) typically derived from a pitch or pilot project, which demonstrates that a design concept or story proposal is feasible, and shows what the idea actually is. In animation it comes from pitching and attempting to sell a new intellectual property as an idea for a potential series or film and it applies for series, shorts and videogames. Creating a proof of concept for a new animated film or series could involve creating a pitch deck, which is a document or package of writings and artwork aimed at outlining what the concept is about and how it looks, usually containing character designs, background designs, character descriptions and the story premise. Sometimes an animated teaser or sizzle reel is made to show off the look and feel of what the animated property would be and these are also called a Proof of Concept.
  • Public Domain: This refers to creative materials that are not protected by intellectual property laws such as copyright, trademark, or patent laws. The public owns these works, not an individual author or artist. Anyone can use a public domain work without obtaining permission, but no one can ever own it. Researching which characters are in public domain can help insure that and resemblances you do create for your own characters will or will not be in danger of any copyright infringements.
  • NDA: A Non Disclosure Agreement is one of the first forms a producer will have you sign for them, even before a work contract. This is because an NDA is a legally binding contract that establishes a confidential relationship between you and your employer (producer/studio/client). The individual or parties signing the agreement all agree that the sensitive information they may obtain will not be made available to others. An NDA may also be referred to as a confidentiality agreement. If you are employed by a studio, oftentimes you need to sign one just to receive the visuals or written info about the characters/productions before your studio bids on the production or negotiates for the contract and budget, long before the work begins.
    • Non-disclosure agreements are also common for studios entering into negotiations with other studios, or a producer wanting to share information (scripts, designs, discussions about the production) with you, or the studio you‚Äôre with. They allow the parties to share sensitive information without fear that it will end up in the hands of competitors. In this case, it may be called a mutual non-disclosure agreement. They are all the same in their intentions and purpose. In the video game industry there are very strict processes for security and not allowing any information about their projects to leak out to the public. They are very common and nothing to be afraid of, it means that you agree to not talk about the property with anyone else outside the production and your bosses. This includes anything online; emails, texts, conversations, and social media.
  • Onboarding: A human resources term referring to the process of introducing a newly hired employee into an organization. For a new designer in an animation or gaming studio, it‚Äôs an important part of helping employees understand their new position and job requirements. This usually consists of tours and handing the new employee some documents showing contact info of your leads/supervisors, descriptions of the production you are working on, getting familiar with the file server, workflow guides, schedules and lists of deadlines, info as to how to submit your work, and receiving any references for the tasks that are required of you.
  • Pipeline: A term that refers to the workflow for the entire production. The bigger the production, the bigger the crew, the bigger and longer the pipeline. Design departments sit near the beginning of the production pipeline, but various forms of design tasks continue throughout the entire production, especially on an episodic-based series, and even in feature films where designs are needed even in final visual effects stages. See this for where and how designs fit into the pipeline of a typical 2D animated series, and see this for how the production portion of the pipeline works in a 3D animated feature film.
  • Demographic: This term is used to describe who the designs (and the production) are aimed for. In most cases there‚Äôs an age range (or a target audience) for the animated characters being created along with the stories & world they‚Äôre a part of. While designing the character you should keep in mind the nature of the audience viewing these characters. For example, if you are designing the characters for children it should be designed with basic shapes and colors. The genre and rating on the game or the genre and rating of the film you are designing for is usually your main indicator of what age group this production will be targeted for. Designs for a horror game will have a very different art direction than what you will find in a kids‚Äô game like Minecraft, Lego, or Little Big Planet. A game or an animated series for mature audiences will have characters looking differently compared to characters found in a game or series made for kids under 12 year-olds, and even more different for preschoolers.
  • S&P: Standards and Practices, the term used by broadcasters and networks as a set of corporate rules to follow to self-regulate the content on the animated series, strongly depending on the demographic, the younger the intended audience the longer the list for S&P for everyone to follow. For example, there are long lists of S&P for children‚Äôs or pre-school series, since it‚Äôs intended for young audiences, for adult animated content, the S&P regulations are far less. For PG or Teen related demographics, the level of violence depicted on screen must be limited. S&P passes are made during script writing, designs, storyboarding, and final animation stages. Revision notes may come in on character designs in order to remove or change aspects of the design they may deem as inappropriate or not on-brand.
  • EP: Executive Producers consult on the production and provide specialist expertise. They may also help other producers on the business side of things by facilitating the finances and contracts between broadcaster, distributor and the service studio providing the design and animation work. Some producers are in charge of fundraising and marketing for the production. The title of executive producer may also be credited to the original creator of the source material that is being adapted, and as a result, may also serve as head writer and showrunner for the series. They are often the ones seeking out and hiring the writers, directors and other key personnel to get the production started once it is greenlit and set to go.
  • PM: Production Manager, the person to go to for contracts, schedules, and payment questions and concerns. They manage all the stages of the production‚Äôs deadlines and track each artist‚Äôs daily and weekly progress. They can often act as the human resources person as well, but usually are the ones most in communications with the client/executive producer.
  • Showrunner: The series showrunner is the person with the overall vision for the production. Writing, producing, and creative leadership is their main focus. They work closely with voice casting, voice recording, scriptwriting, character development, and guiding the heads of departments like episodic directors, art directors, animation supervisors, storyboard supervisors, and post production supervisors. This is to ensure that all aspects of the production in each stage on the creative level, are on-style and up to the appropriate levels of quality.
    • Oftentimes the showrunner is the creator of the series concept, and sometimes this role is in two people‚Äôs hands, where they split the responsibilities - usually one showrunner focuses on scripts, voices, storyboards, sound designs & music, and the other showrunner overlooks designs, animation, backgrounds, colors, compositing/FX and quality assurance. Sometimes they act as the head writer, supervising director, or creative director for the series. Other times they will communicate all their thoughts about character design to the art director so that the info can be transferred to the designers. Other times the design style comes from their personal style directly, so a designer would work closely with showrunners at the beginning, to achieve the look they‚Äôre going for.
  • Design Coordinator: This can have many other names, such as, Assets Manager, Design Supervisor, Artistic Coordinator, but their role is to assist the Art Director in managing the deliveries, approvals, versions, and scheduling of the design process. When there‚Äôs dozens of episodes overlapping, the number of characters, props, and key locations that accumulate on the production can go into the hundreds, and when the Art Director is focusing purely on the creative & artistic side of things while conducting quality control amongst their designers, sometimes a design coordinator is needed to manage all the many lists of assets and design elements on the show.
  • Pilot: The first episode of a series, created to test the concept, characters, and overall potential of the show before committing to a full season. It serves as a proof of concept and a pitch to networks and streamers, showcasing the show's premise and style. If the pilot is successful, the show is "picked up" and a full season is produced, with the pilot often becoming the premiere episode.
  • Visual Development: The building blocks for any type of visual art, from concept art to graphic design, illustration, animation, and gaming. The scope of a visual development artist‚Äôs work gives them a level of professional creative control that is vital to the entire process. This comes from the versatility of their work being applicable to a wide variety of media formats. They‚Äôre responsible for creating the mood, tone, and color palette of these worlds created from the writers, and creators of the property. No task or element is too large or small; as a visual development artist, you can design sets, environments, characters, clothing, vehicles, props, furniture, lighting, visual effects, and just about everything else that appears in all aspects of a visual work. More info here on the skill sets required in being part of a Vis Dev team. More info here on the work that is made to develop the Art Direction for a film or series.
  • Shape Language: A concept used in design and animation to communicate a mood, story, or history behind the design. Giving familiar meanings to the shapes the characters are made with. When used in character, prop, and background design, shapes can tell a story, show personality, and elicit an emotional response in the viewer without using any words or movements. Depending on the character‚Äôs overall shape or combination of shapes that make up their physicality, it can give visual hints as to the character‚Äôs nature and core identity. Design Language and Shape Design are alternate terms used for the same theory.
  • Thumbnails: Small rough sketches, drawn fast to create lots of ideas for designs or poses, and used to explore various concepts, creating a way to visually rehearse what works and what doesn‚Äôt.
  • Reference: Materials, assets, and images provided to (or researched by) the artist to have as a strict or casual guide to follow. These can be used for inspiration to the art direction of the property being designed.
  • Turnaround: The rotation of the character displaying multiple views/sides to be used as reference for the building and animating of the character, see this guide on the process.
  • Construction: Refers to when you‚Äôre breaking down your character into their basic shapes and forms to reveal the inner structure. Construction lines are usually the geometry, cross-hairs, and guidelines that are drawn through the character‚Äôs features to show the three-dimensional aspects of their body parts.
  • Anthropomorphic: Human-like physical attributes in a non-human character, usually referring to an animal character that acts human, as seen in many animated properties and storybooks. Not just biped animals but 4-legged animals standing up-right. These are animals depicted as walking, talking, dressing, and behaving like people. Even animated smoke that takes the form of a person can be described as anthropomorphic.
  • Asset: A general term for all aspects of designs and rigs, including all states and versions of characters, props, FX, and backgrounds. Even though they go through their various stages of rough, color, builds, etc., they all need to be tracked for the progress of each design element, and these are all referred to as ‚ÄòAssets‚Äô. On big productions there can be Assets Managers and Design Coordinators in charge of tracking all assets, which is usually referring to all the individual visual elements seen on-screen. Designated asset names and numbers are determined after the script is complete and then again after storyboards are complete.
  • Lead Sheet: A spreadsheet outlining all design elements found in the production. In an animated series all characters, props, FX, and environments/locations are listed and cataloged for which shots they all appear in for each episode. At first these are broken down from a script as a preliminary design pass, then after storyboards are complete, any additional designs created are extracted from the animatic to then be added to the library of design assets to be officially made. As the production moves forward and more episodes are completed and locked at script and storyboard stages, the more lead sheets are made and updated. These breakdowns are sometimes called a Route Sheet, Assets List, Design List or Log List. A script ‚Äòbreakdown‚Äô is the process of extracting all the information from the story to produce lists of all design assets like this, the process is done again after a storyboard/animatic is finalized to list all the on-screen design elements that were added by the story artists.
  • Design Brief: A meeting to discuss the design tasks, happening just before the first pass of the design process. On a feature film this can happen for each new sequence that goes into production, on a series it happens before each episode enters production, usually every week or two. It‚Äôs accompanied with a document created by the showrunner, creative director, art director, or episodic director to give to the design crew as a guide for each episode‚Äôs breakdown, for any new design elements that need to be created. It‚Äôs a process usually reserved for 2D or 3D animated shows where each script is analyzed and visual reference is compiled for specific direction on how to produce the new key backgrounds and characters found in the story.
    • Also known as a Design Kickoff or Design Launch. These meetings with the designers have the art director or series director talk about the specifics of the design tasks that are next to be started in the production. These are discussions on the direction and approach for new characters or character re-dresses.Briefings can look like excerpts from the script pages with images in the margins (on the side of the text); Showing reference images like sketches, photos, and thumbnails for the artist to follow as visual guides. Other briefings can look like a simple document or design kit with reference images and descriptions, provided by the director.
  • Design Kit: Usually something handed out during a Design Brief, these are also known as a Ref Sheet, Guide Sheet or Reference Pack. It‚Äôs a document containing various visual references or guidelines for particular designs that can be used by the designers as a target to have in mind while they work. A Design Kit or Design Handout can even be a ‚Äúhow-to guide‚Äù with files and imagery showing how to create certain artistic aspects of the design style, it can have tutorials on how to achieve a certain look or effect. These sometimes include focused step-by-step processes and technical information as well. These kits can be handed out during or just after the design launch and are made for designers to have them to better know what the intended look and style of specific design tasks they are assigned, usually made after the storyboards are done.
  • Show Bible: Can be referred to as a Design Bible when specifically made for Art Direction. Show Bibles contain all the general information needed for the design team to use as reference for the overall style of the series for both characters and backgrounds. It can also have guidelines for how to handle and produce other aspects of the production as well, like the writing style (for the scriptwriters), the shooting style (for the storyboard artists), and the sound design style (for the musical score and sound effects artists).
  • Deliverables: A list of what stages will need to be delivered for your work. Sometimes there‚Äôs specific deadline dates to follow, other times it‚Äôs more of a general weekly arrangement of deliverables to submit for review for each character and for each stage of the design process. The actual design package put together that is needed to go out every week for a client or showrunner to review can also be referred to as ‚Äòdeliverables‚Äô.
  • Redress: A character that‚Äôs already been designed and finished, but is now partially re-designed to create new and different versions. Usually alternate varieties like hair style and costume changes. Examples can be stuff like adding a backpack, having wet hair, added gloves, torn clothes, different boots, burned/charred clothes, damaged armor, muddy clothes, or a new hat/hooded jacket pulled over the head.
  • ALT: Alternate or Alterative, usually referring to an alternative to a design. When presenting ALTs, you usually show different versions as options to use, sometimes minor design or color variations.
  • Off-Model: A term used to point out how a design or the drawing/posing of a character is off-style or inaccurate to how they should look. When some feedback is given on some designs, character layouts, or animation poses the notes can refer to them as being ‚Äúoff-model‚Äù. They need to be revised and redrawn to be ‚Äúon-model‚Äù by using the reference shown in the provided revision notes, style guides and model sheets. Sometimes moments are drawn to purposefully look off-model to capture the intent of the scene for the more extreme nature of the expression/pose at the time, Ren & Stimpy famously did this constantly. Other times the designs go off-model but for the purpose of creating or copying another art direction style entirely, therefore creating new models to be used for just a scene or specific to that sequence or episode, as often seen in Teen Titans Go!
  • 3pt, 4pt, 5pt: Abbreviations for how many views to illustrate when creating the rotations for the characters, for example - 3 point turnarounds are usually Front, 3/4 Front, 3/4 Back views - 5 point turnarounds are usually Front, 3/4 Front, Side, 3/4 Back, Back views. This can range between 2pt and 16pt.
  • Lineup: When one view of multiple characters is placed on one sheet to see them all at once for size comparison purposes. It‚Äôs important to show the scale of all your characters relative to each other.
  • Fun Pack: The pre-board design bundle, all the design sheets for the primary characters and locations, sometimes as rough designs, to be used as reference for the storyboard artists. This is usually the bare minimum design sheets a board artist needs to know what the cast and settings depicted in the script look like. Final model sheets then builds happen during and after the storyboard process.
  • MDP: Main Design Pack, the completed sets of all the main characters and background designs, to have as reference for all departments.
  • Style Guide: This expands upon the Pitch Deck/Show Bible, having elements of the model pack but refers to all character and prop/location design references for the film or series, and expands upon it. With a focus on guides for how to create the backgrounds and characters in its particular style. It covers many aspects of the production‚Äôs art direction; as shown here and here. They can be small or large and elaborate documents that outline DOs & DONTs for the animation teams and background painters, or specific ‚Äúhow-to-draw‚Äù playbooks for the design crew.
    • These are sometimes also called Pasteup Sheets and Breakdown Sheets. Style guides can also be made for background layout designs and storyboarding; and for painting and color guides as shown here. Style guides are a great way to bring together a group of people to create one cohesive style for the design and layout/animation teams. Style guides can even be created before the concept goes into production, and later the MDP becomes included in the style guide.
    • Thinking about what your style guide would look like is a great place to start when developing your show or film concept as a pitch idea, you begin by laying down all the visual rules and standards. These can also be called a Design Bible, usually seen in animated series, and one can be made for each department: Script Bible, Storyboard Bible, Animation Bible, each one made specific for the team of people working in those stages of production.
  • Workbook: Usually used in 2D animated feature film productions, it is an expanded upon style guide/lookbook & background design guide. It is made during the character design / production design phase and it keeps growing in size and scope while overlapping storyboards and layout stages. It‚Äôs a collection of artwork and guidelines for design, color, lighting, staging and layouts, and handed out to all artists to use as reference.
  • Readable: A term used to identify how much a design or pose can be ‚Äúread‚Äù. Meaning the more readable a design is, the more clear and recognizable it will be. A design that doesn't read refers to a character visual that is difficult to understand or identify at a glance, often due to confusing silhouettes, overly complex details, or a lack of distinct features that make it hard to discern their personality or role in a story.
  • Plusing: A broad term you get from notes made by a director or producer, generally meaning to plus the design further, adding more quality and refinement to a certain area or to the overall artwork.
  • Naming Convention: A system established on how to label and name the designs relative to how the digital files are named. There‚Äôs a million different ways of doing so, and most of the time your supervisor/director on the production will establish a template for you to follow.
    • Char_Julian_CLR_TRN_V05
    • Char_Maximus_CLN_TRN_V04
    • Char_RoyBatty_RGH_TRN_V03
    • Here‚Äôs a common template to use for the asset naming method. This one shows the first part identifying the asset as a character, the second part is the name of the character, then the status being concept art or rough or clean line art or color, then the state that it is a turnaround, and then the version number. You can have acronyms of the series name and the season and episode numbers and short forms for Rough (RGH) Clean-up (CLN) and Color (CLR) and many others that a supervisor or director usually determines.
    • You can also have the series abbreviated name and season+episode number as shown here‚Ä¶
    • JT_S01E10_Cha_MrEagle_CON_TRN_V02
  • Color Roughs: Thumbnails or rough sketches made with paints rather than line work.
  • Draw-Overs: When a supervisor or director sketches over a pose or design to visually show what adjustments need to be made for the design revisions. These are also called redlines or red-lining.
  • Clean-up: The finished line art, the process of tracing-over rough designs to produce the final lines on the characters. Various styles and weights to the lines can be used. Depending on the art direction, sometimes a much thicker outline is required (like Dexter‚Äôs Laboratory and The Grim Adventures of Billy and Mandy), or very thin linework (like Family Guy and Bob‚Äôs Burgers), or wobbly/boiling linework (like Ed, Edd & Eddy), or thick-to-thin tapered lines (like Sam & Max, The Hollowand Primal), or a messy dry brush style (like Foster‚Äôs Home for Imaginary Friends and Jacob Two-Two), or a loose/sketchy line style (like Tante Hilda), or a graphite pencil-textured line art style (like Penn Zero and Green Eggs & Ham), or a nearly lineless style (like Samurai Jack). Search up all of these, have fun collecting design styles!
  • Line Weight: The thickness of your lines. Sometimes referred to as Line Gauge.
  • Line Value: The color and lightness/darkness of your lines.
  • Self-traced: Also known as self-colored or self-inked, referring to the colored line art in your design. The line work is sometimes just a lighter or darker version of the base fill color found around the line, instead of the typical black lines on a character design.
    • Example; Spongebob‚Äôs colored outlines are just a darker tone from his base skin color. See how Daffy‚Äôs arms and facial features have outline colors turning white when crossing over the body or line work needing to be visible for expressions - this way they don‚Äôt get lost as the dark-on-dark color overlaps. FX designs like water, fire, and smoke are often made of colored outlines, or no lines at all.
    • Self-traced lines can be used to make the lines visible when they would normally disappear or blend in too much, or can simply be painted lines, anything other than the default black tone, to make the linework stand out more clearly. Another example is when you need to isolate the edges of where the shadows are on your black-and-white character designs.
  • Draw-Throughs: When you need to show what is underneath a design element or body part, usually hair, capes, cloaks or even an arm that is overlapping the torso.
  • Economy of Line: The concept of the line economy is a familiar one to anyone who has studied Ô¨Ågure drawing or has done classical animation. Basically, it means that we should strive to draw efÔ¨Åciently by using fewer lines to say what we need to say, rather than a lot of unnecessary lines that lack a sense of intent. In character design this translates to visually depicting everything you need without unnecessary details, capturing the shapes, forms and personalities with as few lines as possible. This is associated with the term ‚Äòline mileage‚Äô which means the amount of lines drawn in a design, illustration, or any hand-drawn animation scene. The more line mileage a design has, the longer it takes to re-draw that design over and over again.
  • Cel Shading: A technique used in illustrations and animation to give 3D objects and characters a more 2D/cartoon-like appearance. The term "cel" refers to celluloid, the clear plastic sheets that were once used in traditional animation to create hand-drawn animations. Most commonly seen in anime, where the shadows are hard-edged as opposed to a soft-edge. There‚Äôs a similar look with toon-shading; a rendering technique designed to make 3D computer graphics appear to be flat by replacing smoothly mixed in gradient shaders from the shadows, highlights and tones to appear as blocks of color and hard-edged shapes, along with the outer edges and inner details having an illustrative line added to it, to mimic flat cartoon/comic book style artwork.
  • Rim Light/Rim Shadow: Tone shading indicating a light source on a character, they can also have a hard edge or soft edge.
  • DX or Alpha: The level of transparency on a color or tone, usually for a shadow overlay, or the amount of opacity on a certain character element, often seen for eyeglasses.
  • Back Lit: Also known as BCKLIT, simply an old school term that means having a glow effect on a certain element or the entire character. Could be a glowing gem on your character‚Äôs chest, a glowing light from the helmet on their head, or glowing eyes.
  • Breaking the Model: Also known as ‚Äòbreak the design‚Äô - simply the process done before cleaning up the character into vector art, by separating all the elements of your character. Which means drawing all the pieces in full that are hidden by other overlapping body parts. This is to set it up for the rigging process by layering all the elements separately, filling in any gaps. This can be done by a clean-up artist or character setup artist or builds artist.
  • Builds and Rigs: When character designs are broken up, converted and assembled into fully setup rigs with all their colored assets included. Mouth packs for lip sync, body/head rotations & tilts for various views of the subject, and blink comps for the eyes are just some of the many components created in the rigging stage of production. These rigs, once completed, get handed over to scene setup and then for animators to use in their shots. Often referred to as Builds or Models in 3D/CG animated productions. Also referred to as Puppets or Rigs for Stop-motion productions where the 2D designs are translated into handmade sculpts and fabricated into 3D-printed body parts to be assembled with armatures for them to be posed, animated, and filmed.
  • Stock: Referring to Stock Art or Stock Animation. It‚Äôs when there's a pre-made set of designs, animated movements/cycles or body parts that animators use to recycle and implement into their scenes. Stock Hands could be a library of hand poses that animators extract from a build to use in their scene. Stock Walk Cycle would be an animated walk that was created for animators to grab and insert into their scenes to save them the trouble of re-doing one from scratch. Actions and poses like Jumps, Turns, Blinks, Runs, Expressions and Mouth Shapes (mouth charts for lip sync) can all be considered Stock Art to be saved, archived, and re-used by the builds & animation crew when needed.
  • Character Layout: On a 3D/CG production this is the process of setting up the initial staging of a scene, including camera angles, character positions, and key movements to match and improve upon the storyboard‚Äôs composition and shot framing. This sets up the scene to be prepped for the animators to start on their shots. On a hand-drawn/2D animated production this is a rough posing pass made after storyboards are completed on a traditionally-animated series or film. This is where you draw out the extremes and the main tent poles of the character staging to plot out the acting and action along with the camera angle & framing. Special Poses and Character Layout work can be a great start on your road to becoming a character designer, as work like this shows your ability to draw consistently and on-model, while displaying artistic skill, speed, and diversity in your draftsmanship.
  • Special Poses: When a designer draws certain moments from the storyboard that need more attention to make sure they‚Äôre clear for the animator. Similar to character layouts, where the artist essentially does a re-draw of the storyboard pose to be more on-style and more specific, this can include forced perspective, difficult angles, or a very extreme/exaggerated or particular pose of the character as well. A director or supervisor chooses which drawings get to be made into special poses. These are moments that are either pivotal to the episode or gags that need to make a super clear translation from the storyboard to the final animation. Sometimes these special poses are meant to purposefully be off-model to exaggerate the acting in the scene.
    • ‚òùÔ∏èSpecial Poses by Bob Flynn
    • ‚òùÔ∏èSpecial Poses by Earnie Gilbert
    • ‚òùÔ∏èSpecial Poses by Chris Battle
    • ‚òùÔ∏èSpecial Poses by Mike Milo
    • ‚òùÔ∏èSpecial Poses by Serapio Calm

Summary

  • Designing a New Character

    • Step 1: Research and discovery
    • Step 2: Hone in on your character's traits
    • Step 3: Do lots of sketching
    • Step 4: Finalize your character design drawing (begin with a front view or 3/4 view)
    • Step 5: Create the full rotation for the turn-around views
    • Step 6: Develop some rough poses and expressions
    • Step 7: Get feedback

  • Strategies on your Approach

    • Personality!
    • Visual Interest!
    • Simplicity & Recognizability - Add details and complexity later, if needed
    • Avoid Clich√©s - Unless that‚Äôs the intention
    • Know The Character‚Äôs Story - Where does a character begin, where does it end, and what does it do in-between?
    • In the beginning, when you‚Äôre only exploring concepts; draw more loose, rough, and fast - create lots of thumbnails!
    • Shape & Form - Consider how the character's shapes work in three dimensions and how they look from different angles, while you are searching for that character of yours, play around with hair/fur styles, various attitudes/postures and costumes/outfits > Afterwards, think about detailing & surfacing - are textures, shading or shadows needed?
    • What is the character‚Äôs linework? - Is it thick or thin, tapered or varied, bumpy or textured, colored lines or perhaps no lines at all!
    • Paint - Explore color options (if required), use color to differentiate personalities, highlight features and create contrast > Color can also convey a character's mood, attitude, and role in the story!

  • Things To Keep in Mind

    • [‚úì] Appeal/Believability: Balance originality and familiarity. Create visually appealing silhouettes. Consider how the character's design reflects their role and personality traits. For example, iconic characters like Homer Simpson, Mickey Mouse, and Mario have simple yet recognizable designs.
    • [‚úì] Use References Effectively: Research distinctive styles. Try out a range of possibilities, then repeat and experiment. Seek out and incorporate feedback from others you trust.
    • [‚úì] Uniqueness: Make your character visually distinct and easily recognizable by incorporating symbolic or iconic elements.
    • [‚úì] Audience: Consider what will make your character appealing and believable to your demographic.
    • [‚úì] Backstory: Give your character some history, personality, and motivations that help explain how they look; torn jeans, makeup, scars, braces, hunched back, messy hair, a cane, an eyepatch, an arm cast - things that could add story to their disposition and individuality.
      • Full breakdown here and here.

Four factors to consider when designing your new characters from an established property:

  1. Recognizability of the intended visual style
  2. Shapes, features, and volumes are consistent throughout the rotation
  3. Linework and details all follow a natural sense of logic and appeal
  4. Overall anatomy, proportions, and costumes/surfaces are consistent to the established graphic style

‚òùÔ∏è This is also a general rubrics for each of the assignments outlined below.

The assignments and theory covered below are more intended for 2D animated series/films, but the practice and approach for them can all be applicable to 3D/CGI/Stop-motion & Video Game development as well. The most important thing to remember is to have fun with them!

In terms of timespan; you can hit these 12 assignments full-time and expect to complete them all in roughly 30 to 60 hours total -- Depending on research-time and personal speed, your results may vary. This was originally created as part of an animation program where students would spend 3-6 hours per week, executing each assignment as homework.

The hours increase as the requirements & parameters go up in the later assignments - in which case, picking away at these exercises and completing all assignments within 10 weeks at a very casual pace is often the preferred approach. But this could be all done within just a 1 to 3 weeks at a more steady and dedicated pace, this includes reading, watching, listening to all external links and supplementary materials. So don‚Äôt be concerned about your output, go at your own speed, and produce the best work you can.

You may use the designs you create from these assignments for your portfolio, while citing credit to the original studio/artist --- Example: ¬©Disney ¬©WB ¬©Matt Groening

The Assignments outlined below are to create a NEW character within a chosen established style from an existing series/film.

There are many more specific aspects of character design methods and theories to apply to your work within the rest of these chapters - you will see these with each Assignment, as they gradually cover more principles and techniques with tutorials and advice on various subjects.

You will notice a wide range of design aesthetics to learn from, and some visual guides showing you how to explore these design styles even further. So far, this document (and the many links provided) display a solid starting ground, which can help inform your decisions in the early stages of the development for your character design process. There‚Äôs a few more technical things to be aware of, and you‚Äôll see them as you go through these series of practice exercises. There‚Äôs lots more links and visual aids to be inspired from, time to place all this knowledge into practice, keep scrolling down to see.

Assignment #1

Use these two reference packs STYLE GUIDE & MODEL SHEETS + DESIGN PACK to learn the style of this series and create a new character that would fit in Cartoon Network‚Äôs Adventure Time animated series. Another series in the same style to research is Bravest Warriors.

The Art of Ooo

Designs by Pendleton Ward, Andy Ristaino, Natasha Allegri, Alex Campos, Michael DeForge, Joy Ang, Jon Vermilyea, Michelle Xin, Matt Forsythe, Tom Herpich, Erica Jones, and Derek Kim.

Begin with lots of rough sketches, study the references provided, and start to narrow down the sort of character you want to create.

Make some decisions as to which character you wish to refine and create a front or 3/4 front view.

By Adem Kaan

Advice from Andy Ristain, lead designer on Adventure Time:

Developing an understanding of how to construct a successful character design, and bringing together the components required to execute it commercially, are hard-won skills. But with this expertise comes a wealth of opportunity for those with the passion, drive and imagination to pursue it.

The goal of a character designer is to make the main characters as appealing as possible in as simple a way as possible.

A cartoon about the last human boy, Finn, and a shape-changing dog named Jake, Adventure Time follows their journey in a post-apocalyptic world called Ooo. I think that challenge is what attracted me to character design.

The cast of characters from the Adventure Time series, featuring the main protagonists Finn and Jake, was originally created for a younger audience, but the series has grown and matured into a cultural phenomenon that appeals to adults and children alike. The show has continuity and the actions and decisions the characters make have lasting repercussions.

To become truly great at crafting characters, illustrators need to invest the time in developing their own visual language at the most basic level ‚Äì by drawing every day. Taking inspiration from nature and his surroundings, mastering character design is a process that can't be rushed.

If you're building a family of characters, it can help to create a set of rules that define their graphical elements, before then experimenting with size and proportion. And try to think in three dimensions, draw a character and consider how it would look from the side, back and front ‚Äì not just flat on the screen.

For budding character designers, I have three key tips:

  • Experiment with shape and form
  • Take the time to let your mind wonder
  • Keep drawing

‚ÄúIt takes time, practice, effort and a lot of exploration, but if you keep digging you'll eventually find a place that's totally your own.‚Äù

Feel free to go the extra mile, to do clean-up and color if you want to.

Approach for Designing Characters

The secret to being a versatile character designer is to absorb the knowledge from as many artists as possible and filter-in the aspects that speak true to you. Take your time, watch and listen to the video essays provided below here, and in all the chapters from all the following Assignments. Remember and apply the aspects that make most sense to you. But be open to everything, various styles and techniques are explored and displayed. These are just to get you in the proper mindset, they explore an assortment of different principles, methods, and theories:

  • Learn to Draw in Any Style with Joshua Adeoye
  • Talking About Character Design with Daniel Arriaga
  • Wouter Tulp Design Tips 1
  • Wouter Tulp Design Tips 2
  • Brookes Eggleston - How To Be A Character Designer
  • Brookes Eggleston - The Process

Valerio Fabbretti was the art director for The Fungies! animated series and Becky Dreisdadt who worked as the character supervisor for Adventure Time: Distant Lands, have gone over the process together involved in a typical cartoon character design and how to build a portfolio focused on this aspect, these are portions from a webinar they had hosted:

CHARACTER DESIGN FOR AN ANIMATED SERIES

In terms of character design, Becky comments that it is important to know how to draw in a great variety of angles. Depending on the relevance of each character, it may even be necessary to include additional information and sketches. That‚Äôs why it‚Äôs better that you are deeply familiarized with each character, in case you ever need to bring up more details about them as needed.

Either way, with every character that you work on you need to create a register on how the mouth moves when making sounds or speaking. This is of great help to the animators when they need to work on scenes where there are dialogues. Of equal importance is to draw the characters with different expressions on their faces, for example, when they are happy, sad, scared or angry.

And although it is something not done frequently, Becky admits that one of the most difficult tasks in character design is to draw scenes where there are crowds involved. You need to know not only which characters are going to be in the crowd, but also how many of them will be and how to draw them together but in a way that each one is easily distinguishable. Of course, you need to avoid making tangent lines. This can be difficult while working in a unique character design and even more so where there are multiple ones in a crowd. If this isn‚Äôt hard enough, you also need to be able to draw the crowds as seen from different angles.

Sometimes you also need to modify a character design that‚Äôs already established. This can be either because it needs a new costume, accessories or to show some battle damage in their attire. In this case, you need to follow the references provided by the artist that made the original design, but you are also free to take your own approach and give it some of your personal style. This way you can come up with new and exciting versions of a character design.

THE PROCESS BEHIND CREATING A CHARACTER DESIGN

  • The Art of Steven Universe (1) (2)

When a team of artists gets a new storyboard, Valerio explains that they first analyze it to find if there are any new characters or elements that need to be designed for the episode. After taking note of any new designs that need to be done, he assigns them to each artist taking in consideration their strengths and weaknesses, as well as what they tend to like drawing more.

Being the art director, Valerio is responsible for making sure that each character design stays true to the show creator‚Äôs vision. Not only does he supervise the design process for the characters, but also the way color is applied on them and finally, the way they are animated. After all, it‚Äôs his job to make sure that there aren‚Äôt any errors and that the final art is top notch.

As for Becky, being a character supervisor, she is also deeply involved in this process. She is in charge of supervising her team of artists specializing in character design, as well as reporting back to the art director. One of her tasks is also making a guide of rules and important elements that every artist on her team must follow while creating a character design.

This can be like the way eyes should be drawn, the proportions that must be considered, general mistakes to avoid and so on. This in turn helps to decrease the times a design needs to be corrected, speeding up the process of working on each one of them. Also she is the one responsible for creating the designs for the major characters in each episode.

THINGS YOU NEED TO TAKE INTO ACCOUNT FOR YOUR PORTFOLIO

Valerio says that one of the most important things is to know about anatomy. That‚Äôs why he is so keen on observing if an artist is capable of drawing eyes, hands and feets. In his opinion, this is an indication that the artist knows about anatomic proportions, thereforth is easily able to adapt to different art styles while maintaining the correct proportions.

It‚Äôs also vital to know about perspective, because as a character design artist, you need to be able to draw in a wide range of angles while giving a sense of depth in your work. Becky, on the other hand, says that it‚Äôs fundamental to know about storytelling. That is why she prefers portfolios that show some experience or at least understanding in storyboards and comics. Of course, authenticity is greatly appreciated, so you need to include mostly designs for original characters that you created.

How about showing off that you are proficient in drawing multiple art styles? If you are really great at it, then go for it. But it‚Äôs not necessary in Valerio‚Äôs opinion. He says he prefers seeing an artist with a unique style in which he truly is good at. After all, if you are talented enough at drawing and understand about anatomic proportions, you can learn the art style for a series over time. Becky agrees with this statement, saying it‚Äôs preferable to show consistency and mastery of one art style than regular or poor abilities in multiple ones. But if you‚Äôre sticking to one style (your own personal style), then make sure it‚Äôs appealing and consistent.

When including your character designs, you must show them not only in static poses but in movement also. A good artist is capable of showing some of their character‚Äôs personality through movement, and that‚Äôs why Valerio says he values a portfolio where he can see characters with more dynamic poses.

And while a finished character design is the most valued work in a portfolio, you also need to show the creative process behind it. Valerio comments it‚Äôs helpful to include at least some quick and rough sketches, so a recruiter can see your ability in cleaning up lines and strokes. Finally, this may seem obvious but Becky states that you need to include colored works of art. While it is true that most character design artists only need to work in grayscale or monochromatic schemes, including some colored pieces can make your portfolio look more visually appealing.

The show is set in a comical version of a family living in the Stone Age, but has added features and technologies that resemble mid-20th-century suburban America. The plots deliberately resemble the sitcoms of the era, with the caveman Flintstone and Rubble families getting into minor conflicts, characteristic of modern life. The show is set in the Stone Age town of Bedrock. Dinosaurs and other prehistoric creatures are portrayed as co-existing with cavemen, saber-toothed cats, and wooly mammoths, Brontosaurus are all used as pets or tools in everyday life.

The series draws its humor in part from creative uses of anachronisms; elements belonging or appropriate to a period other than that in which it exists, especially a thing that is conspicuously old-fashioned. The main one is the placing of a "modern", 20th-century society in prehistory. This society takes inspiration from the suburban sprawl developed in the first two decades of the postwar period. This society has modern home appliances, but they work by employing animals. They have automobiles, but they hardly resemble the cars of the 20th century. These cars are large wooden and rock structures and burn no fuel. They are powered by people who run while inside them. This depiction is inconsistent, however, on some occasions, the cars are known to have engines (with appropriate sound effects), requiring ignition keys and gasoline. Also, the stone houses of this society are cookie-cutter homes positioned into neighborhoods typical of mid-20th-century American suburbs.

Expressing Shapes in Design

Practical Character Design Tips from the Etherington Brothers:

All character designs, no matter how complex they seem, can be broken down into a set of simple shapes. The more through lines there are from one shape to another, the better the shapes combine as a single form. Bold silhouettes come from a symbiotic combination of shapes.

To expand upon the three basic shapes of Round, Box, Triangle - there are these 7 shapes you need to design any character.

If you can draw these shapes, you can draw anything.

There can be squashed and stretched variations of these as well, but these are the main ones used for all foundations of character design. The less shapes at the core of your design, the more iconic it becomes.

Building your design around a primary and secondary shape is very effective.

Primary / Secondary can be applied to the face as well.

Using one shape in multiple positions and angles allows a more complex design to remain visually balanced.

Sometimes combining shapes inharmoniously can work well.

‚òùÔ∏è You can see how these design principles apply to backgrounds and scenery as well ‚òùÔ∏è

Dividing Characters into 3 Sections.

A great approach for designing a wide range of distinctive characters is to divide them up into three sections.

  1. Begin by randomly choosing the height of each section as either short, medium or tall.
  2. Next, randomly make the width of each section narrow, medium, or wide.
  3. If we just draw these as standing boxes we can already see the range of body proportions.
  4. Connect the shapes using either curved or angled lines.
  5. With your proportions in place, draw small thumbnail sketches of characters within them. Try lots of designs for each shape, it always works!

The 3-shape approach is so effective, you can use it to take one character and generate endless variations! Let‚Äôs start with this simple robot‚Ä¶

There‚Äôs no end to the options, just change the width and height of the three sections!

Although the 3-shape process is just one of many approaches to character design.

It also helps you to better analyze other characters‚Äô proportions.

Notice the effect on the feel of the character when you cluster the main volume around different areas.

Shapes and Proportions are always working together - See all the various combinations at work here:

By Chris Battle

Why Batman The Animated Series Changed its Character Designs - Writer, Paul Dini Explains the Controversy:

Few adaptations are as beloved as Batman: The Animated Series, however, the series' decision to update character designs in between its original run and The New Batman Adventures is one aspect that did not sit right with fans. Timmverse legend and Harley Quinn co-creator Paul Dini has revealed what led to the polarizing changes.

"I think that was all from the desires of [BTAS co-creator] Bruce Timm and the other artists and directors to vary the look of the characters somewhat between iterations," Dini explains. "Partially it was to make it look a little more like the new look of the Superman animated series, but also to just experiment and refine a few things."

Some of the design changes were warmly received by fans, such as the choice to have an older version of Dick Grayson portraying his popular alter ego Nightwing, and giving Scarecrow a creepy new visage that even included a frayed noose around his neck. Others, though, didn't go over as well. Specifically, the choices to give Joker hollowed-out, black eyes, Catwoman abnormally chalky skin, and Bane's new mask left some fans cold.

Being a veteran of the industry since the 1980s, Dini found the fan reaction to the design changes a bit odd, especially given that character refining has been a common practice in animation for decades. "That tradition of visual reinvention has always been a part of Warner Bros Animation," Dini stated. "No one ever asks about Bugs Bunny and Daffy Duck getting visually redefined over the years, it was just something the directors did over time."

There is also a commercial reason for animation studios to change the look of their characters. Given that the majority of revenue surrounding animation properties comes from merchandising rights for toys, new designs give companies new editions to place on store shelves.

Starring Kevin Conroy, Efrem Zimbalist Jr.¬†and Mark Hamill, Batman: The Animated Series debuted in 1992, and stands as one of the most acclaimed adaptations of comic books to another medium. The series won four Emmy Awards and ran from 1992 to 1995, with follow-up series The New Batman Adventures airing from 1997 to 1999, for a total of 109 episodes.

Construction & Rhythm

Construction lines in animation are the guidelines sketched in to show the angle and forms of all the shapes that make up the design and poses, they often look like crosshairs on the body parts.

Contrasting Shapes

One vastly important aspect of all design is Contrast - it improves your character art by providing the viewer with two (or more) things that play off one another, simply by being different.

Contrast = Interest.

This applies to almost every aspect of art, but it‚Äôs especially important when we dive into character design.

Draw-over notes and feedback from a director:

Unique Shapes + Contrast in Individual Characters:

Designing characters around base shapes for contrast and appeal is a time-tested tradition among animators and illustrators.

Take a look at this image of Po, for a few great examples. First, his color scheme is classic contrast: Black and white. There is no greater contrast than these opposing forces. Look deeper into his design and you‚Äôll see a myriad of contrast from shape. As you can see, the design of the pose itself is a factor in the appeal and clarity of the character and its attitude.

He is made up of many circles overall, all these elements work together to create a visually interesting character. If he was made entirely of one shape it would not be nearly as interesting. Yet at the same time, there is an overall form to adhere to rather than placing things sporadically or randomly. His overall pose is a triangle, which you‚Äôll see repeated in many of the promo images and poses Dreamworks puts the character in. Everything about his design speaks of heavy, friendly, solid.

Breakdown By J.K. Riki

Samples of contrasting shapes:

Unique Shapes + Contrast in a Cast of Characters.

It doesn‚Äôt stop there, though! Not only should characters have contrast in their individual designs, there should be contrast among the whole cast!

These rough drawings from the Aladdin sheet are a perfect example. You see the characters each have a variety of base shapes showing how they contrast nicely against each other. Providing a lot of visual interest, appeal, individuality, and insight on each character‚Äôs core personalities.

In a cast of characters, you want to work with contrasting and complementary shapes:

How to Draw yourself as a Simpson‚Äôs character here.

Finished sample of the ‚ÄòSimpsons style‚Äô with poses and expression sheets:

Although the Simpsons characters and their appearances have become a familiar part of our everyday lives now, it‚Äôs not hard to imagine that it must have taken people quite a bit of time to get used to, when these characters first came on-air with their bright yellow skin tone. The Simpsons creator Matt Groening has spoken about what it felt like to finally arrive at a decision, as he was choosing his characters‚Äô skin color.

Apparently, Groening‚Äôs main motivation was simple: He wanted to make the Springfield residents look memorable and interesting. With all of the other animated series on the air, Groening felt it was important to make sure that Homer, as well as his family and neighbors, stood out from the crowd. Although he considered other colors, yellow was the one that shone through in the end.

‚ÄúThis is the answer! When you‚Äôre flicking through channels, and a flash of yellow goes by, you‚Äôll know you‚Äôre watching The Simpsons,‚Äù Groening once stated.

Character Dimension

Advice from Sam Nielson:

Character dimension is a concept I‚Äôve found very useful when striving for more appealing character designs. I‚Äôm not talking about the literal dimension of forms, but how to give the characters the appropriate amount of personality and interest relative to their screen time in the story. There are a lot of theories on this, so I‚Äôm picking the pieces from those theories that best apply to character design.

This idea of character dimension is best explained with contrast. There are four different levels of dimension as it relates to visual design, with the highest level being a three-dimensional character.

The first level of depth is a ‚Äúbit‚Äù character. Think of a bit as a single point in space, with no dimension. A bit character displays only one character attribute‚Äîusually meant to fulfill a single purpose as that character stands in the spotlight onscreen for a very brief time. Here‚Äôs an example of a bit part: the hopelessly nerdy character.

To keep this character aligned with that bit part, I tried to make everything on him give a ‚Äúsad sack‚Äù feeling‚Äîdroopy, soft, and ineffective. This character is understood in his entirety almost immediately, which makes him useful for brief moments with no introduction. A character like this can still act against type in that instant, but that action must be understood quickly in context of that simple and clear character bit, or the contrasting attributes will only create confusion. The weakness of a bit character is that after this character serves his momentary purpose, he will become insufficiently interesting* for extra time in the spotlight.

(*As a side note, I should mention that any character design can be enriched through great writing and animation. Appealing and appropriate characters can make great writing and animation better, but even the best character design will struggle without the other two.)

Imagine taking that single point in space, that surface character attribute, and stretching it into a line. Now you have two poles possible with contrasting components, or a one-dimensional character. The contrasting attributes here are different from a bit character acting against type, because this character may be seen at varying points along that line throughout the film (while a bit character acting against type will always act the same). My example of a one-dimensional character adds aggressive confidence to a hopelessly nerdy character.

I communicated the two poles of this character‚Äôs single dimension by contrasting gesture, expression, and shape language against his easily understood role or type. You can also do this with contrasting costume decisions, color scheme, and other elements. Usually, the one-dimensional character is defined by ‚Äúwhat,‚Äù meaning both poles are expressions of how that character fills his role in the world. One-dimensional characters remain interesting for longer, and they can even have simple character arcs, but they lack any real depth.

Adding one more dimension to a character creates depth and a lot more interest. This second dimension is usually defined by ‚Äúwhy?‚Äù, or the character‚Äôs history and motivations. Expanding on the example character, who now has one dimension that goes between hopelessly nerdy all the way to confident villain, but also adds a second dimension that shows a little bit of the inner demons driving him; a life of bullying and misfortune.

In this case I imagined the opposing poles of his second dimension are his need to be someone‚Äîto have power over his life, contrasted with his complete helplessness in the context of the strength and socially-driven world he has always lived in. I used recent fight-related injuries and the expression to suggest the character‚Äôs history and the hardening of his motivations. Typically, the visual language for history and motivation is going to be a second read, meaning that the first dimension is the first stuff the audience sees, but is then modified with a closer look at the character. A character with this much complexity needs enough screen time to show all combinations of the four extremes of these two dimensions; otherwise he collapses down to a one-dimensional character in the story, in which case the audience will feel dissatisfaction and an intuitive need to know more about him than his screen time allows, because of the amount of conflict the character suggests. That said, even if I‚Äôm designing a one-dimensional character, I will often explore the second dimension story-wise because understanding the backstory and inner demons can suggest interesting expressions of that single dimension that I wouldn‚Äôt have thought of otherwise.

The third dimension is the ‚Äúwho‚Äù of the character; and it is the hardest one to quantify in the visual parts of the character design. This dimension demands a lot of screen time, as the character now needs to go through the hero‚Äôs journey. A protagonist, the love interest, a sidekick, or even the main villain can be three-dimensional, so long as there‚Äôs enough time to unravel all three dimensions.

This character starts the story being defined by WHAT he is (the hopelessly nerdy kid bordering on supervillainy), then the story would explore WHY he has become that way (inner demons of social and physical abuse by peers he wants to impress), but to gain the third dimension, then the story must test the limits of that character beyond the surface attributes and internal desires or motivations. In other words, you can‚Äôt know WHO this character is until you have seen the things that outwardly define him stripped away, and see the choices he makes when facing the resulting desolation of his failure. In visual design terms, you cannot show this journey all at once! But you can show the potential for that character to change. More nuance in shape language, a little baby face bias, more thoughtful, vulnerable eyes, and even a less ‚Äúset‚Äù demeanor can help cue the audience that this character is not yet done with his journey. Unlike previous dimensions, you can sometimes apply three-dimensional design to a character in a two-dimensional role without frustrating the audience; so long as the suggestion of potential-for-change doesn‚Äôt compete with the role the character plays in the story.

Is it possible to have a character with more dimensions than this? Yes; even four and five-dimensional characters can work in a story, but fitting that extra information into a design is very difficult! My experience is that the first and second dimensions can be layered into a character after the initial design, so your design process doesn‚Äôt need to include all this information from the start, so long as you can get your art director to be patient with the exploration of those dimensions. Character dimension is less about which is good, better, or best; and more about designing characters that are appropriate for their roles in the story. I hope this proves to be a helpful tool to other aspiring character designers out there!

Explore Gesture Drawing

One way to accelerate the growth of your drawing skills and observational skills is through gesture drawing.

What a Gesture should be:

  • Loose
  • Fluid
  • Tells a Story / Conveys a Mood
  • Don‚Äôt be a slave to the model - Push the poses

7 Principles of Gestures:

  • Line of Action
  • Shapes
  • Silhouette
  • Space
  • Exaggeration
  • Extrapolation
  • Story

Line of Action

  • What is the MAIN IDEA of the Pose?
  • How do I represent that idea with a LINE?
  • Show the main idea of the pose with 1-2 lines
  • It‚Äôs a base for your drawing
  • Dynamic poses = angles and harsh/straight lines
  • Relaxed poses = curves and soft lines

Shape

  • Represent the pose in a simple shape
  • Dynamic pose = Obtuse Triangle, Extreme Rhombus-shape
  • Relaxed pose = Semicircle, Relaxed Peanut-shape

Silhouette

  • Needs to be clear/readable
  • Should be able to tell what every part of the body is doing

Space

  • The model in relation to 3D space
  • Avoid always placing the feet on the same plane

Exaggeration

  • Avoid a literal translation of the pose
  • Push the pose
  • Communicate the idea, not the pose

Extrapolation

  • Draw animals or alternate characters from the model using the same pose
  • Push past what you see for the idea/emotion

Story

  • Tell a story with the pose
  • What is the main idea of the Pose?
  • How do I best communicate it?

[The Science of Drawing] vs [The Soul of Drawing]

  • Anatomy
  • Emotions
  • Light
  • Feelings
  • Proportion
  • Ideas
  • Technique

Combine both Concepts:

[Science of the Soul]

  • Draftsmanship vs Vitality
  • Tedium does not equal Sophistication
  • Realism does not equal Quality

Try out free online Figure Drawing resources (warning - nudity):

  • Figure Drawing 1
  • Figure Drawing 2
  • Figure Drawing 3
  • Figure Drawing 4
  • Figure Drawing 5
  • Figure Drawing 6
  • Figure Drawing 7
  • Figure Drawing 8
  • Figure Drawing 9

How to do a Gesture drawing:

  • Observe the pose for 10 seconds

  • Try to find the main idea/feeling of the pose

  • Grab a loose basic drawing or a small thumbnail

  • Push that pose

  • No one will know you changed it!

  • Emphasize the pose, don‚Äôt always make it dynamic

  • Use the model as a jumping off point

  • Go with your first impression - follow your instincts

  • üëâ Great tips from Kristian Nee

  • üëâ Advice from Joshua Jacobo

  • üëâ Gesture drawing ideas from Tracy Strong

Learning these habits while practicing observational sketching and anatomy studies can translate into big improvements for your character design techniques and draftsmanship. Notes and drawings by Dermot O Connor:

Structural Drawing

There have been many well-known authors of anatomy and figure drawing books, they‚Äôre fantastic resources to find and research through - masters such as Loomis, Bridgman, Tiner, Hamm, and Sterling, just to name a few. They all have their own styles for how structural anatomy drawing should be approached, there‚Äôs lots to learn from all of them.

Here‚Äôs a breakdown of shape design combinations by Nicolas Rix:

Grab artwork or photos from online, or pause on videos, take a screenshot - bring it into your drawing software and DRAW OVER it to FIND the structure, it can be fun and a great learning experience:

There‚Äôs many ways to approach how to make breakdowns:

Doing a character study like these can help you see where the patterns are in the anatomy that is unique to that property‚Äôs style, here‚Äôs an example of a breakdown of the various shapes, proportions and body types from Total Drama Island:

Structural anatomy is one of the most basic and important aspects to learn about character design - it can be broken down many different ways, here‚Äôs just a few examples:

Your drawing skills, your drawing speed, and your work ethic, are your primary tools that will make you a valued employee in the field of character design. Creativity and Style develop naturally and slowly over time. Your level of draftsmanship in figure drawing is something you will work on during your entire career. Even though you‚Äôll never directly use the practice drawings you do with figure drawing in your work, the underlying skills it develops will increase your knowledge and abilities when it comes to design.

üëâ One of the best books out there on the subject is Walt Stanchfield‚Äôs masterpiece: Gesture Drawing for Animation

Character Model Pack

  • Page 2: Concept Art (rough) - Have a page full of thumbnails showing 10-15 more exploration sketches

  • Page 10: Mouth Chart (line art) - 16 to 20 drawings (two sets of 8-10 happy & 8-10 sad, in 3/4 view)
  • Page 11: Hand Chart (line art) - 20 drawings (poses of various angles and gestures)
  • Page 12: Color Breakdown (color) - Create close-ups of certain parts of your design, showing various angles and hidden areas that would reveal more potential design & color options, like close-ups of under the feet, closed eyelids, inner mouth, inside sleeves, inner cuffs or collars, along with any draw-throughs like under the cloak, behind the wings, or any other hard-to-see places.

Conclusion

Here is an extended wrap-up to this crash course, with lots more info on how to get ready, how to continue with your training, how to branch off into other aspects of this industry, and how to proceed with seeking out employment in character design. This covers the many avenues for you to choose from and expand upon, how to continue on your journey to improve further, and what to expect as you get into this field.

Advice for the Aspiring Character Designer

  • Practice Turn Arounds
    • Because turns are so important to the TV animation pipeline, knowing how to rotate a character properly is essential in showing off your skills and how to be useful in the design team.
  • Be Familiar with Mouth Charts
    • Less integral than turns since most shows teach you their in-house system for mouth charts, but researching what the standard mouth shapes are and memorizing them is a good way to prepare, this way you only need to adapt them to the style you will be doing them for.
  • Draw Animals
    • Nothing really shows your draftsmanship skills quite like drawing a horse or giraffe. Practice drawing animals because inevitably, there will be a time when you have to draw one as a character designer. See if you can stylize animal anatomy in different ways.
    • By Ben Balistreri
  • Hoard Images
    • Professional character designers usually have massive collections of photo references of things that inspire them. Save photos of outfits, hairdos, people, poses, images of models sheets and sketches from various artists and styles -- Anything that sparks your imagination. Keep them organized in lots of folders.
  • Keep your Layers Organized
    • In TV animation, one Photoshop file is passed from artist to artist through the pipeline so a messy layering/labeling system can be a nightmare for whoever touches your file next. Always strive to be organized when setting up your photoshop files so that you are kind to whoever has to deal with it next.
    • By Jerimiah Alcorn
  • Work on Speed
    • The entire character design industry can be summed up in 4 words: GOOD DESIGNS, ON TIME
    • This is NEVER easy. The demand for quality and quantity on a relentless deadline-based weekly schedule, will ALWAYS be challenging. Practice managing your time, and try different techniques and tools to help you stay focused and on track. Practice drawing more efficiently, work on saving sites and resources that are fast to use to have at your fingertips for searching for references. Organize your own folders of saved imagery and website links archived and easy to access.
  • Stay Healthy
    • Here is a document about how to prevent repetitive stress disorder and other injuries that can happen from drawing for too many hours, and how to prevent harm to your body from working at a computer for long periods of time. It comes with some advice on exercising, nutrition, sleep, and how to manage burn-out: Health & Wellness
  • Just Keep on Drawing
    • More than other positions in TV animation, a character designer needs to really know how to DRAW. To what level of draftsmanship? This depends on the type of show, but being a strong illustrator with a wide array of skillsets will only make you a more desirable character artist‚Ä¶ and will ultimately help you maintain longevity in your career. Stay motivated, start doing the work, become focused, don‚Äôt overthink everything, choose action over everything else, watch this!

‚ÄúWhen you just grab the instrument, keyboard, or pen, you just need to start, just do something, anything, you‚Äôll realize it happens almost by itself, you become the conduit for that creative energy that was waiting to flow out. You keep your ego out of it, it happens on its own, it just will pour out of you, but you need to get started first. When it comes to art, you can‚Äôt wait to be ready, you can‚Äôt tell yourself you‚Äôre not prepared and not ready to start, you just need to start, right away, no matter what.‚Äù - Robert Rodriguez

  • How to be more efficient:
    • Drawing - Develop your drawing skills to create compelling visuals and show off the various styles you can do
    • Research - Find and collect references from all types of sources to find inspiration
    • Storytelling - Understand how your characters fit into the narrative and how they relate to each other
    • Digital tools - Practice on the software; Photoshop, Illustrator, Clip Studio Paint and Procreate
    • Keep learning - Make sure to collect as many books as you can, along with any well-established anatomy books

Build Your Portfolio

Strong character design portfolios usually have some key features:

Show you have a range of styles - especially in TV animation, chances are you will need to bounce between several different types of shows with different styles.

A good way to show your breadth as a designer is to have 3 vastly different styles at the top of your portfolio.

For Example:

  1. Your personal style/comfort zone
  2. Super cartoony/simplified/stylized
  3. Very anatomically-accurate/realistic

TV animation has designers who know how to become chameleons. A good strategy for showcasing your design chops is to create one main project in your comfort zone style. Showcase your handle on Emotion and Storytelling and Draftsmanship by using a pre-existing property and create a new character in your own native style. Create character lineups, poses, expressions, vignettes and scenarios, showing their personalities and how they interact.

Consider showing your preliminary sketches and explorations of different potential takes of a character. The other 2 styles (super cartoony and realistic/anatomical) don‚Äôt need to be of the same project, just nice lineups of characters to show you can switch styles readily. The specific design languages don‚Äôt matter, those are just choices to make and to stick to for the specific personality and intentions behind the character‚Äôs design.

Practice your techniques to adjust your drawing style to suit various shows: Inspire Multiple Designs From One Idea

For one or two characters in your portfolio - show your progression stages, employers like to see your thought process:

There‚Äôs no one correct way to present your characters in your portfolio, only some principles to follow to make your designs clear in your layouts to make things visually appealing. When thinking about how to present your characters, follow the patterns other designers take - keep it simple and inviting. A common way of displaying your work is to combine the turnarounds with head angles/expressions and a full-figure pose off to the side like this:

Displaying a wide range of designs, shows your versatility.

Variations of the same type of template is fun and easy to make:

Create an account with one of these; artstation, behance, carbonmade, myportfolio, wix,blogger,weebly,dribbble,format, google, or any other free portfolio-making site of your choice. There's dozens to choose from, they all do the same thing, research through them, find the one that you like best, and sign up for it. What you'll do with it is very simple: you don't need anything fancy; you're just displaying your work, and it‚Äôs good to show some versions with your exploration sketches, rough pass, clean lineart and some color. The important thing is to have your contact info visible everywhere, to keep your site simple, easy-to-navigate, and only having your very best work on display.

Have at least 10 different character design turnarounds (4pt, or more) in your portfolio. Show some variety in your progress and design packs: rough explorations with clean or colored works, only show your very best. Showing your process helps employers see what your thoughts are while creating and they always appreciate that.

For inspiration, here‚Äôs samples of just a random few top-notch professionals and their portfolio sites:

  • Adam Fay
  • Justin Rodrigues
  • Megan Phonesavanh
  • Anya Butler
  • Nikita Greer
  • Moss Lawto
  • Steve Lambe
